                                 Books by the Author
1. 3D Unconditional Security—graphical overview of principle perspectives regarding unconditional security.
2. 4D Forgiveness—examines four levels of biblical forgiveness and explains how it is possible for God to
    forgive and forget our sin in one stratum while not forgiving or forgetting our sin in another stratum.
3. Breaking the Rocking Horse—introductory book regarding the distinction between having eternal life as a
    gift versus obtaining it as a reward in relation to the outer darkness and the parable of the soils.
4. Carnal Corinth—an affirmation of the categorical existence of carnal Corinthian Christians.
5. Destined for Salvation—evaluates five major models for interpreting 1Thess 5:9-10 and takes the passage
    as promising an unconditional rapture (and thus unconditional security) to all believers in Christ, even to
    those who are morally asleep. However, an incentive for being morally awake is provided by making
    salvation (in the form of millennial and eternal ridership) contingent upon being morally alert.
6. Fallen from Grace but Not from Perfection—an advanced defense of unconditional security which affirms
    that believers can fall from grace but not fall from perfection.
7. Is God a Communist?—by comparing Paul’s teaching of the workmen with Jesus’ parable of the laborers,
    this book refutes communistic views of Heaven which think that Jesus is building heavenly mansions in
    New Jerusalem for all believers regardless of the way they live.
8. John in Living Color—a color-coded translation and reference manual of the Gospel of John that focuses
    on the grammatical and aspectual indications of unconditional security.
9. Like a Tree Planted by the Water—expounds upon the OT background for the outer darkness by examining
    the precursor texts in Psalms, especially Ps 1-2.
10. Logic Tables and Diagrams—illustrations of logic with scriptural implications pertaining primarily to the
    soteriological and misthological arenas.
11. Mere Christianity and Moral Christianity—a more comprehensive discussion regarding perseverance (and
    other performance-related themes) as being needed for rewards in Heaven rather than for entrance into
    Heaven. This book provides both introductory material and advanced treatments.
12. Monogamous Sex in Heaven!—at the same time in which the present author was reading books by Marrow
    and Kreeft posing that sexual intimacy continues in Heaven, he discovered that his wife had stage-3 breast
    cancer. As a therapy to help him deal with this news, he poured himself into research to see if Marrow and
    Kreeft might be correct, thereby enabling him to believe that no matter what happened, the best was yet to
    come. This is the conclusion and comfort of this book.
13. New Birth by the Sovereign Wind—refutes the Calvinistic interpretation of Jn 3:16 that limits God’s love
    to the elect. An excursus deals with the Calvinistic claim from Jn 3:3 that one must be born again before
    one can see (i.e., believe) the gospel. An additional excursus entertains a possible duality to what it means
    to see the kingdom.
14. Predestination and the Open View—a discussion of predestination that focuses on free will and the book
    of life from an open view.
15. Rewards are Eternal—is a defense of the eternal nature of biblical rewards against the proposition that
    Rev 22:5 indicates that all believers will rule in the eternal state and against the premise that Rev 21:4
    proves that misthological death will no longer exist in the eternal state.
16. Sealed and Secure—a thorough defense of a popular argument for unconditional security that also defends
    the premise of unconditional promises.
17. The Race of Grace—introductory book in which performance-related themes (such as perseverance) are
    considered essential for rewards in Heaven. Entrance into Heaven, in contrast, is regarded as free. A strong
    defense of unconditional security derived from believers being given to Jesus is a strength of this book.
18. The Outer Darkness—a magnum opus that ties together the author’s arguments for unconditional security
    and conditional rewards.
19. Trichotomy—an advanced holistic dichotomous discussion of trichotomy with an integration of nikology.
20. Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep—a rejection of the notion that profession of salvation is proven genuine
    by the way a person lives. This book focuses on the parable of the sheep and the goats and shows the
    inadequacy of conditional security in interpreting this parable.
                      For books by the author, see http://misthology.org/?page_id=35.
                        For additional material, see www.UnconditionalSecurity.org.

                                              Acknowledgments
      As always, I am thankful for my mother, Gladys J. Cauley, for assisting in the proofreading. Scott Crawford’s
model of the intermediate state was most helpful in formulating a competing model to my own and, ultimately, to
proposing a more satisfying synthesis. Our friend, James Hollandsworth, has being helpful in proofreading this
mediation.
                 Chapter 1. Models for the Outer Darkness
                              Introduction
        Part 1 of this series on misthological models will focus on competing models regarding the outer darkness.
According to Isaiah, the Lord made a promise to “Cyrus, His anointed....I will give you the treasures of darkness”
(Is 45:1-3). Historically, this referred to hidden treasure—secret treasure concealed in subterranean vaults or
rooms—not to the treasures themselves as being dark or evil. God used Cyrus temporally as an instrument to deliver
His people Israel from the Babylonian captivity and return them to their own land. When, with God’s help, Cyrus
captured Babylon through a surprising secret entrance into the city under its walls, he captured the city vault.
Typologically, Cyrus also pictures Christ, as God’s anointed, who delivers His people and captures the treasures
hidden away in darkness. Devotionally, many preachers have applied the principle gleaned from this text to our
lives as followers of Christ. God has many treasures of darkness for those who study the outer darkness in-depth.
        While I was assisting Jody Dillow with the proofreading of his Final Destiny (hereafter Destiny), after its
initial 2012 release, we discussed various modifications for his model of the outer darkness that he might wish to
consider for improved internal coherency. The present book will outline some of the models that we considered and
then compare his improved model with competing models. Theology is a science and, as such, is capable of posing
multiple models for consideration to see which model best accords with the data. Having competing models can
indeed be very healthy. For example, as a creationist I enjoy hearing creation scientists present competing creationist
models. While listening to a lecture by a creationist earlier in 2012, who was explaining four competing creationist
models pertaining to starlight, he discounted one model as being implausible, considered another model to be
plausible but not likely, but regarded the two remaining models to be strong contenders. Furthermore, he considered
having multiple models healthy, from a scientific point of view, and he did not necessarily regard these models as
being mutually exclusive. Similarly, in my interaction with Dillow, we discussed our competing misthological
models pertaining to the outer darkness. He made modifications to his model, as I provided interaction from the
perspective of my models. Eventually, I also posed a mediating model held by my friend, Scott Crawford, and
assisted him in developing a presentation of his model as a synthesis of the models held by Dillow and myself.
        Just as creationists are agreed on the overarching creationist model and yet entertain different cosmological
models to explain the scriptural and empirical data, so Dillow, Crawford, and I (as misthologists) are agreed on the
fundamental axiom that the outer darkness is a metaphorical concept that is applicable within the sphere of rewards
to unconditionally secure believers. Believers are unconditionally secure from eternal damnation but are susceptible
to loss of eternal rewards. The outer darkness, as it applies to believers, is a picture of that potential loss of eternal
rewards. Although not all misthologists perceive the loss of rewards to be an eternal loss, Dillow and I are agreed
that the loss of rewards for soteriologically secure believers is at stake and that this loss is eternal. Our shared
affirmation of the eternal nature of rewards will be considered axiomatic to our models and thus frame the
overarching parameters for our models. The discussion herein will be limited chiefly to those competing models
which hold to these two premises: (1) the outer darkness pictures loss of reward; (2) positive and negative rewards
are eternal.1 Opposing models will be mentioned only in passing so that the focus of the present discussion may be

1
 From our joint shared premises, I will draw a conclusion with which Dillow does not concur, however. A simplistic
representation of my syllogistic reasoning would be:

    1. The outer darkness pictures loss of reward.
    2. Loss of rewards is eternal.
    3. Therefore, the outer darkness is eternal.

Dillow’s reasoning appears to be, however, that while the loss of rewards pictured by the outer darkness is eternal,
the outer darkness itself is not eternal, in that the separation and regret imposed by the outer darkness is, in his
opinion, only temporary. Crawford and I contend, in contrast, that the outer darkness is eternal in that the objective
separation literally imposed by this metaphorical imagery is certainly eternal and the subjective response of regret
on the part of those subjected to this eternal misthological separation is most reasonably presumed to be eternal as
well: “The misthological reality depicted by the outer darkness is eternal,” and, in that sense, the outer darkness is
eternal (TOD, 533).

circumscribed to advanced considerations by those in basic agreement with the eternal-reward premise. Those not
agreeing with these premises may consult my two principle books regarding these two premises: The Outer
Darkness (TOD) and Rewards are Eternal (RAE).

              Misthological Models for the Outer Darkness
       Notwithstanding this shared premise on the part of Dillow and myself regarding the eternal nature of the
rewards, we are not necessarily agreed as to the implications our shared perspective has on related doctrines, such
as the wedding festivities, the duration of regret, and the location and continuation of the outer darkness. The
principle focus of the current study will be on the outer darkness and the possible impact it may have on one’s
understanding concerning residency within the Heavenly City. Auxiliary matters, such as the duration of regret and
the makeup of the Bride, will receive subsequent summary interaction.2 Such matters are intertwined. At one
juncture in our correspondences, Dillow stated:

               All believers will enter the kingdom and eternity full of joy and with no regret because all tears
       will be wiped away.
               Their loss of reward, however, is eternal but the weeping is not because Scripture says it is not. I
       view the wiping away of tears to mean the removal of [a] sense of shame and regret which produces
       them.
               The outer darkness is a temporary experience of exclusion during the time of the wedding
       festivities, it is not a place of confinement and remorse for all eternity. While you seem to be correct
       that it does have a special [i.e., spacial/spatial] element, the primary idea in my view is exclusion.3

2
  Discussing other points of divergency in our models, such as Gehenna, is beyond the scope of the present work.
Further, discussion of Gehenna is not indispensable for the present treatment, which focuses on the outer darkness,
because our two competing models for Gehenna are, surprisingly perhaps, not necessarily mutually exclusive.
Although I presented a comprehensive defense for the classic soteriological model of Gehenna in Darkness, and
even though Dillow has provided an equally suitable defense for a misthological model in Destiny, I had anticipated
such a mature defense on his part and in Darkness provided a merger of our two models into a neoclassical,
soteriological-misthological model that adopts both perspectives as part of the biblical intent. Gehenna may be
understood as not simply either soteriological or misthological but rather as both soteriological and misthological.
Dillow and I may both be right about Gehenna. My impression is that Dillow’s Gehenna argument in Destiny is a
thing of beauty, a very helpful detailed supplement to my discussion of preteristic Gehenna Bema and my theory
of correlativity (see both in TOD.) Our theories regarding the outer darkness, conversely, in posing two competing
misthological models, tend to be more either-or rather than both-and. Even here, though, we have benefited
mutually from comparing our models, and a model posed by Crawford possibly synthesizes the best of both our
models. Yet even Crawford’s model (if regarded as a synthesis of the models by Dillow and myself) is still an
alternative model in its own right. We all three cannot be right in the broad strokes where we pose alternate
misthological projections as to the nature and/or location of this misthological darkness. Our theories regarding the
outer darkness are thus scientifically subject to the criterion of falsifiability. In fact, in part four of this series, I will
create a synthesis of all three models (my original view, Crawford’s view, and Dillow’s new view) to postulate that
the outer darkness is indeed applicable before the millennial kingdom starts. In this modification, I have been helped
by Dillow’s model, who made this original projection in his revised model. Yet I have moved beyond his
modification so as to suggest that the outer darkness might even be applicable during the intermediate state. Iron
sharpens iron, and each of our models have benefited from interaction with the other models.

       As will be seen from our discussions below, however, the popular insistence among misthologists holding
this popular position, that the wiping away of tears proves that the regret associated with being thrown into the outer
darkness is temporary, is fraught with logistical difficulty, so much so that Dillow eventually abandoned using this
defense for his temporary-regret model, at least during the course of our interaction. Despite our disagreement as
to the length of regret, I am very pleased, for example, that in his section within Destiny entitled, “The Duration of
the Remorse,” he does not use the wiping-away-of-tears argument in his attempt to limit the duration of
misthological remorse. To his credit, during our correspondences, he was receptive as to the improbability of these
passages being applicable to that conjectural limitation.4
                                               Metaphorical versus Literal
         Because hermeneutics is, in part, a science, a methodological concern must be addressed (e.g., in interpreting
the parables), even before putting on our lab coats, so to speak, as how to approach, catalog, and correlate the data
(i.e., the parabolic details) that will be encountered. As explained in the introduction of the first chapter of my book,
The Outer Darkness (hereafter referred to as Darkness or abbreviated as TOD), the two basic approaches are
deductive and inductive. I interpret the outer darkness misthologically (i.e., as dealing with rewards). Typically,
those adopting an opposing model (i.e., a soteriological model) take a deductive approach, which focuses on the
central truth of the parable and then, for the most part, ignores the details on the basis of the central truth. Keathley,
for example, takes such an approach and sweeps aside the “little details,” as he calls them, in order to derive a
soteriological interpretation of the outer darkness.5 Huber, conversely, takes an inductive approach that uses the
details to help determine the central truth and then seeks to garner what additional truths may be conveyed by the
details. He used this approach in his pioneering thesis defending a misthological interpretation of the outer
darkness.6 Basically, I adopted his approach as my premise, augmenting it with deductive logic and adductive
methodology. Conceptually, therefore, I can see why some of those developing opposing models might assume that
a misthological interpretation of the outer darkness necessitates an inductive premise. For I am highly critical of the
opposing camp’s approaching the parables from a deductive premise.
         On the other hand, the fallacy of their assumption that a misthological interpretation of the outer darkness
requires an inductive approach may be seen, however, in that Dillow uses deductive logic as his working premise,
yet he comes to a misthological conclusion. He, like Keathley, and thus admittedly to my chagrin, adopts the same
hermeneutical methodology as those who contend for soteriological understanding of the outer darkness. The
difference in methodology between Dillow and myself, therefore, makes it challenging at times to compare our
models. Notwithstanding, as it turns out, our mutual differences are our combined strength. For the assumption by
those taking a soteriological understanding, that a misthological model requires an inductive premise, is refuted by
Dillow’s taking a deductive premise, while nonetheless deriving a misthological conclusion.
         After reading my Darkness and in response to our interaction during my proofreading of his Destiny, Dillow
added some additional material to his 2013 edition of Destiny. Some of his new material was to set forth a defense
of his model over against my own. His doing so was necessary, of course, in that we cannot ignore our differences,
and we should be able to set forth our particular understandings as forcefully as prudently possible. Consequently,
his addressing our differences and providing further rationale for his position in the points where we diverge is well
received herein. Our iron-sharpening-iron reciprocation in our point-versus-counterpoint interaction will provide
opportunity for further elucidation.

distinctions evaporate in eternity, let me know. I believe, as you do, that rewards and distinctions last throughout
eternity” (Personal correspondence, 12-5-2013). Dillow and I stand shoulder to shoulder, therefore, in our joint
affirmation of the eternal nature of misthological distinctions. Therefore, his statement to the otherwise in Reign
and my criticism of that statement in Darkness need not distract our readers today from our fundamental agreement
in the present discussion. The question, now, must be, “Which misthological model best accords with our shared
fundamental premise that positive and negative rewards are eternal?”

Page 4                                                                                     Misthological Models Part 1
Multiple Central Points
       Dillow commences his analysis of the parables with a study on the outer darkness in chapter 49 of Destiny.
The chapter starts with a story that appears to be realistic portrayal of what might literally happen in Mt 8:11-12
and 22:1-14. In Jesus’ opening teaching on the outer darkness, the Lord warns, “And I say to you, that many shall
come from east and west, and recline at the table with Abraham, and Isaac, and Jacob, in the kingdom of heaven;
but the sons of the kingdom shall be cast out into the outer darkness; in that place there shall be weeping and
gnashing of teeth.” But is that place a literal place? Does the outer darkness have spatial impactions? One would
think so, especially given Dillow’s highly appropriate enactment of this future event:

              The festive gathering had commenced. From East and West they had come. The patriarchs were
      there, including Abraham, Isaac, and Jacob. All had gathered to celebrate with the King and to rejoice
      with his companions. As the guests filed into the banquet hall, the King fixed his gazed on one of the
      attendees. He was inappropriately dressed. The King known as “Wonderful” rose from the table and
      slowly moved toward the massive oak doors of the banquet hall. As He walked His countenance
      saddened, knowing the fearful pronouncement He must make....
              With a broken heart, for He loved him deeply, and with a tear in His eye, the King called
      Wonderful replied with these dreadful words:
              “Please escort those who are not prepared for my supper into the darkness outside the banquet
      hall.” (Destiny, 764.)7

      While I was proofreading the 2013 edition of Destiny, I mistakenly thought that Dillow meant to describe
the scene as it might actually take place in a real place. Consequently, I was having considerable difficulty
reconciling his treatment of the outer darkness with such details. Much to my disappointment, however, Dillow
informed me that he did not intend to be taken as describing literal possibilities in his story telling above. To avoid
such misunderstanding, Dillow added the material on p. 765 to clarify:

              Before proceeding, it is important that I set forth my approach to the interpretation of parables.
      Because a parable is essentially a similitude or an extended metaphor, I do not believe that all the
      details have significance. If parables were allegories, and they are not, Blomberg correctly points out
      that allegories “are more complex stories which require numerous details in them to be ‘decoded.’”
      Thus, details of the wedding banquet, where it was located, and how it would be possible to serve
      millions of people a dinner are matters best left to speculation. Similarily [sic.], questions regarding
      the spatial locations relative to the banquet hall, such as the darkness ouside [sic.], are irrelevant to the
      major points [note the plurality] of the parable. The classical view of parabolic interpretation is that
      one should seek the one central point [note the singularity] of the parable. Blomberg has correctly
      argued that there may be several points [note the plurality], not just one [note the plurality], but the
      details do not have independent significance. Ramm says, “A parable is not like an allegory for in the
      latter, most of the elements have meaning.” He continues, “A Parable is a truth [note the singularity]
      carried in a vehicle. Therefore there is the inevitable presence of accessories which are necessary for
      the drapery of the parable, but are not part of the meaning. The danger in parabolic teaching at this
      point is to interpret as meaningful what is drapery.”8 (Destiny, 765.)

       Setting forth one’s methodology for the interpretation of parables is important. Not doing so would be very
unwise. For this reason, my book, Darkness, commences with this very consideration on the opening page of the
very first chapter and repeatedly shows the shortcomings of this one-central-point approach (pp. 7-11, 311, 432,

435-436). Since the readers of the present book are presumed to have read both Darkness and Destiny, repeating
that discussion here should not be necessary. Suffice it to say that I object to this classical position. This classical
position is fraught with classical problems, such as the obvious, intended, literal fulfillment of some of the parabolic
details, such as the burning of Jerusalem in 70 A.D., which is anticipated in this very parable (Mt 22:7). Moreover,
those adopting this central-truth methodology tend to object to giving any significance to the fact that the man in
the feast was thrown out of that feast. The misthological perception, on the other hand, is that his being in the feast
would place him in the eschatological kingdom. But those taking a soteriological interpretation of the parable object
to drawing this conclusion and try to justify their brushing aside what they consider a little, insignificant detail in
the name of central-truth methodology. Dillow, though, correctly uses this detail to justify his misthological
understanding that the man is a believer and in the eschatological kingdom (Destiny, 769-772). Basically, because
the man is at the kingdom feast, he must be in the kingdom and thus regenerate. Dillow’s misthological
understanding in this matter is sound. One must question, therefore, whether his parabolic misthology is equally
sound. How does he pull off this central-truth-only-but-details-also perspective? Simple, not unlike some others
who adopt the supposed central-point mentality, Dillow cheats:

      When interpreting parables, wise scholars follow two basic criteria: (1) discern the central points [note
      the plurality] of the parable, and (2) give incidental details interpretive significance only if they in
      some way contribute to the central points [note the plurality]. The major themes [note the
      plurality] of the parable of the virgins are: the Lord will come unexpectedly; we must be prepared;
      there will be a judgment of the believer’s works; and then the Lord will celebrate with those who are
      prepared. A particular chronology of these events is not as important as the ethical demands to which
      the parable calls us. (Destiny, 808.)

      Dillow’s averred methodology in limiting focus to one central point of a single parable has now become
multiple central points of that single parable.9 Additionally, the so-called “incidental details” are acknowledged to
have possible “interpretive significance” under certain parameters. But this methodology closely resembles my own
adductive approach (Darkness, 9-10).10 To adapt my articulation there to Dillow’s perspective here, which is more
9
  One way that interpreters can cheat hermeneutically in using this methodology is to claim that a certain detail they
want to discount should be excluded because it does not contribute to the alleged singular central truth they are
advocating, even though such interpreters have acknowledged that the parable may have multiple central truths.
Given their multiple-central-truths concession, they seem to forget, however, that ruling out that a detail just because
it does not contribute to one central truth does not rule out the possibility that it might contribute to another central
truth within the parable.

   The difference, then, between the actual implementation of my methodology versus that of Dillow is primarily
one of degree. We both take a number of the details literally; I simply take more of them literally, attempting to do
so whenever feasible. Even when not necessarily taking them literally, I tend to be more open to their having some
sort of significance. For example, in my model, the outer darkness not only corresponds to a spatial reality but might
also involve literal darkness at night, just as indicated in the parable of the marriage feast and parable of the virgins.
Even in those parables the outer darkness is not eternal, in the sense of being dark 24 hours a day, but only dark at
night. Correspondingly, according to the model of the outer darkness in TOD, those cast out of the Heavenly City
to live in the southern hemisphere of Earth will experience literal darkness. For that part of the planet will still
experience day and night. Likewise, on the same page in which Dillow properly insists that the man in the wedding
feast is regenerate because the parabolic detail pictures him as being in the feast, Dillow brushes aside the fact that
the virgins are left standing outside the feast, inconsistently remarking, “probably, all the details of the parables
should not be pressed” (Destiny, 772, n. 2485; see also p. 807, n. 2621).
       In my model, contrastively, I can offer an explanation not available in Dillow’s model. Because I believe that
the man who was let inside the feast and the virgins who were left standing outside the feast principally represent
unfaithful believers from two different time periods, I can suggest a corresponding literal reason as to why the
virgins are left outside. The unfaithful virgins remain where they were (i.e., on the planet outside the festive city).
As survivors of the tribulation, they have not died and thus have not gone on to the Heavenly City. Thus, they never
enter the city. They picture the unfaithful sheep (of the Sheep and Goat judgment) being judged on Earth and left
on Earth. In my original model, the poorly dressed man at the feast, on the other hand, representing the church-age
believers, already would have entered the Heavenly City, at the time of death or the rapture, before being judged at

carefully nuanced in this quote than his previous one stressing one central point, my thesis is not that the spatiality
of the outer darkness is the central point but rather that (1) it is one of the central points or (2) at least contributes
to such a point. In either case, it does not have independent significance:

              The spatial nature of the outer darkness would seem to be rather strongly affirmed by the
      parabolic detail repeated all seven times where the phrase about weeping and gnashing teeth is used:
      “There [ekei], there will be weeping and gnashing of teeth” (Mt 8:12; 13:42,50; 22:13; 24:51; 25:30;
      Lk 13:28) If Jesus simply had meant that there will be weeping and gnashing of teeth (as the NKJ
      translates it), it is strange that He prefixed this experiential there with a spatial there. This does not
      come across in translations such as the KJV and NKJ. The NAS does a good job of bringing it out,
      however, typically by translating it each time as in that place there will be weeping and gnashing of
      teeth or as it does in Lk 13:28 with both: there will be weeping and gnashing of teeth there. Even
      conservatives acknowledge that when this identical phrase is used of the lost being cast into the furnace
      of fire, a literal place with spatial restrictions is in view. The outer darkness should likewise be regarded
      as having spatial restrictions and thus be accurately described as a place. (Darkness, 570.)

       Contrastively, Dillow denies the literalicity of the Heavenly City and the literality of the dining reality.11 The
Messianic Banquet described by Isaiah and the Wedding Supper of the Lamb described by Jesus and John, although
not restricted to parabolic passages, become nothing more than metaphors for celebration and perhaps the joy of
co-ruling with Christ in Dillow’s Destiny (pp. 782, 808). In other words, overcomers will have a wonderful feast
with Jesus, at His table, at a banquet, in the Heavenly City, except for the fact that, according to Dillow, there will
be no food, no tables, no banquet, nor city: “There is no literal banquet, no literal table for millions of people, and
no literal marriage ceremony” (Destiny, 800). Yummy! A feast without food! We will have literal teeth but no literal
food into which to sink them.
Eat Meat
        Whatever happened to Jesus’ promise: “I grant you that you may eat and drink at My table in My kingdom,
and you will sit on thrones judging the twelve tribes of Israel” (Lk 22:29-30)? Why are the thrones taken literally
and the eating, drinking, and table taken metaphorically? Did Jesus run out of plates? Are we to believe that the
Lord can handle providing everyone a crown but not everyone a plate? Some of the material that Dillow relegates
to metaphor is not even in parabolic form. My approach, in counterpoint, affirms that literal eating and drinking
will occur at the table with Jesus in His kingdom. So when Isaiah pictures this reality by saying, “The Lord of hosts
will prepare a lavish banquet for all peoples on this mountain; a banquet of aged wine, choice pieces with marrow,
and refined, aged wine” (Is 25:6), in contrast to Dillow, I anticipate literal eating and drinking. Why else would
they be raising cattle in large pastures if not for meat (Is 30:23)? The objection will be raised that, contrary to a
literal fulfillment of Is 25:6, we will not eat meat during the millennial kingdom, much less during the eternal state.
In response, Randy Alcorn suggests that we will be able to eat meat.12 In further support of Alcorn’s meat-eating
proposition, I would like to propose a solution that comes to mind from an episode of Star Trek. A delegation of
Klingons was visiting aboard the Starship Enterprise and wanted raw meat to eat. They enjoyed the raw meat they
were served. When they asked to see the stalls aboard the ship where the animals were kept to be slaughtered for
such meals, they were informed, much to their surprise, that there were no stalls because no animals were kept or
killed. Rather, the food synthesizer created the meat. After all, meat is matter, and the synthesizer only needed to
convert energy into this form of matter. Since Isaiah says that we get plenty of choice meat (CSB; NET), I think I
will order my steak without any gristle. At last the perfect steak! A food processor that can produce perfect meat
seems reasonable to me.13

       Perhaps the concept of Heaven held by some is too small if they think my suggestion for the kingdom of
Heaven is too big. One fundamental belief seemingly shared across the board is that Heaven will be greater than
anything we can imagine (cp. 1Cor 2:9; 13:12; 2Cor 4:17-18). If Heaven is to be greater than anything we can
imagine, why should I think that it will be less than what I can imagine? If I can compare the meat production
capability of Heaven to a plausible production envisioned by Star Trek, why think that heavenly technology will
fall short of surpassing that comparison? In any event, one may consult Alcorn for less imaginative means to
affirming that such details may have literal fulfillment, or one could just watch the news. Vitro meat is already
being grown in labs. In fact, lab-grown burgers are already a reality, being eaten in public taste tests. One complaint
was that the vitro burgers were all lean, no fat! And PETA has offered a one-million-dollar prize for the development
of lab-produced chicken. Pork is in the wings; pun intended!
       Regardless as to what route is taken to allow literal fulfillment, careful scholars will allow such details
significance because these details contribute to the central points of the material. Denying the literal fulfillment of
many of these details appears to be more problematic than affirming their literal fulfillment. As has been well stated
by others: “If the literal sense makes sense, seek no other sense, lest it result in nonsense.” A literal sense makes
sense in these cases; therefore, one does not need to seek for some other sense, and the models denying the literal
sense in this regard are the very ones tending to result in nonsense.
Computation
      Dillow feels otherwise: “The darkness that is outside is a metaphor for exclusion from the joy of the light of
the wedding festivities which occur in the New Jerusalem during the tribulation” (p. 774). He expounds:


              Presumably, in the story this is a local area immediately outside the banquet hall. The phrase is
       a metaphor for an experience of exclusion from the joy. If it has a spatial inference, it refers to a region
       in the New Jerusalem in heaven which is outside the banquet hall where the wedding festivities occur.
       This does not mean they are [in] the “edge of the kingdom,” but they are outside the celebration. This
       is a parable and it is inappropriate to speculate regarding all the details and then draw inferences from
       them. The present writer doubts there is a spatial inference. Rather this is a metaphor for the experience
       of exculsion [sic.].
              If one chooses to take all the details literally, significant problems arise. For example, it is
       difficult to imagine that there is a literal table with food set for a hundred million faithful believers (or
       however many millions) in some gigantic banquet hall. Imagine ten people at each table. This would
       require ten million tables! Yet if one chooses to press for spatial inferences to the outer darkness in
       this parable, it would be inconsistent to deny that spatial inferences regarding the banquet can lead to
       absurdities. This parable is a story with two main points—exclusion/regret or joy/celebration. In my
       opinion one over interprets by insisting a literal spatial location for the darkness outside. That said, it
       is a metaphor for an experience and any spatial reference is an incidental inference. (Destiny, 776;
       bold and italics his; yellow highlight mine)


       Dillow believes that if the outer darkness is spatial, then the outer darkness is in New Jerusalem! I will
refute that notion momentarily. But first, let me accept Dillow’s challenge to imagine a “hundred million faithful
believers” seated at “ten million tables.” After all, imagining this possibility is much more palatable than imagining
a feast without food! Given the figures that he himself has suggested, would Dillow not allow that there are a
hundred million literal thrones for the hundred million faithful believers? Yet, if that is the case, then is it really that
difficult to believe that they could have literal tables as well? In any event, one rather unimaginative seating
arrangement would be to picture an arena in which all one hundred million believers are seated facing the center of
the arena. When my family and I attended Dixie Stampede in Pigeon Forge, we were seated in this manner and the
food was brought to our table. All the seated guests were seated on the side of the table facing the performance,
circling the arena, while the servers brought the food to us on the other side of the table. It would be like sitting at
the stadium of a ball game, except you had a table in front of you. Some movie theaters have a similar arrangement.

Now, imagine something similar to that arrangement at an NFL stadium. The Cowboy’s Stadium has a seating
capacity of 80,000. So we would be talking about something roughly 1250 times the size of that stadium.14 The
length of the stadium is 900 feet from one end zone retractable wall to the other end. So let us round up to 1000
feet. We would need a stadium roughly 237 miles long in a city that is 1500 miles long. Where is the insurmountable
problem?
        However, that figure of 237 miles is unbelievably inflated. As everyone knows, a large part of a football
stadium is the football field. That part of the stadium should not be included in the seating capacity. Therefore, in
order to be more realistic in computing the seating space required, I went to the first internet site I found for such
computations: http://www.banquettables.pro/space-and-capacity-calculator. This site is designed specifically for
the purpose of computing how much seating is required for a banquet. In response to the question, “How many
people do you have?” I plugged in the number Dillow suggested: 100,000,000. The space needed for rectangular
tables served was: 1,000,000,000 square feet. (It was calculating 10 sq. ft. per person.) There are 5280 feet in mile,
which makes 27,878,400 square feet in a square mile. Dividing the square feet needed by the square feet in a mile
computes to approximately 36 square miles needed to accommodate feeding that many people with rectangular
serving tables. That would only take a 6 x 6 mile area!15 So that we can leave a 1x1 mile area in the center of the
Banquet Hall for a raised platform and large stadium screen, let us suppose a 7x7 mile dimension. To maintain a
stadium perspective, suppose it has a height of 4 miles. A stadium that is only 7x7x4 would be super tiny in a city
of 1500x1500x1500!16
        The 7x7x4 Bema-Banquet Stadium could easily fit in the city square, in the center of the city, as Dillow
fictionally locates it (Destiny, 750). Nevertheless, I prefer to picture it at the top of the city. Thus, in the illustrations
to follow, I will portray the Bema at the top of the Heavenly City. Moreover, Dillow only posed 100 million people
would need to be seated, as if that number would be impossible to accommodate. Actually, I think he has
underestimated how many people would need to be seated. I agree with Carmical that the vast population will be
composed of the Innocents (i.e., those who died below the age of accountability). Would there be enough room to
seat them? Since I pose that the Bema is at the top of the city, postulating some calculation for the square footage
at the top of the city will be necessary.
        Krieger is so intrigued by the possibility that the “Great White Pyramid” of China was built by Shem that he
renames it the New Jerusalem Pyramid of China. After all, “the ‘ancient father of China’ is supposed to have been
Shem, the son of Noah.”17 He argues that such pyramids and that the Great Pyramid of Giza were built by Shem.18
If Krieger is correct, then I simply point out that the connection between the Great Pyramid of Giza and New
Jerusalem could have been personally explained to Abraham by Shem! No wonder Abraham was looking for the
Heavenly City if that was the case! On the other hand, even if one prefers the Nephilim hypothesis, they may have
modeled their structures after New Jerusalem. Incidentally, the Great Pyramid of Giza apparently never had a
capstone. Krieger thinks the missing capstone represented New Jerusalem. In that case, the Great Pyramid of Giza
was truncated symbolically at the top. That is, the pyramid was flat on the top, not pointed. But the flat top pointed
typologically to New Jerusalem. But if these Great Pyramids of China hint at the actual dimensions of the truncation,
then postulating a truncation of 400 x 400 seems like a reasonable estimation.

        The flat-top shape of New Jerusalem that I am suggesting is called a truncated square pyramid. The volume
of such a pyramid is calculated very easily. Simply go online to http://keisan.casio.com/exec/system/1223368185,
for example, and plug in the dimensions for base side (1500), top side (400), and height (1500). The volume for this
city is 1,505,000,000 cubic miles. That is over 1 cubic mile for each person!24
        As noted, my calculations assume that the city is in the shape of a truncated square pyramid because, along
with many others, I believe that its description as a mountain makes this pyramidal shape the preferred model. Many
others, however, prefer the cubical model, in which case the city would have much more room than what I have
calculated. In that case, the volume of the city would be 15003 = 3,375,000,000 cubic miles. So well over 2 cubic
miles per person for 1,500,000,000 people. Krieger insists that the pyramidal and cubical models should be
combined: “Reconciling the ‘Mountain of the Lord” object with the cubed New Jerusalem, one can only conclude
that ‘somehow’ it is BOTH, i.e., ‘multi-dimensional.”25
        Along with many others, for reasons explained in Part 2 of this series, I hold to the multi-dimensional and
multiverse models of metaphysical reality. Additionally, along with others, I believe that the Heavenly City
currently resides in Heaven and will be a bridge/portal between our dimension and other dimensions in eternity.
Therefore, I consider Krieger’s thesis reasonable and will adopt it as my working hypothesis for the following
spatial calculations. However, at the time I developed my models in Darkness and during the time I was interacting
with Dillow on his models, I had not seriously considered the impact that the multi-dimensional and multiverse
models would have on my model of the outer darkness. Those revisions to my model will have to await the
subsequent discussion in Part 2 of this series. My interaction with Dillow resulted in my pursuing those
considerations much more thoroughly in the Part 2. For now, though, in Part 1, for the most part, I am taking a trip
down history lane, for the sake of the history of interpretation, to demonstrate the development that resulted in my
subsequent models.
        Just as a 3D object projects a 2D shadow, a 4D object projects a 3D shadow. The Bible seems to suggest that
the Heavenly City has four spatial dimensions when it explicitly says that the items associated with earthly worship,
and the tabernacle in particular, “serve as copy and shadow of the heavenly things” (Heb 8:5) and that the “holy
place” was “a copy of the true one,” that is, the heavenly one, (Heb 9:24), namely the Heavenly City (Heb 11:10,16;
12:22), which was prepared for those misthologically seeking the city (Heb 11:14; 13:14). These things, and the
tabernacle in particular, are copies and shadows of something that is real. Krieger shows the mathematical

The city truncates at the top, at a height of 1500 miles, not short of the top. Carmical anticipates that there will be
billions of Innocents. If so, my projection of 1.5 billion total city inhabitants would be too low. So suppose there
are 1 billion overcomers and 9 billion Innocents. The total population of the city in that case would be 10 billion
transformed humans inhabiting New Jerusalem. In that case, 1.5 billion cubic miles divided by 10 billion inhabitants
would yield 0.15 cubic miles per person: 52803x0.15 = 22,079,692,800 cubic feet per person! The city would be
sparsely inhabited still. Even if the guardian angels of the overcomers live with the overcomers in New Jerusalem
(an idea that I derive from Tim Stevenson’s book, The Bema), the city still would be sparsely inhabited. Even if I
projected that each of these one billion angels and one billion overcomers each have their own personal spaceship
parked in the city, I cannot produce a number that comes anywhere near high enough to surpass the housing
capability of the city. Note, if these spaceships have teleportational abilities, then one need not pose that space
traffic is even a remote problem. On the other hand, non-transformed humans may be traveling to the city in
spaceships that do not have teleportational ability, a distinction suggested by Carmical’s Omega.
     Suppose that, including visitors to the city, you had a total of 500 billon people in the city, how much space
would they have? In that case, 1.5 billon cubic miles divided by 500 billon people would yield .003 cubic miles per
person. The number of cubic feet in a cubic mile is 52803 = 147197952000. Multiple that by .003 and you still get
441593856 cubic feet per person! How about a trillion people? Half that space: (1.5/1000) x 147197952000 =
220796928 cubic foot per person. This is still plenty of space. Note, those born in eternity future will be in 3D flesh
and blood bodies. See my discussion in Darkness. For historical overview of others holding this position, see

Chapter 1. Models for the Outer Darkness                                                                       Page 11
relationship between the city and the tabernacle, etc. Given that the writer of Hebrews is emphasizing this
relationship and saying that the 3D tabernacle is a copy and shadow of the Heavenly City, one might think that he
is hinting at its 4D dimensionality. Shadow in this context means to be “bound to and dependent on the solid body
which casts it,” and copy means “the earthly copy of the heavenly realities, or possibly a sketch of them, as
in Ezk 42:15.”26
        For that matter, Paul seemingly wants believers to perceive the multidimensional “[1] breadth, and [2] length,
and [3] depth, and [4] height” of God’s love (Eph 3:18; KJV; MT). Many have anticipated that Paul is alluding to
a 4D reality here. The MT gives what appears to be the three earthly dimensions we normally perceive followed by
a fourth spatial dimension, which adds a heavenly dimension, referred to as height (hypsos—spelled with a psi that
is transliterated as ps).27 In his discussion of the 4D nature of this verse, Josh Peck thought that hypsos might be a
rather direct reference to hyperspace, speculating that hypsos served as the prefix hyper used in hyperspace because
of the similarity in spelling.28 Technically, he was mistaken in making this association in this manner. Our English
prefix hyper is actually derived from the Greek preposition hyper, not from hypos. In Greek, hyper is spelled with
a pi (which is transliterated with a p). This Greek hyper is a prefix that can serve as the first part of a compound
word. As a marker of spatiality, hyper means above. Therefore, the word that Paul actually uses in Eph 3:18 (i.e.,
hypsos) does not mean hyperspace, at least not for the reason Peck originally hypothesized, for the simple reason
that hyper is the Greek word rendered as hyper in English, not hypsos. One would be premature, however, to dismiss
Peck’s argument due to his simple error regarding the word involved.
        For one thing, conceptually one could argue that Paul is referring to hyperspace even though he does not use
the Greek form of hyper in Eph 3:18. According to BDAG, hypsos (1) as a dimension means height (Eph 3:18;
Rev 21:16), (2) as a place it means high place (normally Heaven, Ps 17:17; 67:19; 101:20; Lk 1:78; 24:49; Eph
4:8), and (3) as a status it means high position (Job 5:11; Jam 1:9). I would enjoin the usage in Eph 3:18 and 4:8
so as to conclude that Paul is talking about a heavenly place, that is, a heavenly dimension. Paul is describing this
heavenly dimension as a fourth dimension in Eph 3:18. Conceptually, Peck’s overall argument is valid even though
his speculation regarding a direct association with the Greek word hyper was in error. This heavenly dimension
might be described as hyperspace, even without a direct association with the actual word hyper. Upon initial
examination I thought that Peck’s argument would have to be left as affirmed true at the conceptual level, not at the
linguistic level. However, Peck’s comment had peaked my interest as to whether the Greek word hyper might ever
be used in the Greek NT to describe hyperspace.
        As I looked through the compound Greek words composed with hyper as a prefix (hyper-this and hyper-
that), one word stood out as a very strong candidate—hyperano).29 According to BDAG, as a place hyperano means
high above. I then observed that hyperano is equated with hypsos in Eph 4:8-10! “He ascended on
high...He...ascended high above all the heavens” (TM). In this context, as a place hyperano means a space above
all the heavens. This place is a space. One might assume that it is outer space. But actually, the Greek text uses a
compound form of hyper to describe this space, not a Greek word for outer.30 This space is not outer space, per say,
as we know it, but hyperspace, according to Paul. Specifically, according to Paul, this hyper-place is the highest
level of hyperspace! Because the place to which Christ ascended is the highest level of hyperspace, we should
deduce that He had to travel through hyperspace in order to reach this highest level of hyperspace. Spatially, one
should interpret this to mean that Christ ascended into hyperspace above all the heavens. Or, verbally, since
hyperano is an adverb, one might literally translate Paul as saying that Christ “hyperspaced above all the heavens”
(Eph 4:10; TM). This word study thus confirms my newer models of Heaven, which picture Heaven as the outer
realm of the multiverse, reachable through the hyperspace of the multiverse. Peck’s argument was conceptually
valid but linguistically flawed. Nevertheless, his linguistic error turned out to be minor in that he simply erred in
making a direct association with hyper rather than perceiving the indirect association. By combining the hypsos of

       Paul also said, “Eye has not seen and ear has not heard...all that God has prepared for those who love Him.”
(1Cor 2:9). Might these comments have been inspired in part by Paul’s visit to the 4D City in the 3rd Heaven
(1Cor 12:2-4)? This is one of the five interpretations discussed by BNTC:

                The dimensions of the heavenly Jerusalem. A spiritualized topography of the Jerusalem Temple
        may lie behind the symbols of the dividing wall (2:14) and the cornerstone and foundations (2:20). So,
        ‘founded on love’ in the previous verse may evoke the New Jerusalem image again
        here. Rev. 21:16 provides precise measurements for the heavenly city (cf. Ezek. 48:16), using three of
        the same terms as Ephesians, length, breadth and height, which are all equal and measure 1500 miles
        in each direction....strange to say, [there are] four, not three, spatial dimensions of the heavenly
        city: it is a perfect cube (like the Holy of Holies, 1 Kgs 6:20), plus an underneath layer (a ‘depth’) of
        apostolic foundations.31 (Emphasis his.)

       Paul was “not permitted to speak” frankly of his visit to that city, but perhaps he is dropping a clue here.
After all, he uses an allegory to contrast “the Mount Sinai in Arabia” with “the Jerusalem above” (Gal 4:25-26).
Since the former is literal, the presumption should be that the latter is real also. We are expected, in Paul’s mind, to
be able to derive spiritual truths from these literal cities.
       In any event, John was permitted to speak of his visit to this Heavenly Jerusalem, and the precise
measurements he provides for the city indicate a spatial reality, not mere imaginary imagery. If the mention of the
foundations suggests the fourth dimensional nature of this city, whose other three dimensions were already given,
then one is left to speculate that the writer of Hebrews may have had something of this nature in mind as well when
he specified that the 3D city has an extra dimension, a foundation (Heb 11:10).
       How much room would we have in this 4D city for the tables? Proceeding then with the hypothesis that the
city is 4D, we could speculate that the city would look like a truncated square pyramid when viewed from the
outside in our 3D spatial dimension but not when viewed from the inside, at least not by a 3D entity.

        Illustrations of what a 4D cube, that is, a hypercube or Tesseract, would look like are common. If you unwrap
a 4D cube in 3D space, you have to unwrap it on each edge, which results in 8 cubes.32 If you were to look at the
4D cube from a 3D perspective, you would only see a regular cube. But if you stepped into that cube, you would
find it 8 times larger on the inside than it appeared on the outside, a feature useful in sci-fi movies. A hyperpyramid
would work similarly. Even though a truncated pyramid is preferred over a pointed square pyramid by the present
writer, the formula for the pointed (i.e., regular) pyramid will be used for illustrative purposes, even though it
generates a smaller number. According to the most popular formula on the internet, the volume in that case would
be (rn)/n = (15004)/4 = 1,265,625,000,000. Well over 800 cubic miles for each person! Frankly, this computation is
unbelievably high. Dividing that number by 1.5 billon people does not give the cubic feet for each person because
the (rn)/n formula generates hypercubic feet, not cubic feet.33 So some other method will have to be used to calculate
the cubic feet generated when a hyperpyramid is unfolded into 3D space. A reasonable conjecture is that just as a
hypercube has 8 times more volume than a cube when unwrapped around its six faces into 3D space, so this
truncated square hyperpyramid has 6 times more volume than its 3D counterpart when unwrapped around its five
faces. In that case, there would be over 6 cubic miles per person in the hyperpyramidal city. Then again, perhaps
the interior of the city in 4D is a cube. In that case, the volume would be (15003) x 8 = 27,000,000,000, which yields
18 cubic miles per person, for 1,500,000,000 people. Regardless of the calculation employed, I have no problem
whatsoever imagining a literal area for our worshipping the Lord, being judged by the Lord, living with the Lord,
and eating with the Lord inside this city. Do the math! Whether it be 1, 6, or 18 cubic miles per person, the Heavenly
City has ample space to accommodate literal fulfilment. Such computations aid one’s imagination. We literally will
worship the Lord; we literally will be judged before His literal Bema; we literally will live with Him in His literal
city; we literally will eat at His literal table.34

32
   For a video, see: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=BVo2igbFSPE.
33
   For this (rn)/n formula, see The Volume of a Hyperpyramid at http://www.physicsinsights.org/pyramids-1.html.
This formula is confirmed by xa3/4 at http://www.mathaware.org/mam/00/master/essays/B3D/2/egypt.html. If not
truncated, then x = a, so a4/4. It seems intuitive enough, since the volume of a regular right square pyramid with a
height equal to the sides of its base would be a3/3. I am not using this hypercubic computation, nonetheless, because
I want cubic feet, not hypercubic feet. Computing the volume of a hypercube from rn yields hypercubic units. A
simpler way to derive this same formula would be to use a pattern:
     •   1D = r (e.g., 1500 ft, i.e., linear feet)
     •   2D = r2 (e.g., 15002 ft2, i.e., square feet)
     •   3D = r3 (e.g., 15003 ft3, i.e., cubic feet)
     •   4D = r4 (e.g., 15004 ft4, i.e., hypercubic feet)
     The rn formula must express the units properly for each dimension, as proven by the case of r = 1. Without
proper unit notation, you get an obviously false conclusion in that case: 1D= 2D = 3D = 4D = 1. Yes, the answer is
1, but 1 what? A cubic foot is not equal to a square foot, nor is a hypercubic foot equal to a cubic foot. Units make
the difference. Accordingly, the city, if cubical on the inside, would be 15004 hypercubic feet, which in turn
generates a number far and away higher than what is indicated by the visualization of a hypercube, which is only
(15003) x 8 cubic feet. The rn and (rn)/n formulas for 4D yields hypercubic units applicable to 4D space, not cubic
units for 3D space. To compare apples with apples, so to speak, I will opt for the much smaller cubic-foot number
derived by unfolding the hypercube into 3D space. Nonetheless, these hypercubic formulas are not useless. I will
speculate that overcomers living in the 4D city will have 4D bodies and thus be able to experience the city more
fully than 3D visitors to the city. Nevertheless, because overcomers and their tables would be in 4D, and therefore
take up more hypercubic space, computing the hypercubic space per hypercubic person is not pursued herein.
Instead, the cubic space per person is the current focus.

Fictional Food
      After all, as acknowledged by Dillow,

              Matthew therefore leads us to imagine a feast of great rejoicing. All the faithful Christians of
      the church are there to celebrate their wedding feast with their King. This joyful banquet is portrayed
      by the Lord as occurring in the evening in a brightly lit banquet hall. Outside the banquet, where the
      shining lights of the feast are not present, in that place, a metaphor for the relative physical darkness
      [sic.] prevails in the evening. This darkness is not literal, but is a metaphor for the exclusion of the
      carnal Christian from the glorious reign of the Metochoi. It is not the darkness of eternal exclusion
      from heaven. (Destiny, 792; italics his.)

        Since Matthew leads us to imagine a feast, why not imagine food!?! And if there is literal food, why not
imagine a literal banquet hall!?! And if there is a literal banquet hall, why not imagine a literal place outside of the
banquet hall!?! Frankly, Dillow needs this literal spatiality. He has defined the outer darkness as a metaphor for the
exclusion of the carnal Christian from the glorious reign. I agree with that definition insofar as it goes, but it does
not go far enough. Chronologically, Dillow places the outer darkness at the time of the tribulation. However, the
Metochoi are not reigning during the tribulation! His model gives no reign from which to exclude them at the time
he would exclude those cast into the outer darkness from that reign. To be consistent with his definition, Dillow
would have to place the outer darkness during the millennial kingdom. But that is my Darkness model, not his
Destiny model. To place the outer darkness chronologically during the tribulation, as he does, he needs to place it
spatially outside the banquet. He does so fictionally, but he needs to do so literally. He strains his model when he
tries to relegate the outer darkness to an experience of exclusion for an event that is not yet happening! They cannot
experientially be cast outside the glorious reign during the tribulation when that glorious reign does not commence
until the millennium! He has compromised his own model by his definition. Even when he restates his position a
couple of pages later, one can still sense the strain. He states that he “does not agree that the ‘outer darkness’ is a
place, but it may be a metaphor for a ‘position’” (Destiny, 780, n. 2520). What position is Dillow suggesting?
Presumably, they are weeping because they are not given positions of rulership. But this regal position will not
become effective until the future, during the millennial after the tribulation. But, in that case, per Dillow’s model,
why do they cry during the tribulation and stop crying when their loss becomes effective? Why do they cry before
they experience their loss and stop crying when they actually experience their loss? Moreover, why does Matthew,
and Jesus for that matter, lead us to imagine that something much more is at stake than simply not receiving
rulership. For example, the King does not simply say, “You will not rule any cities.” To the contrary, not only is
the lazy slave not given any cities to rule, but punishment is inflicted. In addition to not being given a position of
rulership, the slave is bound and cast outside. These details are not insignificant. They should be allowed to speak!

         Jesus has painted a masterpiece with these parables. Yet Dillow is removing a key feature of this picture by
demoting this spatial feature to the status of an incidental detail. My perception, in contrast, is that we are not talking
about a stray piece of hair that could be removed from an accurate portrait of what happens to carnal believers but
a central piece of information, a crucial piece, that speaks volumes and that would mar the picture if removed.
Dillow is not removing a strand of hair from the parabolic Mono Lisa, he is removing her very lips, so to speak,
needlessly. Dillow overstates his case when he acts as if those holding to his basic soteric-mistholic paradigm think
of the outer darkness in exclusively non-spatial terms: “Partners think of the outer darkness in experiential rather
than spatial terms” (Destiny, 791-792). I am a Partner, but, obviously, I hold to a spatial rather than exclusively
experiential interpretation of the outer darkness. The outer darkness is experiential and spatial. Even if I am a
minority of one, which I am not, I will stand my ground on this issue unless I can be shown from Scripture or logic
that an exclusively experiential interpretation is decisively superior to a partially spatial interpretation.35 But as it
is, all evidence suggests the opposite conclusion. Allowing that the outer darkness has a spatial intention is not only
possible but prudent.
         Naturally, I can understand why Dillow would shrink back from allowing such a possibility within his model.
Allowing such a possibility strains his model to the point of incredibility. If his model of the outer darkness were
taken literally, it would require, as he acknowledges, that the outer darkness be inside New Jerusalem! His
model, as he is presenting it, would necessitate such an unbelievable postulation. No wonder he wants so badly to
deliteralize the city and darkness. If his model were amended, however, to allow that the outer darkness is
outside the city, then the genius of the core component of his new model would provide an attractive and
competitive model to my own original model. The endeavor of this study will be to demonstrate the necessity of
such a modification and to provide the framework for that modification.
Fictional Exclusion
       While I was proofreading an earlier edition of Destiny and seeking to pin down some specifics of Dillow’s
model, I had questioned him regarding his hesitancy toward a literal interpretation by pointing out that he began
chapter 48 of Destiny with this fictional depiction of the Bema: “Suddenly, there was a sound like the rush of a
great wind. In an instant millions of people abruptly appeared in the heavenly city. The dead in Christ had
risen. Those still living had received resurrection bodies, and all had been transported to heaven in the twinkling of
an eye” (p. 750). I responded, “The impression I get from this description is that you are anticipating that believers
(even those who previously have died) will not be brought to the city until the rapture.” His explanation was, “It is
a story, a metaphor with no intended chronology implications.36 As you know, I tend to view some of the parables

Suggesting plausible scenarios (or what I call models) is a valid part of the scientific process. The tentative nature

        My attempt to correlate his fictional events with literal counterparts in a logical chronological sequence was

telling fictional stories in the form of parables to convey a central point (or multiple points), so Dillow’s own

from his fictional story in chapter 48 as well, where Dillow portrayed the effects of the Bema in this vivid manner:

              Then with a stern look on His face, the King rebuked His unfaithful follower, “Depart from Me,
       you wicked, lazy slave.”
              “Noooo...,” the lazy servant screamed. But the angels came and led him to the darkness outside
       the city square where he began to weep and gnash his teeth in profound regret. (Destiny, 752.)

I love the way Dillow illustrates what I believe will actually take place. So I explained why I was having trouble
relating to his metaphorical approach and asked for further confirmation that such was his intent:

880). Fine, but what does that statement really mean since he discounts a literal New Jerusalem, a literal wedding
hall, and literal eating? Since he dismisses the pictures that Jesus gives us, the picture that Isaiah gives us, the picture
that John gives us, and his own fictional pictures as to what literally takes place, just what is Dillow really proposing
as a “plausible scenario” as to what genuinely transpires in his so-called experiential exclusion devoid of spatial
reality? His statements, such as this one on p. 880, ring hollow when divested of literal fulfillment.
     Even at the experiential level, what does it really mean, for example, when Dillow claims that “carnal
Christians” are “restored to eternal fellowship” (pp. 761; cp. 794) at the end of the tribulation (p. 903), and yet he
repeatedly denies that they will have enhanced intimacy: “It is better to understand keeping for eternal life as
persevering and enhancing one’s intimacy with God now and on into heaven when we die. Only by faithfulness
can that intimacy be ‘kept’ and thus experienced abundantly in heaven” (p. 398, n. 1370). As to the unfaithful,
“they will draw back in shame at His coming and they will not experience the full degree of intimacy with Christ
they could have enjoyed in the kingdom and in the eternal state” (p. 554, n. 1790). How is it that “greater intimacy”
is a reward (p. 560) but that restored fellowship is a gift? How does this Dillowian scenario actually work? Is the
prodigal son restored to postmortem fellowship after the tribulation but not to full intimacy? But if the intimacy
bestowed upon the faithful at the Bema is a positive reward and the loss of intimacy at the Bema is a negative
reward and if all Bema rewards are eternal, and Dillow seems to allow all three propositions, then how does he pose
that the degree of fellowship which is lost is restored after seven years? Logical consistency dictates that the type
of intimacy/fellowship which is lost would be eternal. Thus, loss of fellowship is eternal. Dillow appears to be
asserting the misthological equivalent of a round square when he says that unfaithful believers are restored to
fellowship even though they do not keep their intimacy. His imagination must be better than mine in that I am
having trouble imagining such a construct. What does the so-called restored fellowship amidst lost intimacy actually
look like? Is it something like a round square; something easier said to exist than shown to exist?
     I agree with Dillow when he says, “Because if I fail to follow Christ as a disciple, I face shame, profound regret,
rebuke, millennial disinheritance, and vastly reduced intimacy with Christ, service for Christ, and honor from
Christ in the millennium and in the eternal state” (p. 744; italics his). Exactly! Again, I agree when he says,
“Differences in honor, service, reward, and intimacy with Christ will, however, remain throughout eternity” (p.
763). Precisely! Therefore, when he argues that fellowship is restored but the differences in intimacy remain or,
inversely, that intimacy is lost but regret is removed, it sounds like misthological doubletalk to me. For instance, he
asserts, “This remorse will not last throughout eternity, but it will affect our degree of intimacy with Christ” (p.
763). One is left to wonder how is it that something that no longer exists continue to have an eternal affect? In any
event, he acknowledges that the carnal Christian “is judged in the sense of losing intimacy” (p. 930). So how can
that lost fellowship be restored without doing violence to the proposition that positive and negative rewards are
eternal? His chapter 60 is excellent and devoted to the discussion of intimacy as our greatest reward; wherein he
concludes: “A correlation exists between the degree of intimacy we develop with Christ now and the degree of our
intimacy with Him throughout eternity” (p. 941). His scenarios presented earlier in Destiny should be modified
to be consistent with his splendid treatment in ch. 60. Because this correlative degree of intimacy is a reward, it is
eternal—for better or worse, depending on whether one has been faithful or unfaithful, respectively.

       If someone had asked me these same types of questions back in 1985, I possibly would have given the same
type of answers that Dillow is now giving. In what is now a classic, Zane Hodges’ Grace in Eclipse was a brand-
new release in those days. I relished that book when it first came out and still count it as a masterful work. In his
chapter on the outer darkness therein, Hodges warned, “We are not to deduce...that failing Christians will spend an
anguished eternity in some darkened corner of God’s Kingdom with nothing meaningful at all to do. That...would
be a grotesque distortion of our Lord’s teaching.” A few pages later, regarding the talents entrusted to the servant
thrown into the outer darkness, Hodges cautions against reading too much into the story, “It is after all a parable!
There are no literal sums of money...we are in the presence of metaphor.”38 Dillow is simply being true to the
perspective of our mentor. Back in those days, I would ask Zane questions about the outer darkness and related
material. He is the one who put me in touch with Huber’s thesis. But as I studied Huber’s thesis and considered the
spatial implications of Carmical’s fictional depiction of the outer darkness, which came out in the second volume
of JOTGES in 1989, in which he tried to envision what the outer darkness might literally look like, I became more
and more convinced, as I did further research, that a literal correlation exists between the metaphorical details of
the parables and the future reality.
       Nevertheless, not all my fellow misthologists are going to share my propensity toward literality, not even
concerning that Heavenly City. In that early edition of Eclipse, Hodges deferred his readers to Lang for further
information regarding a misthological interpretation of the outer darkness. Here is what Lang had to say about the
city: “After reading various attempts to portray a literal city of this form and dimension, I still fail utterly to form
the vaguest conception of an actual city as a cube...or even as a pyramid....That a city can be so shaped is to me, at

my suggestion is that from the very limited biblical data that the city is a metaphor for the place where the saints
dwell during the tribulation (pre-trib) and during the millennium. How much of it is literal—I am not sure, and I
would not want to press details of the metaphor to construct ideas about locations of various groups. When we get
into the millennium, obviously all do not dwell there. Who does and who does not is not clear to me but all have
access to it.”
        Whether Dillow was entertaining the possibility that those cast into the outer darkness will not live in the
city, but have access to the city, was not clear at this juncture in our discussion. Wilkin had entertained that
possibility many years before, as I pointed out to Dillow. But since that perspective already had been entertained
before, and since I already have discussed it in Darkness, and since I had suggested three options for one of Dillow’s
footnotes, in which his own position would entail eternal residency, I am pleased, from a purely scientific
perspective, that within his neo-Dillowian model he is advocating eternal residency. See footnote 33 herein. I
welcome these competing perspectives as an opportunity for iron to sharpen iron. Still, personally, I strongly prefer
a literal residency in a literal city even for his model over his imagined metaphorical residency in a metaphorical
city. Thus, I will tend to picture his model in literal terms, which is the same way he often pictures it
38
   Zane Hodges, Grace in Eclipse: A Study on Eternal Rewards (Dallas, TX: Redención Viva, 1985), 90,93.
Page 18                                                                                     Misthological Models Part 1
least, so unimaginable as to decide that this chapter [Rev 21] is not a description of anything concrete.”39 Now
obviously, if such heavy hitters as Dillow, Hodges, and Lang are showing such an inclination toward a metaphorical
interpretation of the outer darkness and/or the city, then my taking a moment to develop some working model for
that competing perspective is more than justified. Indeed, doing so actually will be necessary. But that is getting
ahead of the story. In order to see that necessity, dealing with the more literal models first will be helpful. I will not
take time to explain my literal models here or why I believe in the literalicity of the city in this brief book since I
have done so in considerable detail in Darkness. Instead, I would like to relate my attempt to assign literal values
to Dillow’s model. The reason I was seeking to understand his discussion from a literal perspective was so that I
could interact with his models from a combination of my literal perspective.
                                      Spatial Models for the Outer Darkness
       Dillow modified some of his early models that he initially posed in his original version of Destiny because
of the problems encountered within those models. This is proper scientific procedure. Over the years I have had to
tweak my own models as well. Only brief interaction with the failed models that Dillow considered will be provided
herein so that readers of the original release of Destiny might have a frame of reference for the migration that was
taken from Dillow’s original model to his current one. Interaction with competing misthological models will then
be outlined briefly.
Bema Last
       Originally, Dillow located the outer darkness within New Jerusalem, but he did not specify where the outer
darkness would be located within the city. As we now know, that specification would be difficult for him since he
is not dogmatic about affirming the literalicity of the city. In my adaptation of his position, in which I was trying to
consider how his model might work if it were given literal values, I posed that he could locate the outer darkness in
the lower third of the city. Becker already had posed that unfaithful believers would be consigned to the lower third
of the city,40 and I had interacted with her proposal in Darkness. If that consignment is pictured as the outer darkness,
then her model would, to that extent, provide a prototype for Dillow’s model. Thus, my adaptation of Dillow’s
original projection provided a synthesis of his and Becker’s hypotheses. Therefore, I will adopt this adaptation of
his model as my working premise.
