otherwise, they would not be asking how long till God’s judgment would break forth. If these are rewarded church-
age believers, then they pose no obstacle to Crawford’s model.
       On the other hand, the prevalent dispensational perception is that these souls are disembodied tribulational
saints who are given white robes and told to rest until they receive their resurrected bodies at the conclusion of the
tribulation. Being given these white robes qualifies them for city residency. Typically among dispensationalists, the
judgment for tribulational and OT believers is thought not to take place until the end of the tribulation. Even apart
from consideration of the Crawfordian model, this data suggests that either the typical dispensational model needs
to be revised so that judgment (for tribulational believers at least) takes place during the tribulation, or it should be
admitted that white robes may be given as preliminary rewards for those who are qualified. In some way, they are
prequalified for these preliminary rewards—white robes. Being rewarded in this manner qualifies them for
residency in the Reward City.



64
   Berner, Broken, 264; Intervenes, 65-69. Notwithstanding my openness to Berner’s thesis, that those clothed in
white are resurrected church-age believers, because otherwise they would not yet be wearing white, I do not find
the question, “How long?” incompatible with my classic model. They are in the Heavenly City, which is cloaked
in a time bubble. Thousands of years may pass in this city while the Bema is being conducted. Comparatively, the
seven years on Earth would be creeping by like a glacier in the interim. Alternatively, asking the Lord this question
may be understood in its typical manner of imploring the Lord to take action (Ps 6:3; 13:1-2; 35:17; 79:5; 80:4;
82:2,8; 89:46; 90:13; 94:3). Like the little child who continually asks, “Are we there yet?” these believers simply
are eager to arrive at the destination. One need not suppose that these martyred saints are church-age believers.
Ironside, understandably, finds it “very strange that some have taught that in this great multitude we have the
raptured church. They supposed that the Lord would not come for His church until the middle of the tribulation
period” (Revelation, 91). On the other hand, this perspective is not limited to those holding to a mid-tribulation view
of the rapture. Regarding Rev 6:9-11, Hindson thinks, “It is certainly possible the martyrs who cry out for vengeance
could represent all the Christian martyrs of all time who have taken their rightful place in heaven.” But he interprets
Rev 7:9-17 as dealing with tribulational saints (Revelation, 83, 91). Although he cuts against the typical
dispensational grain in Rev 6:9-11, his conceding to the typical persuasion in Rev 7:9-17 suffices for the point I
wish to make: Those dispensationalists who properly interpret these white robes to be rewards and who dually hold
the typical dispensational view that these white robes are given to unresurrected tribulational believers should
amend their misthological-dispensational model to allow preliminary rewards. This adjustment is far superior to
GNTC’s posing that the white garments (himation, Rev 3:5,18) are rewards and then oddly concluding that the
white robes (stole, Rev 6-7) are gifts. To the contrary, if any distinction exists between the white garments and
robes, it is that the robe is the form of this raiment that is given as pre-Bema rewards to disembodied souls (or they
may have temporary spiritual bodies, so BKC), while the garment form is given to resurrected believers. I suspect
that when Moses and Elijah appeared with Jesus on the Mount of Transfiguration, their robes were white. We know
Jesus’ garments were white. The hypothesis that disembodied souls are given temporary spiritual bodies is
entertained by some to answer the question, “How could bodiless souls wear robes?” That conjecture aside, the fact
that angels can wear white robes (stole, Mk 16:5) demonstrates that spiritual beings do not need resurrected bodies
in order to wear such robes. Therefore, I question the supposition by those commentators, such as Becker, who
suppose that these martyred saints have to have resurrected bodies. My working hypothesis for my model is that
they are disembodied souls who are given white robes as a preliminary reward. The angels know who will
qualify to inherit the kingdom (Heb 1:14), and they provide these victorious believers with white robes until such
time that these misthological heirs come into regal possession of their post-Bema white garments. Repentant
believers who have forfeited their inheritance, surprisingly, also have hopes of being clothed in white stole
(Lk 15:22); consequently, they possibly will have access to the city rather than residency within the city (see twilight
zone in Darkness for models). Unrepentant believers, though, will fail to qualify for such white garments and be
taken to the outer court prior to the Bema and then cast into the outer darkness after the Bema (Mt 22:11-13). They
will be allowed to enter the wedding feast but then singled out, in the presence of all, and ejected from the feast.
They will forfeit both city access and residency for all eternity. For such reasons, repentance is an integral part of
the gospel of the kingdom. Faith alone in the gospel of grace will only save believers from the darkness of eternal
damnation, not from the darkness of the outer court. Repentance must be added to faith to qualify believers for the
halls of the Reward City.
Chapter 1. Models for the Outer Darkness                                Page 49
                                      Illustration 23. Caulian Schema
Page 50                                                                                   Misthological Models Part 1
       The view I adopted in Darkness is typical of the pretribulational view (e.g., Constable, Ryrie, Walvoord):
Both Rev 6:9-11 and 7:9-17 are referring to the same group of tribulational saints. These disembodied martyrs of
the tribulational period are pictured under the alter rather than on thrones because they have not yet received their
resurrected bodies. They do not assume their position on thrones until they are raised (Rev 20:4-6). They are
tribulational overcomers who were martyred (Rev 12:11) for refusing to worship the beast or take his mark
(Rev 13:15). They are in the intermediate state (as souls) within the city, serving around God’s throne, waiting for
their glorified resurrected body. Misthological consistency dictates that these souls are worthy to be clothed in white
for three reasons according to 20:4—because of their martyrdom, refusing to worship the beast, and refusing to take
the mark of the beast. White garments/robes are given only to overcomers who, correspondingly, are worthy to
wear them because they have paid the price necessary to become an overcomer and have washed their own robes
in practical sanctification. They are thus rewarded with rulership (Rev 3:4-5,18; 4:4; 7:13-14).
                                                       Conclusion
       Given this typical dispensational perception, because these disembodied tribulational saints are given white
robes and told to rest until they receive their resurrected bodies at the conclusion of the tribulation, only two options
seem to be available: either (1) judgment for tribulational believers takes place during the tribulation, or (2) white
robes may be given as preliminary rewards for those who are qualified. Being given these white robes qualifies
them for city residency during the millennial and eternal states. I will adopt the later view as my working hypothesis.
Doing so will be consistent with modifications in my original model yet to be discussed in the remainder of this
series, namely, that white garments and city exclusion are applicable in the intermediate state, starting with the
commencement of the NT era. We are not yet ready to explore those modifications within Part 1 of this series,
however. For now it suffices to lay the foundation by providing a history of interpretation so the reader may better
grasp the thought that has gone into the modifications considered in the various models explored. Since the classical
model delays the ejection of unworthy/unclothed believers from the city until after the millennial kingdom starts,
my conjecture, within the confines of this classical model, is that worthy believers are granted white robes as
preliminary (yet eternal) rewards and thus allowed unlimited access to the upper levels of the city. The important
point is that Part 1 has found reason to believe that preliminary rewards are dispensed to the faithful.
       Dillow was very helpful as a catalyst in causing me to consider the possibility that rewards (and even the
outer darkness) might be put into effect before the millennium. This led me to consult with Crawford and further
develop his model and mine with sufficient refinement in Part 2 of this series that I was at a loss to know which
model would be more probable: the refined Caulian model or the tweaked Crawfordian model or a Caulian-
Crawfordian model. The additional research undertaken in Part 4 of this series finally proved fruitful in leading me
to conclude that a modified Caulian-Crawfordian is in most accord with the NT and NDE data. Regardless as to
which of the models surveyed in the present book are preferred at this primary juncture in Part 1, a reasonable
inference from the data is that prequalification is granted pertaining to the white garments. This preliminary reward,
in turn, prequalifies worthy believers either (1) to city residency (so the Crawfordian model)65 or (2) to upper city
levels (so the Caulian model as it stands in Part 1). This upper level hypothesis will be demoted to a less probable
status, however, in the Caulian-Crawfordian synthesis yet to come later in this series.
       As it currently stands in Part 1 of this series, prequalification, in principle, is also suggested by parabolic
details. In the parable of the talents, the most productive servants are rewarded first, likewise in the parable of the
minas. In the parable of the workers, similarly, those who worked last are rewarded first, and the note is added that
many who are last will be first. This principle of prequalification is not only useful for the Crawfordian model but
also for the Caulian model. For my classic model, it answers the question as to how unfaithful believers can reside
in the lower level of the city even before being judged at the Bema. My original anticipation was that unfaithful
believers do not meet the prequalification criteria for white garments and thus live in the lower level—the outer
court—even prior to the Bema.66 After the Bema, they are sent back to the outer court (i.e., the lower level of the
city) and remain there until the full effects of the Bema are carried out by their ejection from the city (to the outer
darkness in the lower hemisphere of the planet) at the beginning of the millennial kingdom. This migratory pattern
continues as Gizrah is expanded (see TOD). The regret of those cast into the outer darkness will be profound indeed.

65
   This is not the only option available to the Crawfordian model. Crawford could opt to follow Berner, for example,
in posing that church-age believers are pictured in both Rev 6:9-11 and 7:9-17. If so, they already could have been
awarded white robes at the Bema. Hence, their presence in the city is to be expected.
66
   Later in this series, I will favor a Caulian-Crawfordian model in which the unfaithful never live in the city.
                              Chapter 2. Models for Regret
                                     Introduction
       The discussion as to whether negative rewards are eternal is an objective consideration. Even if negative
rewards are eternal, it is not a foregone conclusion in the minds of some misthologists that the regret that normally
would accompany the loss of rewards is eternal. Regret is a subjective consideration. At least in theory, subjective
regret can be separated from the objective loss of rewards so that the nature and duration of regret can be examined
as an independent study, which is what we will do here.
                                                   Dillow’s Argument
        Dillow allows regret, even profound regret, from a temporary point of view. He projects that whatever regrets
unfaithful believers may have about being cast into the outer darkness will be swept away quickly in light of eternity.
In Darkness (ch. 23, also cf. ch. 24), I had interacted with Dillow’s section The Duration of Remorse from his earlier
book, Reign (pp. 530-532). On this matter we disagree, in that I hold that such regret is eternal. While proofreading
Destiny, I pointed out that he would need to note his disagreement with my position so that his Lordship Salvation
critics would not blindside him with this omission. He did so and expanded his defense of his temporary-regret
position by adding four additional paragraphs within this section and interjecting a couple of additional references.
In the 20th edition of Destiny, this section is on pp. 761-763.
        According to Dillow, because Rom 14:10-12 proves that everyone, lost and saved, “will confess to God”
(emphasis his), when they stand before Him in judgment, we must conclude that “unfaithful Christians will bow
down and confess. When a Christian confesses, he is forgiven....(1 John 1:9). Therefore, claims that unfaithful
believers will not be restored must be dismissed.”1 I am not offended in the least that Dillow cites my book at the
conclusion of this statement and is, therefore, challenging my position, saying, in essence, that I must abandon my
claim that unfaithful believers will not be restored. In fact, for Dillow’s sake, I encouraged him to note our
differences on this matter, lest he be accused by our critics of overlooking our disagreement. Accordingly, he has
stated his conviction forcefully, which is fine. I appreciate his forceful writing style. As one may see, I share the
same stylistic propensity. But I would just as strongly counter: His claim that unfaithful believers will be restored
must be abandoned.
        As to the evidence he cites in this section, frankly, I question Dillow’s use of 1Jn 1:9 to prove that a
postmortem confession of sin on the part of unfaithful believers restores them to full fellowship with God at the
time of that confession, supposedly, just like the prodigal son. First, did John intend for his statement in 1Jn 1:9 to
apply to postmortem confession? Has Dillow moved beyond the intention of the biblical writer in his application of
the biblical promise? I always considered this verse to be a promise of present fellowship for living believers if they
confess their sins in this life, now (cp. 1Jn 2:28), as they walk in the light (1Jn 1:7), not then when they stand before
the Judgment Seat of Christ (JSC): “If we [living believers] confess our sins [now], He is faithful and righteous to
forgive us our sins and to cleanse us from all unrighteousness” (1Jn 1:9, so my understanding). Dillow wants to
expand this verse, it seems, beyond the scope intended by John in this context to mean: “If we [living or dead
believers] confess our sins [now or then], He is [and will be] faithful and righteous to forgive us our sins and to
cleanse us from all unrighteousness” (1Jn 1:9). Of course, John does move on rather quickly to the future JSC, but
not by bypassing the present: “And now [in the present], little children, abide in Him [which includes confessing
sins], so that when He appears [then], we may have confidence and not shrink away from Him in shame at His
coming [then at the JSC]” (1Jn 2:28). The way we live now (nun) will have a serious impact on this future outcome
then at the JSC. Would Dillow exclude the present time from this temporal marker now? I hope not. Has Dillow
perceived something that John failed to perceive? If Dillow is correct in his postmortem applicability of this 1Jn 1:9,
then John is very shortsighted, it would seem, in the timing of this warning. Given Dillow’s postmortem insight,
did John not see how his statement in 1Jn 1:9 would amend his warning in 1Jn 2:28 so that the latter would now be
a yawner. Dillow apparently understands 1Jn 2:28, in light of 1Jn 1:9, to mean: “And now, little children, abide in

Him, because when He appears you will bow before Him and confess your sins to Him and be restored to full
fellowship so that you will have confidence and not shrink away from Him in shame at His coming even if you do
not abide in Him now.” Dillow’s proposal seems to turn the warning in 1Jn 2:28 into nonsense. It would be better
to stick with Dillow’s insightful use of 1Jn 2:28 elsewhere, than to adopt his misguided use of 1Jn 1:9 here in trying
to negate postmortem regret. The reason John is so concerned that we abide in the Lord and confess Him now in
the present is because we will be shrinking away from Him in shame at the Bema if we have not abided in Him
during our time on Earth. Dillow completely misconstrues the relationship between 1Jn 1:9 and 1Jn 2:28 when he
tries to turn 1Jn 1:9 into a postmortem text.
        Second, what about all those passages which seem to suggest that regret exists on the other side of the Bema,
after the supposed confession of sin at the time the JSC has been made, texts concerning unfaithful saints, about
which Dillow and I agree and which he himself mentions on the very next page, no less!?! He acknowledges:

             We are told that even in the kingdom that unfaithful saints of all ages will know and the
      experience of weeping because of being excluded from the Messianic Banquet (Luke 13:28). One
      can imagine the Lord at some time after the beginning of the millennium, forgiving and restoring
      to fellowship all those unfaithful Christians for whom He died and for whom His loving heart still
      desires. (Destiny, 762.)

        A few questions please. Which is it? Are they restored to fellowship when they confess their sins at the Bema,
as Dillow leads us to believe on p. 761 (in relation to the time when we give account of ourselves to the Lord), or
are they restored some undefined period of time later, perhaps hundreds of years later, after the millennium has
begun, as Dillow indicates here on p. 762? How does the mere turn of the page allow Dillow to pose that the
restoration (supposedly given at the time of the JSC) does not come into effect until after the millennium has started,
presumably hundreds of years later during the millennium? Why does the Lord’s death and heart desire, to which
Dillow appeals, take hundreds of years to forgive and restore them on p. 762 but only seconds on p. 761? How are
unfaithful believers being excluded from the banquet by the Bema on p. 762 but restored to fellowship at the Bema
on p. 761? The fact that one can imagine such contradictory scenarios with the flip of a page does not mean that
one can support them biblically or logically. We need evidence, not mere imagination, to support such inferences.2
        Third, what about those texts which indicate that forgiveness in one sphere or plane does not necessarily
result in forgiveness in another sphere or plane, which are additional arguments that I have set forth in my writings.
For example, see “Four Planes of Forgiveness” (TOD, 348). That treatment has been greatly expanded in 4DF. For
instance, I state therein concerning Dillow and myself:

             We both use a provisional view of the atonement to refute the charge of double payment (pp.
2
  Dillow also says, “Our ability to deal with the emotion of grief will be heightened and immeasurably more mature”
(Destiny, 763). Perhaps this is correct, then again, why assume that reaching Heaven makes all believers mature?
More likely, our spiritual capacity to experience immeasurably more maturity will be determined, to a large extent,
by the degree of maturity we develop now. The capacity to grow immeasurably in maturity is probably a reward
for the faithful, not a gift to the unfaithful. Our spiritual I.Q., so to speak, and thus the capacity to grow in this
manner, will be determined, to a considerable extent, by our endeavor to grow now. Such growth is a reward. In
any event, not many pages earlier, Dillow explained that at the Bema the unfaithful believer “winced in pain. This
was heightened in intensity because of the greater sensitivity to sin of the resurrection body” (p. 751).
Unquestionably, Dillow is correct that the greater sensitivity to sin will result in heightened pain by those who are
cast into the outer darkness. Not caring about eternal rewards is a sin. They will no longer be able to sin by not
caring about eternal rewards. The logical extension of Dillow’s argument on p. 763, unfortunately, seems to indicate
that they will not care about their loss of rewards since they supposedly will not regret their loss of rewards: “Regrets
over a failed life will be erased.” If so, what Dillow actually seems to attribute to them is sin rather than maturity.
In any case, his statement on p. 763 is strained by his statement on p. 751. On the one hand, he says that our
sensitivity to sin will result in a heightened degree of pain for those cast into the outer darkness. On the other hand,
he says that their ability to deal with this pain will be heightened. Which is it? Is their sense of pain heightened or
is it lessened? Dillow speaks out of both sides of his mouth when answering this question. For that matter, why
does the Bema result in heightened pain in the first place if confession at the Bema supposedly restores us to
fellowship and removes the pain. His discussion is painfully strained.
Chapter 2. Models for Regret                                                                                   Page 53
      922-927). We both believe that a believer’s unconfessed sins are remembered and judged at the Bema
      (p. 919). Dillow admits that he is speculating when he theorizes that [a] carnal believer’s confessed
      sins will not be judged at the Bema so that the carnal believer is not cast into the outer darkness
      (p. 922). My hypothesis, in contrast, is that, in accord with the Principle of Stratification (as explained
      herein), the forgiveness of sins in one stratum or compartment does not ensure forgiveness of those
      sins in a higher stratum or compartment. Dillow perceives this stratification to a degree. He would
      agree, for instance, that soteriological forgiveness is distinguishable from temporal and misthological
      forgiveness. He correctly acknowledges that the forgiveness entailed in Ps 103:8-12 is a reward for
      those “Christians who continue to walk in the light” and “stay in the race” (pp. 322, 929) and that the
      confession of sins on the part of carnal believers does not prevent their rewards from being “small”
      (p. 922). A carnal believer “will still be held accountable as a servant.” “His reward will be minimal.
      His loss will be great” (p. 930). Accordingly, I conclude that mere confession will not necessarily
      change the misthological outcome for the carnal. The present study demonstrates that familial
      forgiveness, stemming from confession on the part of carnal believers, does not necessarily result in
      their misthological forgiveness. They may be forgiven as children, but not as servants. Consider the
      unmerciful/carnal servant in Mt 18:23-35. (4DF, 40, n. 43.)

      Because misthological forgiveness is a reward and because rewards (and lack thereof) are eternal, this
misthological level of forgiveness can never be granted to those believers who did not qualify for this reward.
Dillow is being completely inconsistent when he correctly acknowledges that misthological forgiveness is a reward
and then pretends that 1Jn 1:9 could result in forgiveness in the misthological plain for those believers who fail “to
walk in the light” or “stay in the race.”
      Much later, Dillow inconsistently inserts a quotation from Isaiah and makes, what he thinks, is a justified,
blanket conclusion applicable to all believers: “‘The former things will not be remembered, nor will they come to
mind’ (Isaiah 65:17). Regrets over a failed life will be erased” (p. 762, emphasis his). He supports this assessment
with a quote from Motyer’s commentary on Isaiah: “The awareness will be of a total newness without anything
even prompting a recollection of what used to be. The divine forgetfulness of verse 16 ff. will be matched by general
amnesia” (p. 762, n. 2444). Alcorn explains how the mechanics would work:

              God chooses not to bring up our past sins or hold them against us. In eternity, past sins and
      sorrows won’t preoccupy God or us. We’ll be capable of choosing not to recall or dwell on something
      that would diminish Heaven’s joy....
              The comfort implies memory of what happened. If we had no memory of the bad things, why
      would we need comfort? How could we feel it?
              Our minds will be clearer in Heaven, not foggier. Memory is basic to personality. The law of
      continuity requires that we will remember our past lives. Heaven cleanses our slate of sin and error,
      but it doesn’t erase our memory....
              Even though God will wipe away the tears and sorrow attached to this world, he will not erase
      our minds....Heaven’s happiness won’t be dependent on our ignorance of what happened on
      Earth....but on our perspective. (Heaven, 331-332; emphasis his. For the law of continuity in which we
      must retain our memories, see his pp. 278-279.)

       Unlike the general consensus, however, in which it is presumed that this ability for a selective recall and
positive perspective that removes all unhappiness will apply with equal effectiveness to all believers, in the very
page range of my book (Darkness, 519-564) which Dillow notes on this same page (Destiny, 762, n. 2446), I had
placed this verse within its OT context (Darkness, 558, cp. 605), both in terms of time and location. Briefly,
residents of the Reward City are rewarded with this comfort. Being misthologically blameless is a reward given to
the faithful, not a gift given to the unfaithful. This verse does nothing to help Dillow’s argument. This general
amnesia, if one wants to call it that, applies only to overcomers. Misthological amnesia for the faithful, not
soteriological amnesia for all believers, is contextually envisioned.
       Or if one wants a simple rebuttal of Dillow’s position regarding forgiveness on p. 762 in general and 1Jn 1:9
in particular, just read his earlier comments on p. 322, where he correctly states:

             It is true that God casts trespasses from us (Psalm 103:12) and that He casts our “sins behind
Page 54                                                                                  Misthological Models Part 1
      [His] back” (Isaiah 38:17). But does this apply to the believer who does not repent of his sins (1 John
      1:9)? As for the promise that He will “remember their sins no more” (Hebrews 8:12; 10:17), this refers
      to remembering them in the sense of applying the penalty of eternal damnation.

        Per Dillow, 1Jn 1:9 does not apply to believers who do not repent of their sins. His postmortem appeal to
1Jn 1:9 crashes upon his own acknowledgment that this verse does not apply to those believers who do not repent.
In fact, in another edition of Destiny, Dillow is even more forthright with this acknowledgment: “The passages
about God removing our trespasses from us (Psalm 103:12) or ‘casting sins behind [His] back’ (Isaiah 38:17) do
not apply to the believer who does not repent of his sins (1 John 1:9).”3 Dillow cannot logically appeal to tardy
repentance because this type of forgiveness is a reward, and, as such, it is conditioned on our works and repentance
in this life, not in the hereafter.
        Fourth, Dillow’s appeal to the prodigal son, on p. 762, as a postmortem example, seems strained as well. If
this parable is teaching that a postmortem return results in restoration, one would be left to conclude that dining
with the swine now prevents no barrier to dining with the Lord then. Give this postmortem extrapolation, if you
dine with the swine now, throughout the entire course of your earthly life as an unfaithful Christian, you will be
restored to table fellowship with the Lord at the JSC. Is this the position that Dillow has taken? No. He correctly
acknowledges that they are excluded from the Messianic Banquet. Thus, in trying to hang onto his temporary-regret
theory, Dillow seems to have landed himself in an inextricable predicament, according to which, at the same
moment you are excluded from the table, you are welcomed to the table.4
        Fifth, his supposition that confession and repentance will take place at the JSC is open to question. In
Darkness, I concurred with Wilkin and Hodges that the believer who was speechless at the Bema in Mt 22:12 was
prevented by his sinless nature from making an excuse at the Bema so that the contrast between this believer’s
silence versus the appeal by those in Mt 7:22 to their works suggests that the latter are unbelievers who are not
silenced from trying to justify themselves by their performance. Dillow, however, has now come to believe that
Mt 7:22 depicts believers at the JSC. Neoclassically, I am very open to Dillow’s perspective on this perspective of
the matter. But if he is correct regarding the Bema applicability in Mt 7:22, then does this not mean that he is
incorrect in regarding 1Jn 1:9 as being necessarily applicable to the JSC? The believers appearing before the JSC
in Mt 7:22 (if that is indeed what is happening, so Dillow), do not bow and confess their sins. Instead, they try to
excuse their sins by appealing to their good works. Rather than being embraced as a result of their tardy repentance,
as Dillow would have us believe happens universally to unfaithful believers, they are rebuked and rejected.5

  Joseph C. Dillow, Final Destiny: The Future Reign of The Servant Kings, Revised Edition (Grace Theology Press,

  Dillow inserts a reference to Ps 30:5, evidently as indicating that postmortem weeping only last for a night even
for unfaithful believers. Yet the context appears to be saying that premortem weeping only last for the night for
godly believers (Ps 30:4). This particular citation is not very persuasive for his argument. As to his assumption that
“those who love Him” in 1Cor 2:9 applies to all believers, I ask for proof, especially considering the fact that neither
Dillow nor the Scripture seem to make that assumption elsewhere. Consider Ex 20:6; Dt 5:10; 7:9; Jdg 5:31;
Neh 1:5; Prov 8:17,21; Dan 9:4; Mt 6:24; Jn 14:15,23-24; Jam 1:12; 2:5; 1Jn 2:15; 4:20. Given the preponderance
of such evidence, I agree with Rene Lopez that even in Rom 8:28 “the phrase those who love God refers only to
faithful believers as opposed to all Christians” (Romans, GNTC, 667; italics his).
5
  Misthological harmonization between the two passages might be attempted along various avenues. First, one could
pose that some believers cast into the outer darkness will be speechless, but not all. Those with a lot of great works
might try to excuse themselves. Second, one could pose that those in Mt 7:22 are in flesh and blood bodies. Thus,
they are not prevented from sinfully trying to excuse their actions. Third, one could entertain the possibility that
they misunderstand why they are being cast into the outer darkness, thinking that their great works would save them
from it since being saved from it is based on a believer’s works; therefore, they sincerely, but not sinfully, try to
present evidence in their favor.
     One can be sincere, but be sincerely wrong, yet being sincerely wrong is not necessarily sin. Even in Heaven
one still can make mistakes and miss the mark. (Frank D. Carmical, The Omega Reunion, 72.) Thus, their mistaken
understanding and trying their best to justify themselves by their performance need not necessarily be considered
sin, especially if they are trying to justify themselves misthologically rather than soteriologically. Trying to define
sin simplistically in terms of classical Greek as missing a mark is imprudent. We will not all be able to hit a hole-
in-one on every shot while playing golf in Heaven. Missing the mark will not mean that we have sinned. Sinless
Chapter 2. Models for Regret                                                                                Page 55
       Sixth, Dillow appeals to the fact that we are with the Lord as grounds for concluding that we will not weep.
Yet this position is self-refuting as well. Dillow would have been better off not adding this new defense to his
already strained argument when he claims: “In view of the glorious promises of heavenly life promised to justified
saints, we can be assured that this remorse cannot continue throughout the millennium or eternal state. All will be
‘with Him,’ and not in the ‘outer darkness’ (John 14:3)” (Destiny, 761; emphasis his).6 Really? I find no such
assurance that remorse will be removed for those believers who refused to repent during the course of their lives.
The alleged assurance that being with the Lord prevents weeping is obviously false since being with the Lord in
His kingdom does not prevent a believer from weeping in the outer darkness! Dillow knows this! He correctly
acknowledges that those who are cast into the outer darkness and weep there are believers. They are in the kingdom
and thus with the Lord. Yet one would think from Dillow’s argument on this page that being with the Lord precludes
one from being in the outer darkness. Not so! Dillow acknowledges this to be so, so why does he say that being
with the Lord means that one cannot be in the outer darkness? Moreover, he recognizes that being with the Lord
does not prevent them from experiencing remorse. To the contrary, their appearing before the Lord is what causes
them remorse! In fact, to the statement above, Dillow immediately adds: “In fact we will ‘always be’ with Him
(1 Thessalonians 4:17)” (p. 761, emphasis his). True. All believers will always be with the Lord. Since being with
the Lord applies to all believers always, from the point of the rapture forward, and since it does not prevent them
from being cast away from the Lord into the outer darkness, at the time of the Bema, then obviously being with the
Lord does not mean that they cannot be cast away from Him into some other part of the kingdom. When unfaithful
believers are cast from Him into the outer darkness, they are still with Him in some other sense. Dillow’s own
arguments concede this point and therefore refute his own argument that being with the Lord must mean that they
cannot be in the outer darkness...indefinitely or even eternally. We can summarize this syllogistically.
                                   Illustration 24. Away from Him is with Him
1. All believers will always be with the Lord (as Dillow and I concur).
2. Some believers are cast away from the Lord into the outer darkness where they weep (as we also concur).
3. Therefore, being with the Lord (in the sense being affirmed in the major premise) does not prevent one from
   being cast away from the Lord into the outer darkness where they weep (which is the sense being affirmed in
   the minor premise).

       Being with the Lord in one sense (i.e., being soteriologically in the kingdom), does not preclude being cast
away from Him in another sense (i.e., being misthologically in the outer darkness). Nor does being with Him
soteriologically prevent those who are with Him from experiencing profound regret. Believers are always with the
Lord in some sense, even when they are weeping in the outer darkness! Dillow himself acknowledges that those
being cast into the outer darkness are being told to depart from the Lord. Clearly, always being with the Lord does
not prevent one from being cast away from Him. With and from are simply being used spatially in reference to two
different locations. Harmonization is maintained easily if one spatially understands the outer darkness as being a
separate place within the kingdom. Dillow’s hesitancy to adopt a spatial model is perhaps a contributing factor as

students in Heaven trying to solve complex math problems will make mistakes. This does not mean that they will
have sinned. All sin is falling short, but not all falling short is sin. By the NT time sin no longer simply meant
“‘missing the mark’ or ‘failing when trying’; rather, sin was considered to be “deliberate disobedience....the word
always had an ethical sense of not conforming to a standard.” R.S. Beal and Earl D. Radmacher, Ephesians: Life
and Love in Christ (Chino Valley: One World Press, 2012), 72. Also, see Does God Make Mistakes? in Part Two
of the present series.
6
  The superficial nature of Dillow’s appeal to Jn 14:3 is all the more conspicuous in that I deal with this verse at
length in Darkness (pp. 337-388, 604-606). To give but a summary statement here, Jesus had indicated that, while
being with Him in the kingdom was a gift conditioned simply on new birth (Jn 3:3-5), being with Him in the special
sense of being exactly where He is at in His Father’s kingdom, in a place of honor, is a reward based on service
(Jn 12:46). This special place (topos) in Jn 14:3 is an intimate place of bridal intimacy, namely, the Bridal City.
Having one’s abode with the Father and Son, in this sense, is conditioned on lovingly obeying the Son (Jn 14:21-
31). In short, Jn 14:3 better supports my model than his. While it might be objected that this counter does not apply
to Dillow’s model since he does not take a misthological view of the bride, the principle component of my rejoinder
is still valid in that Dillow acknowledges that all believers will be with Jesus in His kingdom but not with Him in
other capacities.
Page 56                                                                                  Misthological Models Part 1
to why he overlooked this glaring inconsistency within his argument. Even so, the surprising number of
inconsistencies evidenced in this part of Dillow’s book gives one the impression that he is futilely grasping at straws
in a vain attempt to defend his temporary-regret model. If these are the best arguments that even a great writer of
Dillow’s stature can muster for the temporary-regret model, then the temporary-regret model must be weak indeed.
       Thankfully, Dillow has not found such inconsistencies within his model to be a distraction in his life from
serving the Lord. His personal testimony is that he can serve the Lord faithfully even while believing that he would
have no lasting regret if he were not to serve the Lord. Fine. Such a testimony is a powerful testimony as to the
motivating power of the other reasons that Dillow lists for serving the Lord.
       On the other hand, Dillow may be granting his testimony a universal power that it does not have. Evidently,
his personal testimony is supposed to reassure us that no one needs an eternal-regret model to encourage them in
their service to the Lord. But the extrapolation of his personal testimony so as to grant it universal application is a
two-edged sword. Atheists claim that they can live morally good lives without God. Should we accordingly discount
the need of God to live morally good lives based on their personal testimonies? Some of them live morally good
lives with a no-judgment model. Should we suppose that a no-judgment model is as good as a judgment model?
Obviously, Dillow and I would both concur that this is not the case. Atheistic motivation is a poor substitution for
theistic motivation. Still, why should one totally discount their atheistic testimony (about not needing to be
motivated by fear of eternal judgment) as being inferior and yet count Dillow’s testimony (about not needing to be
motivated by fear of eternal regret) as being superior? Want a closer parallel? What about those believers who think
that the JSC will be nothing more than an awards podium or educational experience. They hold to a no-regret model
of the JSC. Are we to assume that a no-regret model of the Bema is just as good as a regret model of the Bema?
No. Thankfully, Dillow at least maintains that some regret occurs at the JSC. A some-regret model is better than a
no-regret model. I ended Darkness (959-962) with a stern warning to soteriologists who advocate a no-regret
model. Would one really think that such a warning will not prove to be applicable, at least in part, to misthological
advocates who advocate a temporary-regret model?
       A temporary-regret model would tell believers struggling to fight off suicidal thoughts that they will have no
eternal regrets if they cave in and commit suicide. Is Dillow dead certain that believers struggling with suicidal
thoughts should be encouraged that they will not regret it if they commit this deadly sin? Admittedly, not all
believers facing such suicidal struggles need to be warned of eternal regret in order to overcome suicidal
temptations. Even so, what of those who do need such strong medicine? Evidence suggests that some of those facing
suicide need to be told that they are contemplating doing something that they will regret forever (see Darkness,
697-699). But if some suicidal believers wind up committing suicide because they are told that they are going to a
happier place and will have no lasting regrets, what will be the outcome for those who gave them that
misinformation if it turns out to be wrong? If such struggling believers end up in the outer darkness because of a
misthologically accursed gospel that failed to warn them adequately of the eternal repercussions of sin, what will
be the repercussions for the one who gave them that misthologically accursed gospel?
                                   Illustration 25. His Full Cup is Full of Holes
Chapter 2. Models for Regret                                                                                     Page 57
        Dillow ends his defense of the temporary-regret model with the popular cup illustration: Everyone, faithful
and unfaithful alike, purportedly will have a cup full of joy; they will just have different sized cups. I hate that cup
illustration. Commit suicide and you will have a cup full of joy with no regrets. Really? Is that the medicine that
Dillow offers to the suicidal? Supposedly, unfaithful church-age believers will weep for a few seconds or years
(depending on which of Dillow’s pages you happen to be reading at the time) and then have their tears wiped away
and their cup of joy made full; although, their cup will not be as large as faithful believers. Granted, this cup theory
is very popular. I suppose it is presumed to work something like stomach surgery works today. Some people have
their stomach stapled so that it is functionally smaller. As a result they will not eat so much. In theory they will not
mind because they will feel full after eating very little. Likewise, in this popular cup model, you will feel full of joy
even if you have less to be joyful about. Thus, you will not mind being less joyful than others. In fact, you will be
joyful about it. After all, your cup of joy will be full, and you will have no regrets. So if you have the misthological
equivalent of a stapled stomach, you will not regret it, at least not for very long, so Dillow.
                                      Illustration 26. Maximum Positive Model




        I find the Dillowian variation of this cup/regret model particularly interesting. In his model, unfaithful church-
age believers will weep for seven years (depending on which of his arguments you are contemplating). Unfaithful
tribulational believers, on the other hand, will weep possibly for a maximum of one thousand years. Why the double
standard? Even more glaring, what about unfaithful millennial believers? Will they only weep for one day? Is their
so-called profound regret as fleeting as the morning frost? Even in the severest Dillowian scenario, is a potential
thousand years of profound regret really very long in light of eternity? Mathematically, one thousand divided by
positive infinity (i.e., eternity future) is practically equal to nothing (1000/∞+ ≈ 0). Regrettably, from the
perspectives of such regret models, the bottom line for Dillow’s cup model is that if you do not live for Christ,
then you will not regret it, at least not long enough for it to amount to anything. Neither Crawford nor I subscribe
to this popular cup theory. Still, it is interesting to see how Dillow tries to reconcile his affirmation of the cup
perspective with a sound misthology. At least his attempting to do so has the positive benefit of providing another
competing misthological model regarding regret. For that reason, I am thankful for our interaction, even though we
strongly disagree on this particular area. Misthologists thus have several options from which to choose as to which
regret model they find most accurate in relation to the biblical data.
        In my depiction of Dillow’s model above, consider the scenario in which an unfaithful church-age believer
(let’s call him, P.R., for profound regret) dies and is immediately taken to the city, is shown the various parts of the
city, and is allowed to visit briefly, and then is escorted to the courtyard, where he is told that he will have to wait
with everyone else, until the city is ready for residency after the Bema. Exuberating thoughts fill P.R.’s heart with
joyful anticipation. There is nothing but pure joy in his prospects! But after being in the courtyard for some time,
Page 58                                                                                   Misthological Models Part 1
P.R. notices that some of his fellow disembodied souls have white robes, and he does not. So he inquiries into the
possibility of acquiring one. He is informed that these robes are only available for those who laid down their lives
for Christ. He was no martyr, not anything close to it. So P.R. reasons, “Good for them. They deserve it.” Still, P.R
has a little regret that he did not do more, at least take up his cross metaphorically speaking, and in that sense lay
down his life for Christ. After all, those white robes look very appealing, and he would like to have earned one. Yet
he consoles himself with the fact that he is in Heaven, which is far more than he deserves, so there is no need to
bemoan his not having such a robe. However, he soon hears a rumor. Some are speculating as to what their
experience at the Bema will be like. Is it like an awards banquet, in which a few of the top performers are given
plaques and trophies while those who do not receive such rewards will sit and clap in appreciation? This seems to
be the majority opinion, but a few are speculating that there might be negative repercussions for those who did not
lay down their lives for Christ, in the metaphorical sense at least. Naturally, P.R. takes comfort in the majority
consensus which, not surprisingly, was the majority position on Earth as well. On the other hand, P.R. is a little
surprised that being in Heaven did not harmonize everyone’s opinion. Differences of opinion still exist. Thus, some
must be mistaken in their opinion, even in Heaven. Who would have thought that to be the case in Heaven? These
differences cause him some concern, but he soon learns to squelch his concerns by adopting the majority
perspective. After all, that is what he did back in his days on Earth.
        At last the rapture occurs, and everyone is gathered before the Bema! Paul and the apostles come up and are
well rewarded. Everyone, including P.R. and other unclothed believers, clap and cheer those being rewarded. It is
just like an awards banquet after all! Eventually, all those wearing white robes are summoned one by one and
rewarded appropriately. None of those rewarded have any regrets whatsoever. They are more than thrilled with
whatever degree of reward they have received, and they genuinely are happy about the rewards received by others.
They have no regrets. At last, after all the clothed believers are rewarded, the first believer who did not have a white
robe is summoned to stand before the King. The mood changes quickly, however, as everyone realizes that the King
is no longer smiling. Stifling silence fills the arena. More silence. Dillow has well described what happens next:

            Then with a stern look on His face, the King rebuked His unfaithful follower, “Depart from Me,
      you wicked, lazy slave.”
            “Noooo . . . ,” the lazy servant screamed. But the angels came and led him to the darkness
      outside the city square where he began to weep and gnash his teeth in profound regret. (Destiny, 752)

        This depiction is excellent! Further, it illustrates the point I was just making, per Dillow’s own writings:
Always being with the Lord does not prevent one from being cast from the Lord. Unfortunately, however, Dillow
does not take himself literally but just intends for us to take this fictional scenario as being a mere metaphor. In that
case, I would like to have a working model that actually works, from his perspective, as to what literally will take
place. What does this spatial exclusion really look like? Being left with the impression that they will go nowhere
because there is nowhere else to go since they are in Heaven is not a working model. The Bema is literal. Heaven
is literal. Both are places. What will happen spatially at the Bema for those who do poorly? Nothing? Or are they
screaming in horror at the thought of being cast into New Jerusalem, as Dillow is forced to speculate by his model?
        For reasons already discussed, I believe that Dillow’s metaphorical model necessarily lends itself to spatial
extrapolations. We have not been told everything, but we have been given sufficient information by which to make
reasonable projections and to draft workable models.7 Proceeding, then, with the Crawfordian-Dillowian model as
7
  Dillow was concerned that my models might be guilty of over-interpreting. That concern is duly noted, but my
counter concern is that limiting the data to the sphere of metaphor is guilty of under-interpreting. In the field of
eschatology, for example, should dispensationalists throw up their hands in despair because of the complexities
involved and become panmillennialists, saying, “It will all pan out in the end”? Absolutely not! Nor should we, as
dispensationalists, cave in to the criticisms by amillennialists as we construct what they consider to be overly
complex eschatological charts. To give up on such details would not only derail our dispensational heritage but
throw the perspicuity of Scripture into question.
     Correspondingly, panmisthology or amisthology is no more becoming a misthologist than panmillennialism or
amillennialism is becoming to a millennialist. As dispensationalists, we realize how important a literal hermeneutic
is to eschatology. Anderson and Hawley, among others, have shown that to compromise our dispensational heritage
in the eschatological arena, by abandoning a literal hermeneutical, can have tragic results in the area of soteriology.
The very heart of the gospel can become corrupted. See David R. Anderson, “The Soteriological Impact of
Chapter 2. Models for Regret                                                                                   Page 59
being a plausible draft of the spatial implications of Dillow’s model, I would amend Dillow’s statement as follows:
“But the angels came and led him to the courtyard outside the city where he began to weep and gnash his teeth in
profound regret.” There is no darkness inside the city; this detail is metaphorical, but not meaningless. Several
reasonable models can provide highly probable scenarios as to its literal inference. If this darkness refers to
exclusion from the city, then it would be the courtyard outside the city (so the Crawfordian model). If it refers to
exclusion experienced inside the city, then it could be pictured as being cast into the outer court in the lower section
of the city (in the theoretical Caulian model being entertained in Part 1, only to be rejected as the series proceeds).
       Regardless of the metaphorical or literal models employed, however, all regret models concur that P.R. will
weep with “profound regret.” In Dillow’s model, though, this profound regret is limited to something along the
lines of a few seconds, days, or seven years, or a thousand years, depending (in part) on the time period the unfaithful
believers are from and what part of Dillow’s arguments you are reading at the time. According to Dillow, when the
tears of the unfaithful are wiped away, their cup is filled and their regret is removed. No room for an eternal negative
subjective response to the Bema is possible. Regret is short-lived. An eternal positive subjective variation is allowed
(per the filled-cup theory). They have eternal joy. The objective variation might be considered to have a negative
dimension. Having no co-rulership, for example, is an objective negative result. But per Dillow’s temporary-regret
model, the subjective response cannot allow a concurrent negative frequency that dips into the negative arena.
Whatever objective differences may exist between unfaithful and faithful believers, unfaithful believers can rest
assured, per Dillow, that throughout eternity they will not regret having been unfaithful. In terms of negative
subjectivity, this is the bottom line for the Dillowian regret model when extrapolated into the eternal state:
                                   Illustration 27. Dillowian Eternal Variations
•   Objective Variation:                  True, believers will have different degrees of rewards.
•   Positive Subjective Variation:        True, all believers will be happy, but not equally happy about their reward.
•   Negative Subjective Variation:        False, no believer will have regrets or anything less than joy unspeakable.

      For competing misthological models regarding regret, the third point is the one in contention. My model will
pose that this profound regret is eternal. As a plausible corollary, this regret may spill over into tears on occasion
throughout eternity. My eternal-regret model is at the opposite end of the misthological spectrum from that of
Dillow’s temporary-regret model. Whereas Dillow’s is at the positive end, projecting no eternal regret, mine is at
the negative end, projecting eternal regret that can result in occasional tears. As to the wiping away of tears in


Augustine’s Change from Premillennialism to Amillennialism: Part 1,” JOTGES 15:28 (Spring 2002): 25-36. Also
see Grant Hawley, “Dispensationalism And Free Grace: Intimately Linked” Part 1, Part 2, and Part 3 JOTGES, each
part available respectively in three PDF files:: Part 1: https://faithalone.org/journal/2011i/Hawley-4.pdf; Part 2:

     Compromising literalicity regarding the city, of which the outer darkness is only a part, may have far more
significant repercussions than are perceived initially. This approach does not mean that we interpret everything
literally, of course, but as dispensationalists we cannot retreat from our pronounced propensity towards literality.
This propensity should be no less prevalent among misthologists. Misthology is a vital part of eschatology. Indeed,
misthology is a key component of prophecy. Let us seek the literal implications of the metaphorical details and not
be too quick to equivocate what might well be a literal detail with a metaphorical ambiguity. Calvinists retreat into
ambiguity, claiming mystery, when faced with the logical inconsistencies within their soteriology. Let us not follow
their example in the field of misthology. Perspicuity must not be surrendered on the altar of ambiguity as a stopgap
measure to avoid facing the logical implications of our models or to dismiss any inconsistencies found therein. If
interpreting the length of the millennium literally is considered so critical to our dispensational system and to our
underlying hermeneutic, should we be so quick to assume that the dimensions of the city are metaphorical?
Nevertheless, if the dimensions themselves do prove to be metaphorical, then surely they point to the literal
metaphysical reality that surpasses those dimensions. The dimensions are not meaningless details but circumscribe
the metaphysical reality to which they point (at least within that 3D dimension). The millennium does not encompass
all eternity future, and the city is not all the metaphysical reality we call Heaven or the kingdom. We cannot equate
the city with Heaven or with the kingdom. Heaven and the kingdom are bigger than the city. The reality we know
by the name Heavenly City cannot be confused with the kingdom of Heaven itself. The city is a reward. Entrance
into the kingdom is a gift. Sound soteriology forbids confusing the two.
Page 60                                                                                  Misthological Models Part 1
Rev 7:17 and 21:4, I interpret the later passage in a manner consistent with my misthological understanding of the
former passage. As already noted, I understand Rev 7:9-17 as dealing with rewards for faithful tribulational
believers (though I am open to the possibility that they are faithful church-age believers). Thus, the wiping away of
tears is a reward, specifically a litotes meaning that, instead of tears, they will have joy unspeakable.8 Therefore,
my model reserves the cup full of unspeakable joy to those believers who are faithful. Unfaithful believers will not
have a cup full of joy, unmixed with regret. Having no regrets is a reward promised to and limited to faithful
believers. Syllogistically, one aspect of my impression runs along the following lines:
                                       Illustration 28. No Regrets Syllogism
                   1. Having no regrets is a reward.
                   2. Rewards are limited eternally to the faithful.
                   3. Therefore, having no regrets is a reward limited eternally to the faithful.

       If all believers eventually will have no regrets, then having no regrets could not be a reward for the faithful.
For, otherwise, having no regrets would cease to be a reward, which is impossible, because rewards do not cease.
To illustrate, consider the case of crowns.
                                          Illustration 29. Crown Syllogism
                   1. Having a crown is a reward.
                   2. Rewards are limited eternally to the faithful.
                   3. Therefore, having a crown is a reward limited eternally to the faithful.

      God does not eventually decide to give the crowns to unfaithful believers. Doing so would violate His having
made having-the-crowns a reward for the faithful. Likewise, God does not eventually decide to remove the regrets
of unfaithful believers. Doing so would violate His having made having-no-regrets a reward for the faithful.
Similarly, I understand the promise of wiping away of tears in Rev 21:4 to be a litotes promising eternal,
unspeakable joy to those believers who reside in the Reward City during the eternal state, which, in my model,
would be only faithful believers. Therefore, I do not pose that the Lord will wipe away the tears of unfaithful
believers.9 Nevertheless, I am willing to entertain the theory posed by my fellow misthologists that the tears may
be separated from regret sufficiently so that one can have the latter without the former.
                                    Illustration 30. Maximum Negative Model

       In my model, P.R. (denoting profound regret) would experience maximum regret in terms of unmediated
sorrow both at the Bema (when taken back outside to the outer court of the city) and at the end of the tribulation
(when cast into the outer darkness outside the city).10 The outer darkness is a metaphorical picture of a literal
reality—expulsion from the city, not from the kingdom. The outer darkness is a metaphorical picture of
misthopology, a doctrine taught in various ways and places in the Scripture. I am inclined to reason similarly about
the tears as I do about regrets:
                                         Illustration 31. No Tears Syllogism
                   1. Having no tears is a reward.
                   2. Rewards are limited eternally to the faithful.
                   3. Therefore, having no tears is a reward limited eternally to the faithful.11

       Nevertheless, my anticipation would be that as P.R. learns to cope with his profound regret, the tears
streaming down his face eventually reduce to a negligible amount, shed only occasionally.12 Although an underlying
regret for not having served the Lord would always be present, that regret is not tied inseparably to the expression
of tears. As a mediating model, Crawford takes this distinction and moves it one step further so as to suggest that
the tears do not last eternally, even intermittently, but the profound regret does last eternally.
                                   Illustration 32. First Mediating Regret Model




10
   The red line shows the degree of regret. Whatever the degree of regret for those suffering profound regret, the
profound regret is always there, perhaps sometimes hidden below the surface in moments of joy. As explained in
Darkness (695), we can have mixed emotions. Underlying profound regret will always exist for those believers who
live in the outer darkness, though perhaps it is hidden below the surface at times, such as when the Lord visits with
them in their part of the kingdom.
11
   Although I have pictured my no-tear conclusion deductively in the syllogism, I cannot claim deductive certainty
for my no-tear position. The problem is that promise of no tears is a litotes, promising supreme joy as a reward.
The reward of supreme joy is limited to faithful believers. I can deduce that unfaithful believers will not have that
supreme joy, but I cannot infer, with deductive certainty, that they will have tears. I can only infer that they will not
have the reality depicted by the no-tear promise, which is supreme joy, a joy untainted with sorrow. I am bound by
logic from inferring with absolute certainty that the level of sorrow necessarily experienced by subcomers
axiomatically proves the presence of tears. In the present discussion, I am content with inductive probability and
with indicating the probable presence of eternal tears in the case of subcomers.
12
   Yes, there are stages to grief, as Dillow notes. But contrary to Dillow, not all grief simply passes away, leaving
no regrets. Not even an infinite amount of time will completely heal these broken hearts. Some have learned to live
their earthly lives with underlying, never-ending regrets. The same will be true in Heaven.

        Basically, we might picture the Crawfordian model as raising the tear threshold a little higher on the graph.
On behalf of this mediation, let me point out that I cannot necessarily throw a litotes into full reverse.13 From the
perspective of my regret model, just because I hold that the wiping away of tears is a litotes promised to faithful
believers, I cannot automatically infer that unfaithful believers will have tears. My inference of misthologically
induced tears would have to be deduced from something else other than simply throwing a litotes into reverse.
Logically, I am bound by the laws governing contrapositives and litotes. Obviously, though, I do not have to throw
a litotes into reverse in order to deduce that unfaithful believers will experience tears of regret at the Bema or after
the Bema in the outer darkness. We have direct confirmation from Scripture that this will be the case. They will
weep and gnash their teeth for some undefined period of time. Therefore, my conclusion that those tears may extend
into the eternal state is an extrapolation open to cross examination. Crawford, therefore, entertains the possibility
that unfaithful believers eventually will learn to cope sufficiently with their profound regret so as to cease shedding
tears altogether. After all, in his model unfaithful believers will be located statically below the New Earth, so this
cessation model regarding the tears seems to be a rather reasonable extrapolation given his static perspective of the
outer darkness.
                                  Illustration 33. Second Mediating Regret Model




       Alternatively, from the perspective of my TOD model, which projects that the distance from the Reward City
and New Earth to the outer darkness dynamically increases over time (see Gizrah in Darkness), if I were to graph
a mediation of my model, so as to place a cap on the duration during which unfaithful believers may shed tears, I
would think that their degree of regret would increase over time, after first reaching a point of relative stabilization.
Though in theory they may never shed another tear, their level of regret could increase to some limited degree as
they (1) are removed further and further from the Reward City14 and (2) come to understand more fully what they
have missed out on, while faithful believers are given more and more meaningful activities. For that matter, I
consider this increased regret to be probable regardless of any conjectural mediation. The only real question is
whether such regret caps out just below or just above the tears threshold or, more likely, vacillates just above and
below the tears threshold. For the sake of mediation, I have pictured it just below the tears threshold.

Chapter 2. Models for Regret                                                                                  Page 63
        So let us put the matter of tears aside for the purpose of the present discussion. I do not need to be dogmatic
about the placement of the tears threshold. Such theoretical considerations are interesting but need not be held onto
dogmatically. More important is the joint agreement on the part of Crawford and myself that this profound regret
is eternal. The regret of those cast into the outer darkness will diminish but nevertheless continue at a fundamental
level. Clarification must be added, however; such remorse is limited to those in the outer darkness. Subjectively,
they, and they alone, will regret their subjective loss of intimacy for all eternity. Overcomers will have no regrets.
Their tears (and their tears alone) will be wiped away. Their cups (and their cups alone) will be full of unmitigated
joy. I am quite open to Dillow’s cup model, but I limit it to overcomers. Unfaithful believers will not have a cup
full of joy.
        Moreover, I am sympathetic with the basic thrust of some of Dillow’s arguments as he tries to limit the
duration of the remorse. Yet, instead of attempting to limit the duration of our regret, I would use those arguments
to limit the duration of God’s anger. Dillow rightfully appeals to fatherly imagery to help us understand the nature
of this punishment. I do so as well in my books. Still, I believe that Dillow has strained his extrapolation when he
pictures God as eagerly embracing us like returning prodigal sons at the Bema or rejoicing in our postmortem “tardy
repentance” (Destiny, 762). My impression of the scriptural evidence and my fatherly experience is evidently
different from what Dillow conveys as happening at the Bema. For example, I compare the outer darkness in TOD
to the time when my wife and I had to put Jeremy, our oldest son, in the playpen:

             These parables remind the author of a time when his oldest son, Jeremy, was a one-year-old and
      started crawling up on the coffee table in the living room. The problem was that whenever Jeremy
      climbed up on the table, he would fall off backwards and land on his head. Thankfully, the impact was
      softened by the carpeted floor; nevertheless, it was a frightful experience for his parents. His mother,
      Dianne, tried spanking him to teach him better. It did no good. He was persistent, or shall we say,
      stubborn. As a result, she decided to try something new. Whenever he climbed up on the coffee table,
      she would take him off the table and put him in the playpen. Weeping and gnashing of teeth promptly
      would follow, since Jeremy was quite aware that he had forfeited his rights to roam the house freely.
      He was now limited to one corner of the house. Not only had he lost his freedom within the house, he
      had also lost his right to toddle over to his parents whenever he wanted and get a hug and a kiss. To
      be sure, he would still get an occasional kiss on the cheek or a pat on the head, but not nearly as many
      kisses while in the playpen, and the hugs practically were forfeited. You cannot easily embrace a child
      in the playpen. (Darkness, 421.)

In another one of my books, Trichotomy, I relate the following example regarding my youngest son:

              When the author’s son, Jonathan, was very young, he misbehaved badly, while his father and
      mother were in a public setting that would not be conducive to the type of discipline his father would
      have been inclined to inflict had they been at home. Perhaps his son thought that he would be able to
      get away with such behavior since they were in public. He was mistaken. When they got back in the
      car to take the twenty-minute drive back home, his father simply said, “I will deal with you when we
      get home.” In short order Jonathan apologized for his behavior and asked for forgiveness. Forgiveness
      was granted. His father was no longer angry at him. Cognizant of this fact, Jonathan then asked, “Will
      I still be disciplined when we get home?” His father’s response was, “Yes.” The discipline would no
      longer be an expression of his father’s emotional anger, but it would still be administered as a
      demonstration of his fatherly wrath (i.e., his volitional displeasure with his son’s previous behavior).

       Jeremy’s embrace was forfeited when he was put into the playpen, and, contrary to Dillow’s expectation, I
did not rejoice in Jon’s tardy repentance. I accepted it, to be sure, and my anger was dispelled in short order, of
course. My perception is that something similar will happen in regard to the Bema and its attending consequences.
At the Bema, God will be angry with His unfaithful children, and He will punish them. Even so, He will not stay
angry with them forever. Dillow’s arguments have merit in that anticipation. Dillow quotes Ps 30:5: “For His anger
is but for a moment, His favor is for a lifetime; weeping may last for the night, but a shout of joy comes in the
morning.” Contextually, I question the full applicability of this verse to unfaithful believers. Nonetheless, more
passages could be marshaled so as to support the general impression that God will not be angry forever, even with
His disobedient children:
Page 64                                                                                   Misthological Models Part 1
                                         Illustration 34. How Long Oh Lord
•    How long, O Lord? Wilt Thou be angry forever? Will Thy jealousy burn like fire? (Ps 79:5)
•    Wilt Thou be angry with us forever? Wilt Thou prolong Thine anger to all generations? (Ps 85:5)
•    How long, O Lord? Wilt Thou hide Thyself forever? Will Thy wrath burn like fire? (Ps 89:46)
•    But now, O Lord, Thou art our Father, we are the clay, and Thou our potter; and all of us are the work of Thy
     hand. Do not be angry beyond measure, O Lord, neither remember iniquity forever; behold, look now, all of us
     are Thy people. (Is 64:8-9)
•    Will He be angry forever? Will He be indignant to the end?...“I will not look upon you in anger. For I am
     gracious,” declares the Lord; “I will not be angry forever.”15 (Jer 3:5,12)
•    For I will not contend forever, neither will I always be angry. (Is 57:16)
•    He does not retain His anger forever, because He delights in unchanging love. (Micah 7:18)

        Contra Dillow, the Lord will not rejoice in our postmortem repentance. Sometimes repentance can come too
late to avoid God’s wrath (cp. Eze 9:18). After God swore in His wrath that Israel would not be able to go into the
Promised Land (Heb 3:10-11), God did not rejoice in their tardy repentance when they changed their minds and
tried to go in and take the land. Instead, God poured out His wrath upon them (e.g., Eze 20:21). The Lord did not
rejoice in Saul’s tardy repentance either. Rather, the Lord eventually proceeded to take the kingdom away from
him. God did not rejoice in David’s tardy repentance so as to spare the rod. To the contrary, the Lord subsequently
took the child away from him.
        Nevertheless, notwithstanding my objections, and even though the bulleted passages I listed above are not
addressing postmortem repentance, I believe that, given the general tenor of such passages and other passages that
could be added, Dillow is correct to suggest that our postmortem repentance will have an impact upon God’s fatherly
heart. I do not believe that God’s children will be the object of His eternal wrath. Albeit, the objective results of His
wrath will be eternal and our subjective regret will be eternal. When in His anger, and as an expression of His
misthological wrath, God decrees that certain believers will not inherit the kingdom, we have no right to think that
eventually they will inherit the kingdom. Dillow and I agree on this matter. They never will inherit the kingdom.
        Therefore, given our joint agreement that rewards, even negative ones, are eternal, I am somewhat at a loss
as to understand how Dillow can appeal to the Talmudic ban formula in entertaining the possibility that
misthological separation from the Lord is not permanent. According to Dillow, if the Lord’s misthological rebuke
in which He tells unfaithful believers to depart from Him refers to this ban, “then this does not refer to those who
experience a permanent separation, but only to a temporary separation....Elsewhere, this experience of exclusion
is called ‘outer darkness.’ Their separation will not be permanent, but they will forfeit their reward” (Destiny, 325;
emphasis his, cp. 331). To his credit, Dillow does acknowledge that any parallels to this ban “are uncertain,
because they are based mainly on references from the Talmuds in AD 400 to 600. It is debatable how much
of this material reflects first-century Palestinian Judaism” (p. 325). Still, he would have done better, in
deference to our agreed premise that rewards are eternal, to stress concerning these “questionable” Talmudic
writings, as he does a little later regarding another matter, that “these late references prove nothing....One can
prove almost anything he wants by citing Talmudic sources dated 600 years after the time of Christ” (pp.
347, n. 1195). So how can he appeal to what he acknowledges are questionable sources that prove nothing in order
to support his belief that the exclusion is temporary? Why do sources that prove nothing, when providing counter
evidence to his thesis, become creditable sources when providing questionable support for his thesis?
        If any parallel to this ban is applicable, then we should conclude that the temporary ban is being used by
Jesus to picture a lasting result. For example, even in the parable in which the unfaithful servant is cast into the
darkness outside the wedding festivities, I do not pose that the darkness outside must be understood parabolically
as lasting eternally in order to be understood didactically as picturing eternal exclusion. Parabolically, the darkness
only lasts a few hours. Doctrinally, the exclusion to which it alludes would be eternal, though the form/location of
that exclusion may vary. Did Jesus not make it clear in Lk 16:1-9 that He can use temporal pictures to illustrate
eternal results? Thus, in conjunction with my arguments in Darkness, wherein I demonstrated that rewards are
eternal, my conclusion is consistent: once cast out always an outcast.
        On the other hand, although the results of God’s wrath (in terms of misthological exclusion) will be eternal,

15
