one need not suppose that the Lord will be eternally angry with those whom He has placed in that exclusion. With
Dillow, I would reject picturing God as once angry, always angry. Perhaps the Lord will be disappointed with such
disobedient children forever, perhaps not. Regardless, He will not be angry with them forever. Notwithstanding the
cessation of His anger and the possible termination of His disappointment, I doubt that the unfaithful believers
themselves will every cease completely being disappointed with themselves. They will have to learn to live with
their regrets. Perhaps they will be able to take classes in Heaven to teach them how to live with regret. We have
such learning opportunities now. Lots of information on the internet addresses regret and learning how to live with
regrets. Conversely, lists on the internet suggest ways to live without regret. I will narrow my list as to how to live
without regret in eternity to just two items: Trust and obey the Lord presently. Conversely, I have reason to believe
that those who do not abide by this two-item list will have regrets eternally. Like so many now, they will have to
learn how to live with regret.
       Contemplating the biblical data from a fatherly perspective, accordingly, does encourage one to view God’s
anger as temporary, but it does not necessitate that one think that the regret is temporary. A disinherited child’s
regret can last a lot longer than a father’s anger. Some children, even today, can disobey their parents to such an
extent so as to bring a lifetime of regret upon themselves. Regret that lasts a lifetime can be a very long time when
one has eternal life. Eternal regret would be the natural corollary of doing something you will regret forever if you
live forever. Eternal life does not nullify eternal regret; it simply makes it eternal.
                                         Illustration 35. Duration of Regret




      In appreciation of Dillow’s insistency that theology should be conducted scientifically,16 let us switch from
16
   See his section, “Theological Science,” Destiny, 28-31. My eternal-regret model has more explanatory power
than does his temporary-regret model in that my model has more facts divided by fewer assumptions. My model is
not subject to the logistical nightmare of trying to explain how the misthological tears of unfaithful believers are
wiped away, for example. My simple presumption is that the tears of the unfaithful are not wiped away, at least not
by God, only by themselves. The promises regarding God wiping away the tears are for faithful believers.
Suppositions to the otherwise are fraught with difficulty. For instance, Dillow’s position seemly could be
represented syllogistically as:
     1. The loss of rewards is portrayed by the outer darkness.
     2. The outer darkness is temporary.
     3. Therefore, the loss of rewards portrayed by the outer darkness is temporary.
But how does one reconcile Dillow’s acknowledgement that rewards are eternal with his assessment that the outer
darkness (and the loss of rewards portrayed by that imagery) is temporary? His attempt to make the outer darkness
temporary is very divisive, in terms of explanatory power, dividing his thesis that rewards are eternal with many
unnecessary assumptions. If he makes the loss of rewards temporary, has he not made rewards temporary? On the
other hand, if rewards are not temporary, why should the biblical imagery used to portray this imagery be understood
as picturing a temporary reality? How are we to console the faithful with the teaching that bestowment of many
positive rewards is exclusively theirs eternally, without warning the unfaithful that the loss of those same positive
rewards will never be theirs eternally? With his doctrine of a temporary regret and temporary outer darkness, Dillow
appears to take away from the faithful what he gives to the unfaithful—eternal rewards. What started off as a reward
to the faithful is downgraded, sooner or later, to a gift to the unfaithful. He seems to be robbing the faithful of some
of the encouragement to which they are entitled in order to provide the unfaithful with a comfort to which they are
not entitled. See Robbing the Faithful to Encourage the Unfaithful, 68.
Page 66                                                                                    Misthological Models Part 1
a fatherly perspective to a scientific investigation regarding the duration of regret. A scientific analysis regarding
our theological inquiry about the duration of regret is certainly in order. Dillow is correct, after all, in understanding
theology to be a science. Let DR1 be defined as the duration of regret for those cast into OD1, where the latter term
refers to those first cast into the outer darkness. In some of Dillow’s earlier models that we were discussing, he was
attempting to link the removal of regret with the wiping away of tears. This is a popular assumption, but it is fraught
with problems. In some of those early models at least, when he was trying to link the cessation of regret with the
removal of tears, OD1 would occur in his model during the seven years of the tribulation and then be followed by
the removal of tears/regret at the conclusion of the tribulation.17 As a dispensationalist, who acknowledges that the
tribulation is literally seven years, Dillow evidently grants that the numeric value of DR1 roughly equals seven. So
let DR1 be rounded to this figure (DR1 = 7). In other words, the duration of regret for those cast into the outer
darkness at the beginning of the tribulation would last for seven years.
       Dillow’s model also proposes that another group is cast into the outer darkness at the beginning of the
millennial kingdom. Let DR2 be defined as the duration of regret for those cast into OD2, where the latter term
refers to the second group cast into the outer darkness, which would be at the beginning of the millennium.
Presumably, as a dispensationalist, Dillow will grant that millennium is a literal 1000 years. Therefore, let this value
be used as the working calculation so that DR2 = 1000. The duration of regret for those cast into the outer darkness
at the beginning of the millennium would last for a maximum of one thousand years.
       If unfaithful church-age believers are cast into OD1 at the conclusion of the church age and unfaithful
tribulational believers are cast into OD2 at the conclusion of the tribulation, as Dillow’s model entertains, then
surely the reasonable extrapolation is that his model would infer that unfaithful millennial believers are cast into
OD3 at the conclusion of the millennial kingdom. But since the eternal state commences with the wiping away of
tears (Rev 21:4), which was presumed (at times by Dillow) to be applicable to all believers (within some of his
earlier models), then the value for DR3 must be very short, probably measurable in terms of days, minutes, or
perhaps even seconds. The duration of regret for those cast into the outer darkness at the beginning of the eternal
state would be a maximum of a few hours.
       His temporary-regret model generates several questions, especially at those times in his earlier arguments
when he thought it could be linked with the wiping away of tears, a speculation concerning which he wavered.
Numerically, why are those who are unfaithful during the period of time when it is hardest to be faithful (namely
during the tribulation) assigned such a potentially long duration of regret (possibly a 1000 years), while those who
are unfaithful during the period of time when it is easiest to be faithful (namely during the millennium) assigned
such a short duration of regret (probably only a few hours)? Moreover, if those who perish in the rebellion at the
conclusion of the millennium include a number of believers in flesh and blood bodies (which is a reasonable
inductive inference), then why do these rebels only suffer a few hours of regret?
       In the early phases of his model, when Dillow limited the outer darkness to be a temporary experience in
Heaven during the tribulation, Dillow responded to my regret model by stating that he believed “mourning and
weeping are incompatible with the millennium or with the eternal state.”18 Yet, now, with his revised model, Dillow
finds mourning and weeping compatible with the millennium (see DR2).19 Given that he has found such expressions
of regret compatible with the millennial state, what is to prevent one from finding them compatible with the eternal
state? One cannot respond that such regret is incompatible with the eternal state simply because it represents a
sinless Heaven. For Dillow already has found such regret compatible with a sinless Heaven in DR1. The only
mechanism that I can see as being readily available within his model, by which to pose that such regret will be
removed, is the reference to the wiping away of tears and removal of all death, mourning, crying, and pain in
Rev 21:4, which is at the beginning of the eternal state.20 By projecting a universal applicability of this verse to all

the redeemed within the eternal state, his regret model could postulate the removal of all regret at that time (which

       In evaluating these competing models, one will have to consider whether Rev 21:4 is intended to be applied

to be the case, then it does not apply to all church-age believers, with the result that Dillow’s proposal of the
universal removal of regret for those who do poorly at the Bema must await the assumed universal wiping in

though trials and not wasted lives” (personal correspondence, 12-16-2012). Splendid!
        Dillow’s astute assessment would lead, however, in my opinion, to his having to acknowledge that the wiping
away of tears is misthological, which is the very argument I put forth in Darkness! Perhaps this is why that, despite
his telling me in 2012 that he had backed off using Rev 7:17 as a relevant indicator as to when remorse would cease
for wasted life, he unfortunately still retains this very argument in the 20th edition of his book, saying, “Although I
know of no Scripture that explicitly teaches this, I suggest that when the carnal believer is confronted with
the wasted life he has lived, he will confess, repent, and be restored to fellowship with Christ just prior to the
second coming (Revelation 7:17)” (Destiny, 903). This statement conflicts with what he told me in personal
correspondence in 2012 in that he is still appealing to Rev 7:17 when he has acknowledged in our correspondence
that he doubts its applicability—and for good reason, he refutes its applicability in his own book! Earlier in the
book, he imagines that restoration to fellowship occurs “at some time after the beginning of the millennium (p. 762),
but here (p. 903) he places it before the beginning of the millennium. Furthermore, it suffers various logistical and
inductive considerations. This quote from p. 903 is from a section of his book called, “Protestant Purgatory?” Dillow
is open to the possibility of a Protestant purgatory. In contrast, I staunchly oppose this position in Darkness. In this
section, he appeals primarily to extraneous Jewish Pseudepigraphal literature in support of the notion that Gehenna
consists of “temporary punishment” and “temporary rebuke by the Lord at the Judgment Seat of Christ” (Destiny,
902, n. 3008). Somehow, it escapes his attention that the very verse he quotes, in this very same footnote no less—
Dan 12:2—is opposed to his position. Dillow appropriately quotes this verse as saying that they are submitted to
“everlasting contempt.” Everlasting is not temporary! In Darkness (533-535, 812-813), I demonstrate that this
verse is best understood as supporting my thesis that the outer darkness is eternal. Also, see my book, Rewards are
Eternal. The shame and disgrace that unfaithful believers experience at this Jewish Bema is everlasting, not
temporary, and, therefore, not purgatorial. If Dillow, contrastively, perceives eternity to be temporary, should he
not at least provide some explanation for a temporary eternity? Semantically, should not everlasting contempt at
least last longer than seven years? Logistically, what does Dan 12:2 have to do with church-age believers? Is it not
talking about the Jewish Bema (i.e., the Jacobean Bema) at the end of the tribulation and therefore referring to an
event that will occur at the beginning of the millennium. If that is the case, does their everlasting shame only last
for a few hours?

                                                 Illustration 36. No Death




       Certainly, no physical death will be present in the eternal state for anyone in the kingdom in this sense: They
will not die. What about misthological death? The loss of one’s soul-life is eternal, even in both our models. The
loss of the crown of life is likewise an eternal loss in both our models. If death is the loss of life, would not the loss
of one’s soul-life and the loss of the crown of life be forms of death, misthologically speaking? Of course, they
would. They would be a form of misthological death. Yet to claim that there is no death at all would mean that there
is not only any literal death but also no misthological death for those who qualify for the litotetic range of the
spectrum. But I thoroughly refuted this notion in Rewards are Eternal. Misthological death will certainly exist in
the eternal state. Apparently, then, a range of values is implied between the literal and misthological poles
concerning death. Literally, no physical death will exist for people living in the eternal kingdom. Namely, people
will not die in the eternal state. Litotetically, however, misthological death will exist for unfaithful believers within
the eternal kingdom.
                                                 Illustration 37. No Death




      Might one pose a similar range of values for the other items listed in that verse? For example, could one not
pose that there will be no literal tears in the eternal state but that the litotetic range is limited to faithful believers
and means that their cup will be full of joy unspeakable? If so, then could not a medium exist between the two poles
where literal tears do not exist but regret, even profound regret, is still found to exist. Indeed, Crawford entertains
such a mediating position. Therefore, even if Dillow’s model is allowed to apply Rev 21:4 to all believers, one has
to question if (1) it applies litotetically to all believers or (2) only literally to all believers and litotetically to faithful
believers. If the latter is true, then Crawford’s projection has merit: profound regret falls short of eternal literal
expression in tears. Consequently, these three competing regret models are submitted for peer review.
                                  Robbing the Faithful to Encourage the Unfaithful
      As I was writing this section, I responded to an email I had just received from a pastor, who related:

                I taught the outer darkness where [there would be] weeping and gnashing of teeth [of] the
         unfaithful believers—after the service today there was a huge protest—most of them were clear about
         the doctrine. I guess they were upset knowing that most of them are unfaithful and will be in the outer
         darkness. This fact made them mad. What can I do? I told them the punitive regret of loss will be
         millennial and eternal. They were so mad.22

    Prudence would suggest that I interrupt this technical discussion to address a pastoral concern. I responded that
he could either tickle their ears by consoling his congregation with the widespread belief that their loss, in certain
aspects, would be temporary (so Dillow). According to this Dillowian perspective, their pastor could tickle their
ears however he desires; he will not regret it in eternity. Or he could man up and preach a sermon, perhaps entitled,
“Robbing Peter to Pay Paul,” in which he urges them to face up to the fact that the only way to make the loss of
rewards to the unfaithful temporary is to take those rewards away as eternal rewards to the faithful. Yes, even the
unfaithful will receive the rewards to which they are entitled (see “Theory of Misthological Relativity” Darkness,
687ff.)—let them be consoled to the extent they are entitled with this doctrine, among others)—but this does not
mean that we should take way the rewards to which the faithful are entitled and give them to the unfaithful.
    Dillow and I agree that the faithful are promised that they will have no regrets for living for Christ. Jesus

22
     Personal correspondence, 1-26-2013.
Chapter 2. Models for Regret                                                                                     Page 69
promises to comfort them: “Blessed are those who mourn, for they shall be comforted” (Mt 5:4; Is 49: 13; 61:2-3;
Jer 31:13). The Lord promises this comfort to the faithful: they and they alone will be comforted; He does not
promise comfort to those who cause them to mourn. The Lord promises, “He who overcomes shall inherit these
things” (Rev 21:7). What things? Having one’s tears wiped away (Rev 21:4). Such comfort is promised to them and
limited to them. To suggest that the Lord will extend this comfort to those who do not mourn is to give those who
do mourn further reason to mourn because it adds insult to injury. For instance, when the Bible comforts those
believers who overcome by promising them that the Lord will give them the right to eat of the tree of life (Rev 2:7),
we realize that this means that, conversely, some believers will lose their right to the tree of life (Rev 22:19).
Misthologists should know better than to think that the Lord eventually will give this right, this comfort, to those
who do not deserve it. To tell those who overcome that their right to eat of the tree of life will, sooner or later, be
extended to those who take away from God’s word does not encourage the faithful to be true to God’s word. It
discourages them in their faithfulness. The same logic applies to the mistholic comfort of having one’s tears wiped
away. To encourage the unfaithful in this manner would be to discourage the faithful. Do not rob the faithful of
their comfort by taking that comfort way from them as a reward in order to give it as a gift to everyone. Why should
the faithful be the ones who pay for the comfort of the unfaithful?
     Dillow believes that the unfaithful will, relatively quickly, also have no regrets for not living for Christ. In this
Dillowian proposal, the promised comfort of no regrets is taken away (as a reward) from the faithful, after a few
years, hours, or minutes, as the case may be, and given as a gift to everyone, including the unfaithful. Having no
regrets ceases to be a reward for the faithful when God allegedly wipes away all regrets for everyone. What was
once a special and limited reward becomes a common and unlimited gift. I find such misthology to be profane: It
takes what is consecrated for the pure in heart and gives it to the impure in heart. Should a pastor discourage the
faithful in his church by encouraging the unfaithful that, in terms of eternal regret, it does not matter if they do not
live for the Lord? Does he have the right to assure the unfaithful that they will not regret failing to live for the Lord?
Would the pastor not be robbing the faithful of the comfort which they are promised in order to give it to the
unfaithful?
     Comparatively speaking, I would suggest that this pastor have his congregation imagine with him what would
happen if he were to preach a sermon entitled, “10 Minutes of Regret,” claiming, “Men, it does not matter if you
are unfaithful to your wives; you will only regret it for 10 minutes” (24 hrs x 7 yrs / 1000 yrs ≈ 10 mins). If a
thousand years is but a day, when viewed from a heavenly perspective, then what is seven years of disappointment
compared to a day, when viewed from an earthly perspective? Would their wives appreciate such a sermon? Would
men who are striving to be faithful to their wives be encouraged by it? Or how about the making this ear-tickling
statement to the children, “Children, it does not matter if you obey your parents; you will only regret disobeying
them for 10 minutes. Yes, rest assured, no matter how long or how badly you disobey your parents, when all is said
and done, you will only regret it for 10 minutes.” How well would that sermon go over with the parents in the
congregation? How well do they think the equivalent sermon will go over with our Heavenly Parent for pastors who
give the equivalent exhortations, saying that there will be no regrets on the part of any believers after the seven
years of tribulation? If a day in Heaven is as 1000 years compared to our time on Earth, then the 7 years of earthly
regret that will be experienced after the Bema by the unfaithful who are then in Heaven will only seem like 10
minutes. In the Dillowian model, a life time of unfaithfulness will only be met with a relative 10 minutes of regret.
       Why not preach a follow-up sermon, “Heavenly Cheat Days”? While controversial, some dieters advocate
taking cheat days once a week to encourage them in sticking to their diet. According to this game plan, you will
only regret cheating temporarily. Are husbands to be encouraged to limit themselves to cheat days sexually as a
means to being faithful to their marriages with the assurances that they will only regret their cheat days temporarily?
No! The doctrine of rewards does not work like dietary cheat days! If you cheat misthologically, you run the risk
of regretting the results eternally. I closed my email to that pastor by saying, “Be encouraged. If you are faithful,
you will not regret it. If they are unfaithful, they will regret it.” And I would add, “Eternally.”
       This pastor who emailed me enjoys using the illustrations in my books for his congregation, so I created the
following one for him to share. Hopefully, some within his congregation might be more receptive if they could be
given material suitable for their stage of development.
Page 70                                                                                     Misthological Models Part 1
                                         Illustration 38. Balancing the Scales




                    1.   No discipline; only reward.
                    2.   Bema discipline; millennial reward.
                    3.   Tribulational punishment/hurt; eternal reward.
                    4.   Millennial punishment/hurt; millennial reward.
                    5.   Millennial punishment/hurt; eternal reward.
                    6.   Eternal punishment/hurt; eternal reward.

        At level one, the face showing no hurt would be Sarah’s offender described subsequently in the discussion
to follow. Her offender has gotten off scot-free at the Bema. Sarah is depicted as experiencing some hurt. Her face
is in the foreground for each level. So, at level number two, Sarah has little hurt, and her offender has little hurt. At
level number three, Sarah has little hurt; her offender has some hurt. Level number four depicts those ultraistic
Gehenna misthologists who believe that unfaithful believers literally will burn in Gehenna for 1000 years. They
tend to limit the positive affirmation of rewards to the same time period as well. Some of my fellow misthologists
who hold this perspective would acknowledge that my view is harsher than theirs; although, I do not believe that
believers literally will burn in Gehenna. At this level, Sarah would have no hurt; her offender would have more
hurt. The level of hurt for her offender increases in levels five and six. In the illustration, I am simplistically equating
punishment with hurt. If it does not hurt, then it is not punishment. Punishment should not be considered pleasurable.
Therefore, from this simplistic perspective, although the loss of inheritance is eternal, even at level 3, if the loss of
inheritance does not hurt (i.e., there is no regret), then it is not considered punishment. And this punishment will
not be found pleasurable by those submitted to it. Mistholic pain is not masochistic.
        To recap the differences between me and Dillow in terms of the illustration, the perception that the Bema
involves no discipline, only reward, is the first level pictured. I find level one to be totally nauseating, and I
thoroughly refute it in Darkness. I deeply appreciate Dillow soundly refuting a level-one understanding in Destiny
as well. I also interact in Darkness with the other levels pictured on the scale. Level number three, for the most part,
seems to represent Dillow’s model: tribulational punishment for the unfaithful; eternal reward for the faithful.
Personally, I advocate the model pictured by level number six: eternal punishment for the unfaithful; eternal reward
for the faithful. Since I hold the severest level on the scale, my model would be the most shocking for those only
accustomed to misthological milk. Therefore, I would advise pastors contemplating teaching my model to their
congregations to consider providing their congregants with some misthological milk first, depending on the maturity
of their congregations, so that their congregants do not choke to death on misthological meat.
        As I reminded this pastor in our email exchange, a congregation should be educated as to the different models
regarding the doctrine of rewards so that the immature, who are only accustomed to milk, do not choke to death on
a more mature understanding of misthological themes. His congregation was struggling with basic issues, such as
acknowledging punitive misthological suffering and kingdom ranking in Heaven. I suggested that he get Dillow’s
Destiny as a supplement to my own Darkness.
Chapter 2. Models for Regret                                                                                   Page 71
                                              Misthological Comfort
      Although in the present book I am interacting with some of the areas where Dillow and I disagree in our
models, I am fully appreciative of the numerous areas where we complement one another. For instance, we are
agreed that the Bema involves punishment for the unfaithful and corresponding resulting kingdom ranking. Dillow
shares a splendid illustration loaded with pastoral application:

             When I explained that in my opinion the phrase “depart from me” was addressed to true
      believers who practiced lawlessness and who will face rebuke and shame at the Judgment Seat of
      Christ, a middle-aged woman named Sarah began to tear up. She said, “You have no idea how much
      this means to me. When I was a child I was severely sexually abused by my ‘Christian’ father. I have
      struggled with anger and bitterness for years and have worked hard to deal with it. However, all I ever
      heard from other Christians and from the church was that it was my responsibility to forgive. ‘What
      about him,’ I always thought? Does he just get away with it and enter into the kingdom with less
      reward than others? That does not seem just. To know that he will face this terrible rebuke somehow
      makes it easier for me to forgive.” (Destiny, 321-322.)

       Dillow’s account of Sarah was a large part of my inspiration for the seesaw in the illustration. Learning that
the offender would hurt then, at the Bema, enabled Sarah to hurt less now, in the present life. This consolation is
biblical (Rom 12:19-20)! Dillow does a great job in this area, insofar as he goes, in noting that those like Sarah can
take comfort in knowing that those who cause them to suffer will themselves suffer. An inverse relationship exists
between the amount of discomfort her offender will receive in the future and the amount of comfort she is receiving
now in the present. To advance this discussion even further, several questions may be considered.
       If knowing that this offender will be punished and hurt temporarily at the Bema provides this measure of
comfort for Sarah, how much more comfort would be provided to learn that the offender would be punished and
hurt eternally after the Bema? Moreover, might not this same type of comforting consolation that Sarah is receiving
presently from such knowledge be part of the comfort and consolation that she receives at the Bema? Could not this
offender who abused her be caused to come and bow down before her at the Bema (cp. Rev 3:9), before he
subsequently is cast into the outer darkness for all eternity? Could not his tears be part of the wiping away of her
tears? Could the Lord not use his tears to wipe away her tears?
       A reasonable proposition is that for the faithful to have no hurt, the unrepentant who hurt them must have
profound hurt at the Bema. A slap on the wrist will not do! Tardy repentance will not suffice! Some holding to a
wimpy one-dimensional view of forgiveness and rewards will recoil at this comforting aspect of mature misthology.
Nevertheless, it is thoroughly biblical. Part of the anticipated comfort of the faint in heart who mourn is that the
Lord will deal harshly with the wicked: “They have heard that I groan; there is no one to comfort me; all my
enemies have heard of my calamity; they are glad that Thou hast done it. Oh, that Thou wouldst bring the day which
Thou hast proclaimed, that they may become like me. Let all their wickedness come before Thee; and deal with
them as Thou hast dealt with me for all my transgressions; for my groans are many, and my heart is faint”
(Lam 1:21-22). The comfort the biblical writer desires is that God will deal harshly with his oppressors. The
anticipation of comfort anticipates that those who hate God’s servants will be ashamed: “Show me a sign for good,
that those who hate me may see it, and be ashamed, because Thou, O Lord, hast helped me and comforted me”
(Ps 86:17). The comfort he desires is for those who hate him to be ashamed. When Jesus promises the rich now that
they are receiving their comfort in full (Lk 6:24), what right does Dillow have to say that Jesus is wrong, that they
are not receiving their comfort now in full but will receive further comfort after the Bema? Is not the comfort of the
poor naturally juxtaposed with the agony of the rich (Lk 16:25)? Does not the vindication of God’s people assume
that He will repay vengeance on those who afflict them (Dt 32:36-36; Ps 94:1-23)? The vindication of the afflicted
assumes the crushing of their afflicters: “May he vindicate the afflicted of the people, save the children of the needy,
and crush the oppressor” (Ps 72:4). Does not vindication anticipate the condemnation of those who condemn
wrongly (Is 50:8)? Does not vindication from the Lord promise the condemnation of those who accuse His faithful?
Indeed, part of the reward of the faithful is to be able to condemn those who condemned them: “And every tongue
that accuses you in judgment you will condemn. This is the heritage of the servants of the Lord, and their vindication
is from Me,’ declares the Lord” (Is 54:17)! Even more astonishing, “They will take their captors captive, and will
rule over their oppressors” (Is 14:2). For vindication to sit at one end of the seesaw, commendation must sit at the
other. Does not the comfort of those who mourn involve vengeance on the part of the Lord (Is 61:2-3)? Are mourners
not comforted by His vengeance? When Jesus promises that those who mourn will be comforted (Mt 5:4), does He
Page 72                                                                                   Misthological Models Part 1
not inversely, in a seesaw manner, promise that those who laugh now will mourn then (Lk 6:25)? If this
eschatological mourning is a negative reward and if rewards are eternal, by what right do we assume that their
mourning is not eternal?
      During the course of my finishing this book, a pastor friend informed me via email that he had just been
forced to resign from a mission board for which he had been the chairman for the past eight years. He had written
an excellent book on rewards and had been viciously and slanderously dismissed from the board as a result. I
responded along the following lines:

                As I was reading your email, a verse kept coming to mind: “Behold, I will cause those of the
         synagogue of Satan, who say that they are Jews, and are not, but lie—behold, I will make them to
         come and bow down at your feet, and to know that I have loved you” (Rev 3:9). We are to be motivated
         and comforted by the promise of rewards and even desire those rewards. But this verse causes me
         some trouble. In the case of those falsely claiming to be doing God’s will when they do us harm, would
         this verse not mean that we should look forward to their bowing before us at the Bema? It certainly
         seems so. But if so, how do we reconcile that with the injunction to love our enemies and pray for
         them?....
                As it turns out, Scott [Crawford] was covering this very verse in Sunday School today. Two
         supplemental passages he mentioned were: (1) “Alexander the coppersmith did me much harm. May
         the Lord repay him according to his works” (2Tim 4:14; MT). And (2) “At my first defense no one
         supported me, but all deserted me; may it not be counted against them” (2Tim 4:16). Paul is expressing
         two desires, two prayers.
                For those who stood against him, Paul’s prayer/desire is that the Lord will repay them according
         to their works for vigorously opposing his teaching (2Tim 2:15). Contrastively, for those who should
         have spoken up for him but didn’t, Paul’s prayer/desire is that the Lord will not repay them according
         to their works. Paul wants the Lord to show mercy to the latter but not to the former. BTW, the
         contextual focus is on eschatological payment (2Tim 4:8), not temporal repayment (contra GNTC).
                If I combine Rev 3:9 with 2Tim 4:14, I derive this result: Paul was looking forward to the day
         when those who opposed his teaching would be repaid according to their deeds, the day when they
         would have to acknowledge that he was right and they were wrong, the day when they would have to
         bow at his feet.
                From what you have shared, it seems that you had both groups at your meeting. To me it would
         seem biblically permissible to follow Paul’s example and have mixed feelings. For those who stood
         against you, I look forward to the day when they have to bow down before you and acknowledge that
         you were right at the Bema. For those who should have stood with you but failed to do so, I pray that
         the Lord will show them mercy and grant that they grow a backbone before the Bema.
                The contextual objection might be raised, “But all those who have loved Christ’s appearing will
         receive the crown of righteousness regardless of what they did in that meeting. Right? So even those
         who opposed you will receive the crown of righteousness if they have loved Christ’s appearing”
         (2Tim 4:8). Wrong!
                During the Sunday School class, Scott expressed the opinion that Alexander the coppersmith
         was a believer. Maybe. If that was the case, and if Alexander was looking forward to Christ’s
         appearing, does this nullify our expectation that Alexander will receive a negative outcome at the
         Bema? No! I know many preachers who say that they are looking forward to Christ’s appearing and
         yet are teaching a false gospel. Paul says, “Let them be accursed.” Surely, Paul would object to the
         notion that such preachers will receive the crown of righteousness. So how do we reconcile Paul’s
         promise that all those who have loved Christ’s appearing will receive the crown of righteousness?
                I would pose that the perfect tense entails a future contingency in this context. They may love
         the idea of Christ’s appearing now, but they will not then, when they shrink away from Him in shame
         at the Bema (1Jn 2:18). Paul intends for the perfect tense to denote a continuous state that is inclusive
         of the Bema. Those who are able to maintain their love of His appearing at the Bema will receive the
         crown of righteous. Those who are not able to maintain their love of His appearing at the Bema will
         not receive the crown of righteousness.23
23
     Personal correspondence, 1-29-2017.

This pastor responded and expressed his agreement:

                For those who actively criticize and oppose the word of the kingdom, we should pray and expect
         that God will reward them according to their works, for that is in accordance with His will. On the
         other hand, those who are not opposing but refuse to speak out (either out of ignorance or fear or both),
         we should pray and expect that their eyes will be opened to truth and/or that they will find courage in
         the Lord to profess Him before men. In both cases, love is shown, for in the former case, love rejoices
         not in iniquity but rejoices in the truth and in the latter, love bears all things and believes all things.
                I also agree about the awarding of crowns. Those who love Christ’s appearing will receive the
         crown of righteousness, all things being equal. That is, if a man claims to love Christ’s appearing but
         doesn’t walk in obedience and truth and righteousness, he will not receive the crown. I believe that is
         correct, and I really like your further explanation that they must love His appearing WHEN HE
         APPEARS. That is the true test! And that is consistent with 1 John 2:28 (not ashamed at His coming)
         and 4:17 (boldness or confidence in the day of judgment). There will indeed be shock and surprise for
         some at the Bema, who will discover they really didn’t love His appearing after all.24 (Emphasis his.)

I responded:

                 Given our concurrence on that side of the equation, my thoughts now run to the other side. We
         are to love our enemies and pray for those who persecute us, which helps us in avoiding bitterness. So
         did Paul not pray for Alexander’s repentance? Surely he did—at first. I suspect, however, that there
         are ways to balance both aspects of his prayers. We may, for example, pray that God will reward our
         enemies according to their works even temporally so that they will repent and thus be shown mercy at
         the Bema. I wonder how Paul pulled off praying for those who perverted the gospel. On the one hand,
         he prays for God’s curse upon them. On the other hand, I suspect that he prayed for their
         repentance. Sometimes, after initial attempts to help his enemies see the light, Paul just threw up his
         hands and said, “Your blood be on your own heads.” Sometimes even Jesus said to just shake off the
         dust and move on, invoking God’s judgment according to their works, like Paul did with Alexander.
         God has limits. People can reach the point to where they cross a line and God says enough. It may be
         that rather than following a formula we have to follow the Spirit in determining which type of prayer
         is most suitable for which type of enemy at which stage of resistance. Even Paul started off as a
         persecutor, repented, and became the persecuted, perhaps in part because Stephen prayed, “Father,
         forgive them.” Our discernment of who has crossed the line and who has not cannot be set in stone, I
         suppose. We pray with prudence. Sometimes prudence would seem to dictate that the line has been
         pragmatically crossed. Paul was at the end of his life. Opportunity for reconciliation with Alexander
         was no longer feasible. He was at a point where praying for reconciliation was no longer an option. So
         he moved on to the next logical recourse, praying that the Lord would repay Alexander according to
         his deeds. After all, one way to avoid bitterness is to forgive in terms of seeking our own revenge and
         leaving vengeance in the Lord’s hand. Paul advised this recourse as well. Apparently, Paul was using
         this technique at this stage. It would seem to me that those who have resisted you have probably crossed
         a line from which they will not recant or seek reconciliation with you. If so, then, biblically, concluding
         and praying that God will reward them according to their deeds seems to be the appropriate conclusion.
         At one level we forgive them in terms of holding any bitterness toward them or seeking our own
         revenge, at another level we pray that God will reward them according to their deeds.25

                                                  Principle of Inversion
       As Dillow relates, in the 2013 edition of Destiny, in providing the biblical background justifying Sarah’s
deriving consolation from this biblical doctrine, even while dying on the cross, Christ kept trusting the fact that His
Father, as the one who judges righteously, would one day “deal with His abusers and He trusted that fact” (p. 326).
Unfortunately, Dillow removed this statement from his later edition. Nevertheless, the truth of the statement still
remains. In sharing this perspective, Peter tells us “to follow” Christ example (1Pet 2:21-23). At least Dillow kept
this part of the previous quote in his later edition (p. 322). Paul appears to be doing that very thing when he instructs
believers: “Never take your own revenge, beloved, but leave room for the wrath of God, for it is written, “Vengeance
is Mine, I will repay” (Rom 12:19). Or this verse could be translated as, “Punishment is Mine, I will repay.” The
writer of Hebrews immediately follows this affirmation by saying, “The Lord will judge His people” (Heb 10:30).
Sarah can trust God to punish her abuser, even if he is one of God’s people. Contrary to those naysayers who say
that trusting God to avenge you is an unchristian attitude, Peter indicates that refusal to trust God to punish your
abuser is a refusal to follow Christ’s example. Properly understood and applied, this teaching is a means of freeing
one from a spirit of bitterness, not a pretext for encouraging it. When it comes to revenge, let go and let God. You
can trust Him to avenge you justly. Moreover, my argument is that He will do so eternally.
       Concerning God’s OT people, God promises to bless those who bless them and curse those who curse them
(Gen 12:3). Can they not trust Him to do so? This is God’s foreign nation policy regarding the nation of Israel.
God’s people can trust Him to punish those who do them harm. This policy applies just as strongly to God’s NT
people—His “holy nation” (1Pet 2:9).28 As such, Sarah will be blessed; her abuser will be punished. An inverse
relationship occurs between the two at the Bema. Her abuser will shed tears while Sarah’s tears are dried. Indeed,
seeing him shed tears as he bows at her feet may be one of the means that the Lord uses to dry away her tears. Her
abuser will bow in subjugation; she will sit in exaltation. The Lord will make him come and bow down at her feet
and know that He has loved her, not him, in the misthological manner being depicted by the Lord. The Lord makes
this promise to overcomers: “Behold, I will cause those of the synagogue of Satan, who say that they are Jews, and
are not, but lie—behold, I will make them to come and bow down at your feet, and to know that I have loved you”
(Rev 3:9). At the Bema those who abused the overcomers will have to bow before those overcomers whom they
abused. She is an overcomer; he is not. He will bow to her, not her to him. He will “grovel” at her feet (WBC).

26
   Ibid.
27
   Ibid.
28
   These OT promises to Israel are applied to the NT church, but this does not mean that the church replaces Israel.
Rather, the rich imagery allows us to flesh out the details of a biblical principle as it applies to God’s people, whether
they are OT or NT overcomers. Those among God’s people who are faithful can expect that those who afflicted
them, either corporately or personally, whether lost or saved, will have to come and bow down before them. The
lost will do so before being cast into the Lake of Fire, never to be seen again. The saved will do so before being cast
into the outer darkness, where they will only be seen occasionally. The bowing on the part of the latter will not be
a onetime event but may be expected to occur repeatedly throughout the millennium and eternity.
Chapter 2. Models for Regret                                                                                    Page 75
       This NT principle is embedded in the OT. After Joseph had been imprisoned wrongly by the Egyptians, God
raised him up so that “they proclaimed before him, ‘Bow the knee!’ And he set him over all the land of Egypt”
(Gen 41:43). Whenever and wherever Joseph rode his chariot throughout Egypt, the Egyptians corporately would
have to bow their knee to him. At a far more personal level, his brothers who abused him had to come and bow at
his feet personally (Gen 42:6). Typologically, I would suspect that wherever and whenever Sarah travels throughout
the kingdom, the general populace will bow their knees to her. And on those rare occasions in eternity future when
she travels to the remotest parts of the kingdom known as the outer darkness and passes by her abuser, he especially
will have to bow the knee!
       As a misthopoligist, I fully expect that those who reside in Zion will be bowed to by those who live outside
of Zion.29 Without a doubt, those who live outside of Zion who treated overcomers despitefully in their early days
on Earth will do so: “And the sons of those who afflicted you will come bowing to you, and all those who
despised you will bow themselves at the soles of your feet; and they will call you the city of the Lord, the Zion
of the Holy One of Israel” (Is 60:14). The afflicters bow to those whom they afflicted. This reward is but one of the
many eternal rewards awaiting those who live in the Reward City. As Rev 3:9 demonstrates, the applicability of
this principle is not limited to God’s OT faithful but extends to NT overcomers. Consequently, we have rich OT
imagery by which to stimulate a biblically informed perspective concerning eternity: “They will take their captors
captive, and will rule over their oppressors” (Is 14:2).” “Men of stature,” those who naturally are majestic in stature,
suggestive of those who are first in stature on Earth, “will come over to you and will be yours; they will walk behind
you, they will come over in chains and will bow down to you; they will make supplication to you” (Is 45:14; cp.
Ps 149:1-9). Whatever partial fulfillment this passage may have had in the past is anticipated, by Isaiah, to extend
eschatologically into the future. Therefore, the futuristic application of the imagery indicates that many of those
who are first on Earth will be last in the millennial kingdom.30 Since rewards, such as kingdom ranking and status
are eternal, the reasonable supposition is that such millennial differentiation extends into eternity. In fact, not
stopping at a millennial application, Isaiah immediately goes on to project an eternal applicability: “Israel has been
saved by the Lord with an everlasting salvation; you will not be put to shame or humiliated to all eternity”
(Is 45:17).31
       The typical cup misimpression, which assumes that those so subjugated will have a cup full of joy, should be
obliterated by the biblical impression that those being bound in chains, bowing to the ground, and licking the dust
will not necessarily enjoy the subjugation: “They will bow down to you with their faces to the earth, and lick the
dust of your feet; and you will know that I am the Lord; those who hopefully wait for Me will not be put to shame”
(Is 49:23). Let believers like Sarah be consoled with words like these. Dillow imagines that the confinement in the
outer darkness is done away with at the beginning of the millennial kingdom. If that is the case, why does the
biblical subjugation described above appear during the millennial state? Jesus’ parabolic description of the outer
darkness seems appropriate: “Bind him hand and foot, and cast him into the outer darkness; in that place there shall
be weeping and gnashing of teeth” (Mt 22:13). This parabolic picture could be grounded in the OT imagery and
anticipate a literal binding in chains. Literal tears are clearly associated with the event. Millennial chains suggest
the presence of millennial tears. Since rewards are eternal, the most natural conclusion is that the chains and tears
are eternal as well. Postulating that the chains and tears are eternal does not necessitate thinking that they wear these
chains all day or every day or that they weep every minute of every day.
       On this very page, where Dillow shares this example of Sarah, he relates the example of a Christian minister
who “had sexually abused his daughters and some others.” Yet this minister’s “last words...as he lay dying in the
hospital,” were astoundingly, “I have no regrets” (Destiny, 321). This man’s lifetime of no regrets for having abused
others is met with only seven years of postmortem regret within Dillow’s model. Certainly, I can understand why
people like Sarah would find a degree of emotional release in being told that their abusers will be rebuked and thus
29
   Misthology is derived from the Greek words misthos (reward) and logos (teaching) and refers to the doctrine of
rewards. Misthologists refers to advocates of unconditional soteriological security who have a sufficient degree of
misthological competency to recognize that the outer darkness pertains to rewards. Misthopology refers to doctrine
of the Reward City (misthos + polis). Misthopologists are misthologists who advocate a reward view of the city.

   See Excursus Regarding Millennial Slavery, 81.
31
   If not being shamed or humiliated is an eternal reward for the faithful, then this benefit cannot be extended as a
gift to the unfaithful at some point in the future since, otherwise, at that point in time when it was made freely
available to the unfaithful, it would stop being a reward for the faithful and become a gift to all, which violates our
mutual premise that rewards are eternal.
Page 76                                                                                    Misthological Models Part 1
have some temporary regrets rather than having no regrets. Some is better than none. But would their emotional
release not be far greater, and longer, if they were informed, contrary to Dillow, that their abusers’ regret would be
eternal rather than temporary?
       The principle of inversion strongly suggests the eternality of the consequence in many biblical examples.
Take kingdom ranking for instance. Jesus said, “Many who are first will be last; and the last, first” (Mt 19:30). How
long will Sarah be first? Eternally. How long will her abuser be last? Temporarily? Surely not! If the inverse
relationship of X to Y is eternal, as certainly seems to be the case, then both X and Y apparently are eternal.
Algebraically, we might picture this graphically by plotting the (X,Y) value.
                                            Illustration 39. X-Y Relations




        In the case of this particular illustration, Sarah being first = X; his being last = Y.32 For the X-Y relation to
continue to exist, both X and Y must continue to exist. If the ranking ceases, then the X-Y relationship is only
temporary, and the reward was not eternal because X-Y relation ceases to exist since it is a value determined in part
by its relationship to Y. But this cannot be the case since rewards are eternal. Likewise, Y cannot cease to exist
either; otherwise, you lose X-Y relationship. First place implies the contrastive existence of last place (X in relation
to Y necessarily implies the existence of Y). Yet this is only one such example. Many biblical items could be
plugged into the X-Y values: crowned/uncrowned; ruler/ruled; master/slave; possessor/possessed;
exalted/humiliated; shamed/unashamed; pillar/outcast; golden/wooden; honor/dishonor, gateway/castaway;
unhurt/hurt; life/death; named/unnamed; star/night; white/black; loved/unloved; clothed/unclothed; wealthy/poor;
eat/hunger; drink/thirsty; rejoice/shame; joyful/sorrowful. For many, these roles will be reversed eternally in
eternity.
                                                Temporary Flogging
       At this juncture, I will anticipate an objection to my holding misthological regret as eternal and respond in
such a manner as to allow me to pull several strands together in a clarification. My acknowledgement that the
parabolic darkness is only parabolically temporary, only lasting the duration of that one night, in no way detracts
from eternal nature of the reward, as one need not necessarily insist that the parabolic detail itself is eternal in order
to derive an eternal result. The darkness of that night, parabolically, only lasts that one night, but multiple strands

32
  In such a graph, values are plotted by their (X,Y) relations. Since the value for one is plotted in relation to the
value for the other, you cannot plot X if you do not know the value for Y: (X = she is first, Y = he is last). Her
reward is to be first in relation to his being last. This (X,Y) relation does not change because rewards are eternal.
Chapter 2. Models for Regret                                                                                     Page 77
of evidence indicates that the exclusion which it depicts is not terminated by the morning’s light. Having
demonstrated those strands elsewhere, for the present discussion a shorter consideration of a couple of corollaries
will suffice. Parabolically, the flogging itself is only temporary; even so, no disjuncture in the eternal nature of the
rewards with which it is associated needs be supposed: “Even if the flogging is limited to the Bema, the results of
the Bema are eternal. That the consequences are eternal is a corollary of eternal rewards. Such rewards may either
be positive or negative” (Darkness, 409). Notwithstanding that rebuttal, the temporary nature of the flogging is not
equal to the temporary nature of the parabolic darkness. A table representing my position might suffice for a
summation.
                       Illustration 40. Temporary and Eternal Misthological Consequences

                       Temporary                                                      Eternal
                         Bema                                                        Post-Bema
 Judge (action)                                                   Judgment (state)
 Object of God’s emotional anger (action)                         Object of God’s volitional wrath (state)
 Flogging (action)                                                Flogged (state)
 Burning (action)                                                 Burnt (state)
 Depart from Me (action)                                          Outer Darkness (state)
 Cast into Gehenna (action)                                       Consigned to Gehenna (state)
 Shrink away in shame (action)                                    Shame (state)
 Loss of soul (action)                                            Soul lost (state)
 Tears (intense action)                                           Tears (increasingly infrequent action)
 Regret (action)                                                  Regret (state)
 Exclusion (action)                                               Excluded (state)
 Denied by Christ (action)                                        Denied by Christ (state)
       God will judge us, a punctiliar action. The punctiliar judgment pronounced will result in a linear state. The
flogging could represent a tongue lashing. Even in this life, “the mouth of the foolish is a rod for his back”
(cp. Prov 14:3). Words can hurt, but they cannot break our bones. However, the Lord’s words can break bones,
even break nations, and kill. His words, whether described as the rod or sharp sword of His mouth, have the potential
to kill (Is 11:4; Rev 19:15). Theoretically at least, a verbal flogging from one so powerful could leave physical
scars. Less speculatively, it will result in emotional scars, for eternity. Eternally, those subjected to a Bema flogging
will remain, categorically and emotionally, in the state of having been flogged. Similarly, the actual burning to
which the unfruitful believers are submitted misthologically (in Jn 15:6 and Heb 6:8 at the Bema) will only be
temporary. But the state of having been burned will be permanent. Neoclassically and correlatively, I am open to
Dillow’s misthological use of Gehenna. However, being cast into Gehenna does not indicate a temporary venture
into the garbage dump. Rather, the action of being cast into Gehenna would be punctiliar, but the resulting state of
having been cast into Gehenna would be linear. Those cast into Gehenna are consigned to it for eternity. This
statement is true both soteriologically and misthologically. Likewise, those who shrink away in shame at the Bema
(1Jn 2:28), which is a punctiliar action, will walk around (linear action) in a static state of shame thereafter (Rev
16:15). Those who lose their lives (souls) at the Bema will not lose their lives temporarily. One does not normally
lose one’s life temporarily. They die misthologically at the Bema (a punctiliar action); thereafter, they are
misthologically dead (a linear state). In summary, the flogging is not eternal, but the state of having been flogged
is eternal. If one wishes to press the parabolic detail of the outer darkness beyond its original intent so as to insist
that the casting into the darkness is punctiliar but the state of having been cast into the outer darkness is only for the
duration of the outer darkness, then it should be obvious, since rewards are eternal, that the state of having been
cast into the outer darkness does not end when the supposed parabolic morning comes. For that matter, one might
counter the supposed limitation by conjecturing that instead of the exclusion ending the next morning, those cast
into the outer darkness are sent to even remoter parts of the property. See my Gizrah model in Darkness.
       As to the objection that God will not keep the unfaithful in the outer darkness forever because He will not
stay angry with them forever, in some passages at least, such as Ps 103:9, the promise that God will not “keep His
Page 78                                                                                   Misthological Models Part 1
anger forever” (v. 9) is made to those of “his children” (Ps 103:13) who “fear Him” (Ps 103:11,13,17), that is, “to
those who keep His covenant” (Ps 103:18). Although He relinquishes His anger eventually because “He is mindful
that we are but dust” (Ps 103:14), the fact that He has not “rewarded us according to our iniquities” (Ps 103:10)
seems, itself, to be a reward in this particular context: “Not being rewarded negatively with what one deserves
because of one’s positive performance is itself a reward” (Darkness, 1077, n. 1172). Yet if God’s not being angry
with us eternally is a reward to the faithful in this context, then the question might be posed, “Am I not violating
my own canon by suggesting that God will, eventually at least, grant this reward to the unfaithful as well?”
       The clarification hinted at by the first part of the verse may be offered: “He will not always judge us” (Ps103:9,
TM). Or as some would explain it, He will not angrily criticize us beyond measure. Rather, He corrects us in
measure (Jer 30:11; 46:28). The limitation of God’s anger to our chastisement during the course of this life is a
positive reward. The extension of His anger to the Bema is a negative reward. The latter may be compared to a
whipping, a Bema flogging. Neither the Bema nor a Bema whipping, nor the anger expressed there or therein, are
intended to last forever. The result of judgment, not the act of judging, is eternal. Not having God angry with you
at the Bema is indeed a reward, limited to God’s obedient children. Correspondingly, the Lord will not rejoice in
the tardy repentance of His disobedient children so as to spare them the misthological rod. No conflict need be
posed between the temporary nature of His Bema anger and whipping versus the eternal nature of rewards: “Because
the Bema flogging relates to the dispensing of eternal negative rewards, the results of the flogging are eternal,
although the flogging itself is only temporary. Neither the flogging nor Bema is eternal, but their results certainly
are” (Darkness, 409).

                                                 Conclusion
       Sometimes it is more important to handle our differences rightly than it is to be right. Wisdom involves
knowing how to balance multiple spectrums. Being right must be balanced with being compassionate with those
who hold alternate points of view. On the one hand, we will be judged for interpreting the word rightly (2Tim 2:15).
So we do our best to interpret the Bible accurately. On the other hand, we also will be judged as to our love for one
another (1Jn 3:17-21). So we seek to be understanding with those who do not share our understanding. Passion must
be balanced with compassion. Surely, part of our maturation is learning how to deal with our differences. On matters
that are crucial, the biblical perspective is rightfully not very respectful (at times) with those holding the opposing
points of view, even calling the opposing party snakes and wishing for their castration, as evidenced by biblical
exclamations. At other times, we should not hold on to being right so much so that we unnecessarily would offend
those who hold the opposing point of view in less crucial matters. Holding the opposing point of view in contempt
would be a very grievous mistake, having serious ramifications at the judgment (Rom 14:1-23). May the Lord give
us grace as we learn to interpret His word rightly and to deal rightly with our differences as we debate the merits of
our respective models vigorously.
                                              Illustration 41. No Death
Chapter 2. Models for Regret                                                                                    Page 79

       The models surveyed herein are agreed that salvation from eternal damnation is a gift and that the outer
darkness, conversely, pertains to rewards—both aspects are the fundamental axioms for misthology. Moreover, the
models engaged herein are agreed that rewards are eternal. As misthologists who are agreed on these essential
features, being agreed on issues of a more peripheral nature is not as crucial, naturally. Even on those issues where
we may disagree on where specific details range on the spectrum of ambiguity versus perspicuity, we can hold our
differences respectfully while we vigorously defend what we believe to be the rightful interpretation.
       While assisting in the proofreading of Dillow’s ch. 43, I noted that a three-strand cord is stronger than one.
In contradistinction to Lordship Salvationists (who are locked into singular, problematic, soteriological
interpretation of the New Jerusalem), misthologists are able to offer viable interpretations of the misthological
nature of the city that harmonize with the soteriological nature of the kingdom. The three misthological proposals
entertained herein are unified in: (a) their soteriological insistence that all believers will live freely within the
millennial and eternal kingdom and (b) their misthological affirmation of rewards so that only faithful believers will
exercise rulership therein. Our misthological differences compose a singular cord with three distinct strands of
thought regarding the entrance and residency of unfaithful believers in part of the kingdom—New Jerusalem.
                             Illustration 42. Non-Overcomers and the New Jerusalem

                                   Entrance Residency Rulership
                            Dillow                     
                            Wilkin                     
                            Cauley                     

       The residency model of the city that Dillow came to adopt was to affirm that all believers will have free
residency within the city.33 Wilkin, on the other hand, entertains the possibility that not all believers will be allowed
to live inside the city; nevertheless, they will be allowed to enter the city.34 Crawford and I are more inclined to
postulate, however, that unfaithful saints will not be allowed to reenter the Reward City once they are excluded
from it, though we might prefer different models as to how to explain the spatial mechanics of that exclusion.35 If

33
   During our correspondences, I was concerned that Dillow might have abandoned his original residency model in
favor of Wilkin’s access model when Dillow responded: “Unfaithful saints will have free access to the city but they
have no share in it—that is, they will not enter by the gates and they will not have ownership or position there”
(personal correspondence, 12-15-2012). My apprehension was because such a vacillation would create tension with
his perspective that their tears are wiped away at this time. (At least this was his perspective about the wiping away
of tears at that point in our correspondences.) In effect, such a proposal would have the Lord wiping away their
tears and then casting them out of the city! Consequently, in order to assist him in his competing model, I suggested
to Dillow that, in Rev 20:6, to have a portion in the first resurrection denotes more than merely participating in the
first resurrection. It refers to misthological participation in that resurrection (so Darkness). I made this suggestion
so that he could use my own argument to make a counter argument to my model and suggest, in favor of his
residency model, that to lose one’s portion in the city does not necessarily mean to lose all participation in the city,
only to lose one’s misthological participation in the city. I did not consider my offering this suggestion in support
of his model to be detrimental to my exclusion model since Darkness uses a web of support, not all of which is
found in that book (e.g., I have another book, Like a Tree Planted by the Water, not yet released, which also defends
my city-exclusion model); therefore, from my perspective, this potential counter which I suggested to my own
model would by no means be a serious detriment to my overall argument. Nevertheless, it would help him strengthen
the plausibility of his model as a competing model to my own, and my goal was to assist in strengthening his model
as much possible. As I noted in our correspondences, as far as I am concerned, having internally consistent
competing FG models is better than having only one. (Personal correspondence, 12-18-2012.) For that reason, I am
pleased that Dillow retained this residency model in the latter edition and noted the reference to Wilkin’s model
which I provided (Destiny, 664, n. 2172). Scientifically speaking, three strong FG models are better than one.

Dillow’s discussion of the timing of certain key events should prove to be probable, then probability may be in
favor of a joint Crawfordian-Dillowian model. As to the tears shed as a result of the Bema, our disagreement may
be more theoretical than actual. Crawford has balanced the probabilities and ambiguities nicely:

              Some commentators have indicated that the remorse lasts only for a few moments, some for
       longer – even the entire 1000 years of the Messianic Kingdom (cf. Rev 21:4). It is possible that it lasts

       that the text does not say that all these individuals will do is weep and gnash their teeth. It will in all

        Although Crawford and I are strongly inclined to think that regret lasts for eternity, the projection of tears
lasting that long is much more debatable. My perception is that the tears of the Bride are wiped away, not the tears
of all believers. So unfaithful believers will shed tears on occasion throughout eternity. Even though Crawford
concurs that the Bride is not composed of all believers, he suggests that the tears of all believers may be wiped away
in that they will at least learn to cope with their regret sufficiently to the point that they eventually will not have any
remaining tears or sorrows, though regrets will last. In that case, one can entertain the possibility that they learn to
wipe away their own tears. Hollandsworth does not delve into this level of specificity, but his comments fall well
within the parameters of the evidence: “Unfortunately, many Christians assume that all born-again believers will
be rewarded at the Judgment Seat of Christ—some more, some less. Oh, there may be a moment of regret or agony,
but it will not be ongoing and will be quickly forgotten. They arrive at this conclusion in spite of the clear language
of Scripture.”37 He continues, “I wonder if those not chosen will be upset with themselves and have to learn to live
with regret, when they are given a rebuke and consigned to the darkness outside.”38 Although Wilkin would
consider all believers to be part of the Bride, even he likewise allows the possibility of unfaithful believers feeling
regret in the eternal state: “Everyone in the eternal kingdom will certainly be content. However, there is a sense
in which we might feel regret if we fail to rule with Christ.”39
        At least during various points in our discussion, Dillow took the popular position (1) that the Bride
encompasses both faithful and unfaithful believers and (2) that all tears, sorrows, and regrets will be removed from
all believers because of the wiping away of their tears. However, in our subsequent discussion he seemed to draw
back from the latter point, no longer regarding the wiping away of tears as necessarily being applicable to all
believers, at least as far as the two passages in Revelation would seem to apply. At least his reconsidering the
applicability of Rev 21:4 is a step in the right direction. Bozung, in contrast, makes the unwarranted assumption:
The duration of the loss of reward would necessarily need to be permanent or else the very purpose of having a
judgment would seem to be pointless. However, any regret, remorse or shame experienced (1 Cor 3:15; 2 Cor 5:10;
1 John 2:28) will not endure forever in light of texts such as Rev 21:4.”40 Actually, the opposite conclusion would
seem to be warranted since Rev 21:4 is a reward limited to faithful believers, as I demonstrated in TOD and RAE.
If the absence of any regret in the eternal state is a reward limited to faithful believers, as a proper interpretation of
Rev 21:4 would demand, then one should deduce that the absence of regret is limited to faithful believers within
the eternal state. Regret on the part of the unfaithful will endure forever in light of Rev 21:4. Since this regret is
associated with the removal of tears in the eternal state, one would be inclined to include that the absence of tears
is similarly limited to faithful believers within the eternal state. Tears on the part of the unfaithful will endure forever
in light of Rev 21:4. The discussion as to why these tears cannot be wiped away at the point indicated in Rev 21:4
will be continued in an excursus within Part 1 and within a discussion regarding the eternality of misthological
death in Part 2.
