                   Excursus Regarding Millennial Slavery
                                         Subjugation and Imprisonment
    Regarding the context of Is 45:14, Cyrus was a prototype of the Messiah, and the events of that time were but
a small-scale fulfillment of the complete fulfillment to come in the Messianic Kingdom, the Millennial Kingdom.
“In the Millennium Gentiles will...be subservient to Israel” (BKC). The objection by some commentators to the
idea of forced subjugation is rather forced. Forced subjugation is to be anticipated during the millennial kingdom,
as nations will be ruled by a rod of iron (Ps 2: 8-9; Rev 2:27; 12:5; 19:15). The rod of His millennial rule can
administer death (Is 11:3-5). It is a “rod of punishment” (Is 20:32). For that matter, imprisonment is also to be
expected during the millennial kingdom (Mt 5:22-26; 18:34-35).
                                                  Possession of Slaves
     Some have objected to the idea that such people could be slaves on the supposed premise that slavery will not
exist in the millennial kingdom because, in their estimation, millennial prosperity would make slavery unnecessary,
as would the lack of millennial punishment. However, Is 45:14 states that those in chains “will be yours.” Possession
is indicated. The imagery of slavery is most naturally involved: one group of people belonging to another group of
people. The objection that punishment will not exist in the millennial kingdom is groundless. Slavery could very
well be used as a means of millennial punishment. Additionally, the withholding of rain, for instance, from some
parts of the millennial kingdom as punishment for not submitting to Israel makes economic hardship for those parts
of the kingdom probable (Zech 14:16-19; cp. Is 60:2-12).1 Thus, some of the impoverished kingdom inhabitants
may sell themselves into slavery. Commerce will continue. Those who formerly were oppressed will rule over their
former oppressors as captors over captives (Is 14:2b). This imagery is certainly conducive to the possibility that
slavery will exist, albeit in an inverted relationship in which the rewarded slave now becomes the master. The first
part of the verse clearly confirms the existence of millennial slavery: “And the peoples will take them and bring
them to their place, and the house of Israel will possess them in the Lord’s land as male and female slaves”
(Is 14:2a; ESV). Those who formerly were oppressed will possess their former oppressors as their slaves. The
faithful will possess these people, that is, own them. These people will belong to the faithful. These slaves will serve
as shepherds, farmers, and vinedressers. Yet do not overlook the possessive nature of the relationship: “And
strangers will stand and pasture your flocks, and foreigners will be your farmers and your vinedressers” (Is 61:5).
These farmers and vinedressesers will belong to those so rewarded just as their flocks belong to them. The land and
people will be yours. Possessing these people as slaves is part of the inheritance of the faithful: “Israel will possess
them as an inheritance as male servants and female servants” (Is 14:2; NAS).
                                                     Slave Kings
    To deny Israel her slaves/servants would be to deny her part of her inheritance. Their slaves will range from
shepherds to kings. Even the millennial kings of the earth will bow down before them, bringing gold as homage to
them (Ps 72:10-15; Is 49:23; 60:16; Dan 7:27). Possibly even these flesh-and-blood, slave-kings will rise up in
revolt at the end of the millennial kingdom against their taskmasters who rule them with an iron rod.2 They may
grow tired of having to minister to Israel (Is 60:10), begrudging the serve-or-perish homage they have to yield to
Israel (Is 60:12), get fed up with Israel eating “the wealth of [the] nations [of these kings]” (Is 61:6), resent Israel
having a “double portion” while they only have a single portion (Is 61:7), rebel against being the tail ruled over by

1
  For additional evidence that hunger may exist in the outer darkness during the millennium, see Excursus Regarding
Millennial Tears, 83. Indeed, the outer darkness is characterized as a place where darkness covers the Earth
(Is 60:2). For one thing, that part of the planet will still experience night. The outer darkness is not inside the
Heavenly City.
2
  This suggestion regarding the millennial kings is offered as an alternative to the preference taken in Darkness
(pp. 632-653). If these kings of the Earth are slave-kings in flesh and blood bodies, then they are not rewarded
overcomers (Is 60:2-12). So they could revolt against their guardianship responsibilities. Even though the kings of
the Earth in Rev 21:24-26 might also be regarded as kings in flesh and blood bodies, performing much the same
homage in the eternal state, they would not revolt against paying such homage since no one in the eternal state will
have a sin nature. Only the millennial earthly kings could revolt against having to pay tribute to these heavenly
rulers who control the weather and who withhold rain from those who fail to comply with their rulership. Millennial
slave-kings could resent their millennial enslavement.
Page 82                                                                                   Misthological Models Part 1
                                                                       3
Israel as their head (Dt 28:13), and detest their chains (Ps 149:8). The millennial kingdom will not be equally
wonderful for everyone, not even close. Many will resent the inequality. Not everyone is going to be happy with
the millennial conditions. Many living in the millennial kingdom are going to be quite unhappy with the millennial
state of affairs.
                                                 Millennial Rebellion
     Adopting this perspective helps one understand why Satan will be able to instigate a mass rebellion at the end
of the millennium (Rev 20:7-9). Obviously, many in the millennial kingdom will not be happy with the millennial
kingdom. Otherwise, they would not revolt against their masters at the conclusion of the millennial kingdom! Just
as transparently, not everyone’s millennial cups are going to be full of joy. Granted, those who appeared before the
Bema (before the millennium) will not be involved in the millennial rebellion because they will have transformed
bodies that are no longer capable of sin. Still, even those in sinless bodies can regret imposed slavery, as is proven
by their weeping and gnashing their teeth while in their sinless bodies! They simply resign themselves, eventually,
to the fact that they deserve their chains and the slavery it depicts, and they set about learning how to live with their
profound regret.
                                                  Duration of Chains
       Naturally, this is not to say that one must necessarily picture them as remaining in chains the entire 1000
years, much less for all eternity. Those led in chains in Is 45:14 do not spend the entire 1000 years in chains; rather,
they wear chains on the appropriate occasions. Suppose that every 10,000 years, or so, that Sarah, in the course of
her duties, visits the part of the outer darkness where the man who formally abused her resides. Although he
normally does not wear chains while residing in the outer darkness, he could, on those occasions, be brought in
chains to kneel before her. If so, would it be too much to imagine that a few tears might flow down his cheeks on
those infrequent occasions?




3
  “The kings of the nations in millennial times go up to Jerusalem below, and wait upon the need of its citizens
Isa. lx, 10. [60:10] The saints of Israel and Zion are to bind kings in chains, and nobles in links of iron. Psa. cxlix,
8. [149:8].” Robert Govett, Govett on Revelation, 2 vols. (London: Bemrose and Sons, 1891; reprint, Miami Springs:
Conley and Schoettle Publishing Co., 1981), 2: 491.
                      Excursus Regarding Millennial Tears
                                     Forsaking of the Lord by the Redeemed
        Who are they who forsake [azab] the Lord in Is 65:11? The answer to that question was provided in the
opening chapter of Isaiah. These apostates certainly represent God’s people: “My people....have turned [azab]
away” (Is 1:3-4). The context adds: “Those who forsake [azab] the Lord shall come to an end...there will be none
to quench them” (Is 1:28-31). However, per Dillow, this does not necessarily mean that they burn soteriologically
or eternally. Or, as Dillow would express it, this judgment may “have eternal consequences [in terms of the JSC]
but may not be final consignment to the lake of fire” (Destiny, 878). Neoclassically and correlatively, I agree. After
all, in this opening context, those who forsake the Lord are His people.1 Typologically at least, this could represent
God’s regenerate people. An eternal misthological result could be entailed. I thoroughly appreciate Dillow
acknowledging the eternal nature of these misthological consequences.
        The more immediate context also allows this possibility. The very term, forsake, “implies that they left behind
someone who they had a close relationship with at some earlier time, as [Is] 63:7-14 suggests.”2 How close a
relationship was it? Close enough to be considered God’s redeemed people, who have God as their Savior because
He saved them:
      8
       For He said, “Surely, they are My people, sons who will not deal falsely.” So He became their Savior.
      9
       In all their affliction He was afflicted, and the angel of His presence saved them; in His love and in
      His mercy He redeemed them; and He lifted them and carried them all the days of old. 10 But they
      rebelled and grieved His Holy Spirit; therefore, He turned Himself to become their enemy, He fought
      against them. (Is 63:8-10)

       As to God’s redeemed people grieving His Holy Spirit, Paul urges NT believers: “Do not grieve the Holy
Spirit of God, by whom you were sealed for the day of redemption” (Eph 4:30). Grieving the Holy Spirit in this
manner is not proof that one is not redeemed. To the contrary, the fact that they are redeemed is what makes this
sin so grievous. These NT believers had the Holy Spirit within them, and these OT believers had the Holy Spirit “in
the midst of them” (Is 63:11). As to God becoming their enemy, James acknowledges that this happens to NT
believers: “Whoever wishes to be a friend of the world makes himself an enemy of God” (Jam 4:4). Nothing
prevents one from concluding that those who forsake the Lord or rebel against the Lord in this manner could not be
the redeemed of the Lord. If this is the case, then those who are weeping in Is 65:14 are the Lord’s redeemed people
who had forsaken Him.
                                        OT Background for Outer Darkness
        As a result, Is 65:14 could be a picture of the outer darkness: misthological weeping outside the city. After
all, the contextual juxtaposition is between God’s faithful people who inherit the holy mountain as their inhabitation
versus God’s unfaithful people who do not (Is 65:9). Nonetheless, I will not insist that those who forsake the Lord
in this passage are necessarily redeemed, at least not historically, though it does seem extremely likely that
typologically they are considered redeemed, even in this context. If this is the case, the fate of the unregenerate
versus regenerate apostates is not sharply delineated in Is 65:11-15, just as the millennial and eternal states are not
clearly delineated in Is 65:16-25. The NT is needed for this clarification. After all, being slain by the sword
(Is 1:12,15) does not fit with being hungry and thirsty (Is 65:13). Being physically dead does not mesh with being
physically hungry and thirsty. Dead men are not hungry. Typologically, if not necessarily or exclusively historically,
these apostates, at least the hungry and thirsty ones, probably represent God’s rebellious regenerate people.

1
  In this context, those who revolt against the Lord are God’s children, “sons” whom He has “reared and brought
up,” (Is 1:2), His “rebellious children” (Is 30:1). This “rebellious people” (Is 65:2) are the ones addressed in Is 65:11
as, “You who forsake the Lord.” Unfortunately, the NAS refers to them as “false sons” in Is 30:9. But as numerous
translations attest, they are false in the sense of acting falsely (i.e., lying, being deceptive). They are God’s genuine
children, but His “lying children” (ESV, NKJ). Typologically at least, they are God’s redeemed children. See
Darkness, 375-379. At the very least, typologically speaking, these backsliding Jews represent God’s regenerate
children.
2
  Gary Smith, NAC, Isaiah, s.v., Is 65:11-12.
Page 84                                                                                    Misthological Models Part 1
Unregenerate apostates will be killed at the end of the tribulation and not allowed to enter the millennial kingdom.
They will be slain. Perhaps the passage envisions this possibility, at least inclusively as what will happen at the
future point in history. Contrastively, regenerate apostates, whether they be considered as such historically or
typologically, will be allowed to enter the kingdom in flesh and blood bodies.3 They will not be slain. But they will
hunger, thirst, and be put to shame, while they cry and weep outside of Jerusalem.
       If Dillow’s model for Gehenna is adopted, at least neoclassically, which I would allow, then their unquenched
burning within this background passage of Is 1:28-31 need not be a soteriological burning. The fire could represent
their eternal loss, without necessarily meaning that they are soteriologically lost. If Dillow’s proposal is correct,
then the conflicting imagery in Is 65:11-15 allows both historical and typological fulfillment, corresponding to a
soteriological and misthological application.
       However, if this is the case, then Dillow’s attempt to limit the outer darkness to the tribulation is refuted in
that this OT text, which provides a background for this NT imagery regarding the outer darkness, would place the
outer darkness in the millennial time period, and probably in the eternal state as well since the two periods of time
are not delineated contextually. Whereas the references to those slain (Is 65:12,15) would refer to unregenerate
apostates, verses 13-14 would refer to the regenerate portion of the apostates, thus providing the background for
Jesus’ contrast in Lk 19:24-27 and also for the outer darkness passages in Matthew, applying the outer darkness
exclusively to those who are not slain. Consequently, the heart of the passage from Isaiah would be misthological:
“Behold, My servants shall shout joyfully with a glad heart [during the millennial and eternal states], but you [My
regenerate people who forsook Me] shall cry out with a heavy heart [outside of Jerusalem], and you shall wail with
a broken spirit [during the millennial and eternal states]” (Is 65:14). Those who are slain represent the unregenerate
soteriologically; whereas, those who are not slain, which is the case in this verse, represent the regenerate
misthologically.
       Alternately, one could take Is 65:14 as a picture of those who weep and gnash their teeth outside the city as
a composite picture (1) of those who are slain (thus soteriological) and (2) of those who hunger and thirst (thus
misthological). Jesus used the picture of weeping and gnashing of teeth in this dual capacity. Moreover, the NT
uses being outside the city in both manners also.4 This model is also very attractive. In either case, the evidence
leads to the conclusion that Is 65:14 is an OT picture of the misthological reality depicted in the NT by the outer
darkness as a place (1) of exclusion from the Reward City (2) within the millennial kingdom (3) where unfaithful
regenerate believers weep in profound regret. Conceivably, the verse might also be used correlatively to picture
(4) unregenerate apostates who will weep not only in their exclusion from life in the city but even more in their
separation from life in the kingdom itself, pictured by their loss of life itself in being slain.5
3
  Seemingly, Dillow has abandoned this position as being ad hoc. I maintain that it is a reasonable inference.
4
  For example, see Rev 22:15 in Darkness as a soteriological verse with correlative misthological applicability.
5
  My model does not require that I regard the rebel Jews who are slain in Is 65:12,15 as regenerate. Typologically,
though, their redeemed status may very well be inferred. Historically, however, I do not infer that all apostate Jews
who are slain in that point in history, or any other point in history for that matter, are regenerate. Generally speaking,
some of them are regenerate; some of them are not. I suspect that this prophecy is broad in scope, not just picturing
the death of Jewish apostates at the end of the tribulation but throughout history. In other words, this passage
portrays, in principle, the way that God’s deals with His apostate people, regardless as to whether they are merely
His people ethnically or also in terms of spiritual birth. Quite possibly, the prophecy should be understood as being
broad enough in scope to cover the tribulation, the millennium, and the rebellion at the end of the millennium. The
mixed imagery of being slain and yet being hungry may be intended to be broad enough to cover each possibility
in regard to the type and time of the apostasy.
       As to the type of their apostasy, the apostates envisioned may either be unregenerate, regenerate, or both. As
to the time of their apostasy, it could be before, during, or at the conclusion of the millennial kingdom. Unregenerate
apostates will be killed at the end of the tribulation. During the course of the millennium, apostates will not resist
in all-out rebellion; nevertheless, their rebellious nature will result in their being punished in terms of being hungry
and thirsty. Both unregenerate and regenerate apostates will be killed in the rebellion at the end of the millennium.
At one level, then, Jesus may intend to use this OT material to picture the soteric-mistholic contrast pictured in
Lk 19:24-27. At another level, yet again, this may not be, and probably is not, the only level at which Scripture
intends for us to understand this material in Is 65:11-15. The Scripture intends for us to be mature enough to
differentiate between typological and historical fulfillment and see the applicability of both to a singular passage of
Scripture when so warranted. For example, see “Multiple Vantage Points,” Darkness, 944.
Excursus Regarding Millennial Tears                                                                              Page 85
                                           Premature Death at Age 100
       That Isaiah intends to include those slain during the millennial kingdom within the scope of his statements
in Is 65:12,15 is confirmed, contextually, by his statement in v. 20 regarding the millennial period: “No longer will
there be in it an infant who lives but a few days, or an old man who does not live out his days; for the youth will
die at the age of one hundred and the one who does not reach the age of one hundred shall be thought accursed”
(Is 65:20). As noted in Darkness regarding Is 65:20, Baughman comments:

             The rebellious are ‘destroyed from among the people,” perhaps at the end of each hundred years
      during the millennium. If this is the case, the rebels mentioned in Revelation 20:7-8 are those born
      during the last one hundred years of Christ’s reign on earth.
             The Jews today practice a ritual called Bar Mitzvah on the first Sabbath after a boy’s thirteenth
      birthday….In the millennium, grace may be reached until the age of one hundred before judgment is
      exercised (p. 225).6
6
  Ray E. Baughman, The Kingdom of God Visualized (Birmingham, Shepherd Press, 1972). Compare also the
statement by Lange’s Commentary: “There is clear reference here to the Mosaic law which promises long life and
a numerous posterity to the godly, and, on the contrary, threatens shortening of life and speedy extinction of name
to the wicked (Ex. 20:5, 6, 12; 23:26)” (Isaiah, s.v., Is 65:20). So also Spence: “Even the sinner, who is under the
curse of God, shall not be cut off till he is a hundred” (Isaiah, s.v., Is 65:20). As noted, loss of rain and even death
is decreed to those who do not obey during the millennial kingdom (Zech 14:16-19; Is 60:12). Peters ties these
passages with Is 65:20 (3: 598). See also Pentecost: “Any overt act against the authority of the King will be punished
with physical death.” J. Dwight Pentecost, Things to Come: A Study in Biblical Eschatology (Grand Rapids:
Zondervan Publishing House, 1958), 503. He cites the following passages in support: Ps 2:9 [cp. perish in 2:12];
72:1-4 [crush the oppressor]; Is 11:4 [slay]; 29:20-21 [come to an end]; 65:20 [die]; 66:24[!!!]; Zech 14:16-21
[punishment of no rain]; Jer 31:29-30 [die]. Note that those killed in Is 65:15 have their name left for a curse and
those killed during the millennial state will be thought accursed in Is 65:20. Surely Is 65:15 has millennial and,
therefore, misthological applicability.
     Pentecost’s inclusion of Is 66:24 was most surprising, at least to me initially, in that I had not thought to combine
that passage with these other passages in this way. I had presumed that Is 66:24, in regard to its physical fulfillment,
referred to the fate of those killed at the end of the tribulation (Darkness, 1103). Dillow makes the same assumption
(Destiny, 875-876). Yet Pentecost’s inclusion of this passage as being a picture of the physical punishment for those
living during the millennial kingdom is well received herein in view of the present analysis: “Then they shall go
forth and look on the corpses of the men who have transgressed against Me. For their worm shall not die, and their
fire shall not be quenched; and they shall be an abhorrence to all mankind” (Is 66:24). Ironically, this strengthens
Dillow’s case regarding Gehenna; indeed, it virtually proves his case that Gehenna is applicable to believers.
But it weakens his case that the millennial kingdom does not have a significant population of carnal believers and
that the outer darkness does not extend into and beyond the millennial kingdom. Given the OT context and
background of Is 66:24, the verse should be understood as having a dualistic dual applicability to: (1) tribulational-
millennial temporal fulfillment within the course of history pertaining to physical death, on the one hand, and
(2) soteriological-misthological postmortem application in regard to metaphysical death, on the other hand. As it
pertains to those physically slain during the tribulation, it would have primary, possibly even exclusive, applicability
to the unregenerate. As it pertains to those physically slain during the millennium, it would have primary
applicability to the regenerate. Isaiah begins his book with a lament about God’s people rebelling against Him and
concludes with God’s judgment upon all who transgress against Him. The rebels from among His people are not
excluded from this judgment, which will fall upon those within His kingdom. Since Is 66 is intended to apply to
both unbelievers and believers, this verse, in its OT anticipation, should be taken neoclassically as being both
soteriological and misthological. This consideration sways the argument in favor of interpreting Mt 18:9-10
neoclassically. Therefore, although in Darkness I entertained both a classical (pp. 818-820) and neoclassical (pp.
948-950) model for Mt 18:9-10, my analysis herein, when enjoined with Dillow’s superb study regarding Gehenna,
yields the neoclassical result as the preferred model.
       Not surprisingly, though, is Pentecost’s inclusion of Ps 2:9 in the list of passages proving that the millennial
reign of Christ will administer physical death to the overtly disobedient. After all, the preceding verse, Ps 2:8, is
obviously millennial in its fulfillment, promising that the nations will be given to Christ as an inheritance. He will
rule these inherited nations with a rod of iron during the millennial kingdom. John uses this rod imagery in
Page 86                                                                                  Misthological Models Part 1

       An age of accountability that varies with the dispensation is certainly reasonable, if that is what Baughman
means to suggest. However, even if the age of accountability is extended to age one hundred during the millennium,
this would not necessarily prove that all those who died as rebels were unregenerate. Capital punishment would be
applicable to believers also if they rebel. In contradiction to these rebels who are killed, Eze 20:37 merely poses
that rebellious Jews who are alive at the beginning of the millennium will not be allowed to enter the Promised
Land. If, contrary to popular opinion, any regenerate Jews are in that number, then they would be candidates for the
outer darkness. As to the rebels deceived by Satan at the end of the millennium, what is to prevent one from thinking
that some of those led astray are believers? If so, they will be devoured with fire from Heaven. They, along with
the rest of the dead, will be raised afterward. Like other regenerate rebels killed every hundred years, they will be
sentenced to the outer darkness. (Darkness, 988, n. 121.)
       In that earlier treatment, I also explained why Eze 20:37 is best taken as indicating that rebellious Jews
passing under that rod at the end of the tribulation would be allowed into the millennial kingdom but bared from
the Promised Land, which is but a special part of that kingdom (Darkness, 413-414). Yet Dillow thinks that such
arguments, as what I am implementing here and what he previously used in Reign, are “ad hoc” and “rather strained”
and descend into “absurdity and speculation” in asserting that there will be “carnal unfaithful tribulational saints in
mortal bodies” who enter the millennial kingdom and in thinking that “those who have survived the horrors of the
tribulation but not yet reached the age of accountability” are the ones who enter the millennial kingdom in flesh and
blood bodies (Destiny, 102). To remove all doubt as to his new position, he forcefully states: “One might argue that
unfaithful believing mortals may enter the kingdom and yet not inherit. This, however, leads to the ridiculous
conclusion that the millennium is populated by carnal Christians because all the faithful ones will receive
resurrection bodies” (Destiny, 120).
                                                  Carnal Survivors
        Okay, Dillow finds such positions as what I am advocating here and in Darkness, to be: “ad hoc,” “rather
strained,” descending into “absurdity and speculation,” and “ridiculous.” So be it. But why? What are the biblical
and logical grounds for his objections? Are his forceful assertions matched by equally forceful arguments? Far from
it! My first observation is that he still acknowledges: “It is clear that during the tribulation the love of many will
grow cold (Matthew 24:12), that there will be unfaithful Christians during this time, and some will live to the end”
(Destiny, 392). This latter statement is from an excellent section in Destiny entitled the “Mark of the Beast.”
Originally, he added this section to Reign as a result of a correspondence that he and I had in 1993. As noted in my
book, I posed this model regarding the mark of the beast to him back then, as a solution by which he might
harmonize “his position that unfaithful believers live until the end of the tribulation” (Darkness, 362). To his credit,
he retained that section in Destiny. I am perplexed now, though, in light of this continued acknowledgement, that
he has drawn back from applying it to kingdom entrance. If carnal believers will survive the tribulation, as we both
still agree, and as Dillow has maintained all along, even before that correspondence in 1993, then why has he now
shrunk back from affirming that carnal believers enter the kingdom in mortal bodies? Those dispensationalists
typically arguing that only faithful believers will enter the kingdom normally argue that unfaithful believers will
die during the tribulation. Since Dillow, wisely, has never followed that faulty line of reasoning, not even in Reign,
what recourse is he left with now by which to defend his new thesis that only faithful believers enter the millennial
kingdom in flesh and blood bodies?
        What happens to those carnal believers who survive the tribulation? Does God kill them only to resurrect
them immediately so that He can give them glorified bodies and throw them into the outer darkness? If so, why?

Rev 19:15, not only to help picture the termination of the tribulation, but more so in anticipation of millennial rule
(Rev 20:1-6). Christ will rule the millennial nations with a rod of iron (Rev 2:27). Therefore, the kings being warned
in Ps 2:10-12 are millennial kings, not tribulational kings, as is commonly assumed. Consequently, it should come
as no surprise that the rulers taking counsel against the Lord in Ps 2:1 are this same group of millennial kings. These
Gentile kings want to tear away the shackles and chains of their millennial, slavish servitude to the nation of Israel.
Since Israel will not rule the Gentile nations during the tribulation, a millennial rebellion must be envisioned. Thus,
the battle of Gog and Magog at the end of the millennial kingdom, not before, is the battle envisioned (Rev 20:8).
A predominantly carnal rebellion would be the most natural supposition. Just as not all the Exodus generation would
have been regenerate but, nevertheless, depicts a carnal rebellion on the part of God’s people, so the wicked of Ps 1
(as depicted in Ps 2) would represent a carnal rebellion on the part of the God’s people also.
Excursus Regarding Millennial Tears                                                                             Page 87
Why not allow them to enter the millennial kingdom in flesh and blood bodies. After all, the Lord needs someone
to repopulate the millennial kingdom in order to fulfill His OT promises. Why not use these ready candidates? Why
does Dillow strain so hard with his rhetoric to eliminate this group as the most natural candidates for millennial
procreation? Does he consider the opportunity to have sex and procreate during the millennial kingdom to be a
reward reserved for the faithful!?! If so, it is too bad that this reward is limited by Dillow to the millennial kingdom!
Similarly, Dillow wishes to eliminate the children “who have survived the horrors of the tribulation but not yet
reached the age of accountability” as being candidates from entering the millennial kingdom in flesh and blood
bodies. How does he eliminate them? It will take more than rhetoric. Does Dillow pose that God kills these young
children in order to eliminate them!?! Or does God bypass killing them and just go straight to giving them glorified
bodies? If so, does He just skip over the need of their growing up to the point where they can exercise saving faith?
But if so, why? Why not just let them enter the millennial kingdom in flesh and blood bodies and grow up and
exercise saving faith within the due course of time?
        Dillow has run roughshod over numerous inductive considerations in his attempt to limit millennial
procreation to rewarded believers. He practically has staked his entire procreation argument on the sentiment that
the millennial kingdom would not be populated by carnal Christians because he considers that proposition to be
preposterous. In response, let a few questions be entrained. Are we to suppose that it is faithful Christians who rise
up in mass rebellion against the Lord at the end of the millennial kingdom only to be killed!?! Are we to imagine
that it is spiritual Christians who have to be ruled by a rod of iron during the millennial kingdom!?! Are we to think
that the rebellious children being killed each hundred years during the millennium are the children of faithful
Christians!?! Are we to believe that it is overcomers who have been given white robes who are now procreating
while wearing these robes!?! Were they not given these robes in order to officiate over the kingdom rather than
procreate within it!?! Why does Dillow insist that the opportunity to have sex is an eschatological reward!?! Surely
Mormons will appreciate Dillow’s new position! I suppose they would reason this way: According to Dillow,
millennial sex is a reward for being faithful. Rewards are eternal. Therefore, millennial sex is an eternal reward for
being faithful.7 To put it mildly, Dillow’s new model does not appear to be problem free.
                                            Millennial Blessings and Woes
       Answers provided to objections against my models that already have been expounded in Darkness (pp. 60-
67) need not be repeated here. Our mutual readers may consult our mutual discussions. Let the informed reader
decide, from a scientific perspective, which procreation model, for example, is truly in accord with the biblical data.
For present purposes, it suffices to note that Is 65:20 collaborates my perception that the death rendered by the
sword against the rebellious portion of God’s children in Is 65:12,15 is millennial in scope. In fact, Dillow’s
own summation suggests this very conclusion. In regard to Is 65:20, he correctly cites it in support of his statement
that during the millennial kingdom “there will be physical death.” I would have preferred an explanation as to why,
in his model, people die in their youth during the millennial kingdom according to this verse. Would it not be a
judicial death in response to those carnal youth who rebel during the millennial kingdom? In any event, he does go
on immediately to note the rebellion at the end of the millennial kingdom:

      Due to sin (which is inconsistent with the eternal state) a multitude of unregenerate men in mortal
      bodies will rebel at the end of the thousand-year kingdom and will be ‘devoured,’ hardly an
      experience of resurrected and immortal saints (1 John 3:2; Revelation 20:7-10). We are told that Jesus
      will reign over His enemies in the millennium (Ps 110:2; 1 Corinthians 15:25). (Destiny, 103)

      In counterpoint, I pose that many of the Lord’s enemies at the end of the tribulation will be regenerate.8 That

point aside for the moment, though, note that Dillow acknowledges that Jesus will be ruling over His enemies during

passage is at least inclusively millennial in its intended application. Although I have justified this perception
contextually, above from Isaiah, and collaborated it from Dillow’s own acknowledgment, further inductive data
from Jesus’ use of this OT imagery will be brought to the table to further demonstrate that Is 65:11-15 is millennial
in its application.

      Isaiah anticipates an eschatological reversal denoted by two X/Y categories delineated by behold/but.
Specifically, these reversals are depicted by: eat/hunger; drink/thirsty; rejoice/shame; joyful/sorrowful. Jesus adopts
the same perspective and elaborates upon the reversal with introductory rubric falling under two similar categories:
blessed/woe. The four behold-My-servants promises of Isaiah become the four blessed-are-you blessings of Jesus.

amongst the angelic sons of God from within God’s kingdom, so he will be allowed to come home to roost one last
time and invoke a similar rebellion among God’s children in this final battle. As Satan and his angelic following
who rebelled in the beginning will be cast into the Lake of Fire eternally, so carnal believers who follow him in the
end will be cast correlatively into the outer darkness eternally. The final confinement for both will be eternal and
off-planet. In addition to using the thousand years to demonstrate that carnality can thrive secretly even under a
perfect government and that humanity needs something more than forced external compliance (and thus needs an
internal change that removes the possibility of sin), God will use the thousand years to mirror the original rebellion
that occurred in His kingdom when the sons of God rebelled. God will allow His carnal children to rebel and thus
experience a correlative exclusion. The original rebel will be cast into the literal darkness of the Lake of Fire
eternally at the commencement of the eternal state. The final carnal rebels will be cast into the misthological outer
darkness eternally at the commencement of the eternal state. The final rebellion will give the Lord opportunity to
demonstrate to all, once and for all, how God deals with rebellion within His own house.
       Although the models offered by Crawford and I at this juncture differ regarding the particulars, we both pose
that the outer darkness is eternal in its duration and will be off-planet in its location in eternity future. The Dillowian
model, in contrast, seems ill-equipped to deal with carnal believers who are resurrected at the end of the millennium
to stand before God in judgment. Evidently, within Dillow’s model, their punishment would be to be sent to live in
New Jerusalem. Some punishment! Most people would call that woe a blessing would they not?

Excursus Regarding Millennial Tears                                                                                    Page 89
Similarly, the four but-you warnings of Isaiah become the four woe-to-you woes of Jesus. Seemingly, if we could
determine who the you is to whom Jesus refers, we would have strong grounds for identifying the you of whom
Isaiah prophesizes. To whom and when are these eschatological woes to be applied?
       According to Dillow, we should understand the warnings in the Sermon on the Mount (such as Mt 5:20 and
7:20) to be addressed to Jesus’ believing disciples. Neoclassically, I am completely open to that suggestion. My
friend, Scott Crawford, has been urging me to adopt this perspective for many years, long before Dillow came to
adopt this position. For sake of simplicity, I will adopt this new-Dillowian-old-Crawfordian perspective in the
present discussion. After all, as I noted in Darkness, I am open to their perspective at the neoclassical level, and
Dillow’s Destiny has been very helpful in increasing my openness to this proposition at that neoclassical level. And
Steve Elkins has released a book defending the thesis that the Sermon is urging believers on to kingdom greatness,
not kingdom entrance: Keys to Kingdom Greatness: An Exposition of the Sermon on the Mount. By logical
extension, the Sermon on the Plain should likewise be understood as addressing believing disciples as well. As
noted in the chart above, these blessings and woes are addressed by Jesus to “His disciples.” Certainly, the blessings
are intended exclusively for Jesus’ believing disciples. Consistency would dictate that the same group is being
addressed, at least inclusively, by the parallel woes. Blessings for faithful believers are being set in juxtaposition
with woes for unfaithful believers.10 This answers the question, “To whom do the woes apply?” How about when?

10
   Commentators attempt to avoid concluding that the passage is addressing believers by making the you refer back
to the Pharisees in Lk 6:7-11. However, Jesus has left that setting in the synagogue and has gone to the mountain
where He calls His disciples to Him to choose them (Lk 6:12-13), and then to instruct and warn them (Lk 6:17-20).
Multiple times Luke informs us that the sermon is for His disciples. To import Pharisaical spies into the sermon as
the object of the address must jump over a mountain of evidence. The passage is concerned with eschatological
blessings and woes for His disciples. The Pharisees are certainly included among the men who hate you, who
ostracize you, and who cast insults at you (Lk 6:22). The unaddressed and non-present Pharisees are to be counted
among the third person plural men (hoi anthropoi), not among the second person plural you (humas): men ≠ you.
To make the explicit you (humas) in Lk 6:26 point to the men rather than the same group addressed by you
mentioned in Lk 6:22, as GNTC does, smacks of eisegesis. While it is true that Matthew makes a you = them
equation in Mt 7:23, so that we are left to debate the identity of them in that context, no such equation is made by
Luke in Lk 6:20-27. To the contrary, in this Lukan context you = you, the disciples. When Luke wants to specify
that Pharisees are being addressed by woe, he has no trouble doing so in other contexts: “Woe to you Pharisees!”
(Lk 11:42-43)
       As it is, Jesus did not have to use the second person plural you (humas) in Lk 6:26. He could have used the
third person and allowed it to come across in second person, just as He did in Lk 6:21-22, where the Greek literally
reads in the third person: blessed are the poor ones; blessed are the ones who hunger; blessed are those who mourn.
Yet in English, this third person in these verses is almost always rendered in English as second person in order to
be consistent with the second person plural verbs that follow and thus avoid a mismatch. Otherwise, the translation
would be rendered, for example, as follows: Blessed are those who hunger now, for you will be satisfied. Those =
you. The presence of second person plural pronoun in Lk 6:24-25 is unnecessary in Greek, being redundant (cp. Lk
6:22 in Greek). Thus its presence in Lk 6:24-25 pinpoints, with redundant emphasis, that the woes are being
addressed to the disciples. Even GNTC recognizes that the woe of Lk 6:26 is addressed to the disciples rather than
the men of 6:22-23. GNTC practically had to make this acknowledgment because the you is distinguished from, not
equated with, the men (hoi anthropoi) in that verse! So why, pray tell, assume that the you in the other two verses
equals the men (hoi anthropoi)? The men (hoi anthropoi) of 6:22 is equivalent to the men (hoi anthropoi) of 6:26
(and 6:31): men (hoi anthropoi) = men (hoi anthropoi) ≠ you or you.
       The alleged distinction, made by some commentators, between the you without versus the you within is
nothing but fiction. To suppose that Jesus is using an apostrophe to address an imaginary audience that is not present
takes a flight of fancy from the context which supplies the very audience being addressed. One need not imagine
an audience when it is sitting right there in front of you! Is it not very presumptuous to presume that the audience
is absent when Luke has stressed that the audience is present? Marshall is much more reasonable:

      “It is usually thought that this is a case of apostrophe, since the persons in question are not present,
      and 6:27 takes up the theme of the Sermon addressed to those who are present (ὑμῖν … τοῖς ἀκούουσιν). If this
      is the case, it is hard to see why Luke has used the second person form (especially if the original beatitudes were
      in the third person). It is more likely that the words are addressed directly to persons in spiritual danger, who
Page 90                                                                                        Misthological Models Part 1
        The eschatological nature of these woes is apparent enough. But what specific period of time in that
eschatological future is envisioned? When will these unfaithful believers, to whom Jesus directs these warnings, be
hungry, thirsty, ashamed, and sorrowful? Presumably, it is not before the Bema; eschatologically, it must be after
the Bema. So when and how long after the Bema will this state of affairs exist: seven years, a thousand years,
eternity? The impression from the text itself is that the state of affairs within the kingdom is being described. These
are kingdom blessings, as is denoted by Jesus linking them with “the kingdom of God.” Specifically, they are
kingdom blessings for those who will possess the future kingdom. Jesus is saying that the kingdom is yours, it
belongs to you, if you are a faithful believer who qualifies for these blessings. Conversely, if you are an unfaithful
believer, these kingdom blessings are inverted into kingdom woes by the role reversal. Instead of being blessed at
that future time period, you will suffer then. When? During the same time period—the time of the kingdom
blessings. Because rewards are eternal and the passage is explicitly talking about such rewards, explicitly affirming
that “your reward is great,” the most natural supposition is that the woes are equally eternal and great. This
impression is confirmed by the OT background from Isaiah in that he immediately follows his equivalent
eschatological woes with a telescopic description of both the eternal and millennial state, which merges them
together (Is 65:16-25). These blessings are not for seven years up in Heaven. Those receiving these blessings are
“blessed in the earth” (Is 65:16), even the “new earth” (Is 65:17). Glory! One reason these rewards are so great is
because they are eternal. Transparently, one of the reasons these woes are so horrific is because they too are eternal.
The Lord does not take away the misthological status of these blessings by sharing them eventually with the
unfaithful. These blessings do not cease to be rewards reserved for the faithful after the unfaithful have had a few
years or a thousand years to mourn and weep.
        Most importantly, note carefully that this cross comparison between Isaiah and Jesus reveals that Is 63:13-
14 is a misthological text pertaining, at least inclusively, to the state of affairs as it will exist during the millennial
kingdom. At the very least, this misthological-millennial text proves, upon inductive examination, that unfaithful
believers will be hungry and thirsty in remorseful shame during the millennial kingdom. Any theoretical notion that
their tears are removed at the beginning of the millennium is falsified.
        Might one object that Isaiah is merely describing the state of affairs for those unfaithful believers born during
the millennial state? That is, might Isaiah just be picturing the carnal children living during that time period and not
their carnal parents who entered the millennial kingdom in flesh and blood bodies? Such an objection, if raised,
would be truly ridiculous. The text addresses the millennial and misthological fate of the carnal, irrespective as to
whether they are children or parents. The only distinction suggested within the context is that the rebellious children
are killed every hundred years. Now if these rebellious children are killed every hundred years, as Baughman
suggests, then how much greater would be the rebellion on the tenth anniversary of this purging when Satan is
released from his prison to inflame this festering, rebellious spirit? The full-fledged uprising, pictured by John in
Rev 22:7-9, is not that surprising if one properly understands what is happening during the millennial kingdom.
Many of the rebellious subjects within that kingdom will need to be ruled with a rod of iron. One does not rule
meek, compliant sheep with a rod of iron. The nature of that iron rule will result in many of these rebellious subjects
hungering and thirsting, and some of them being slain by the sword. Hunger will be no game in that kingdom.
Hunger will be a punishment administered to those who are noncompliant with the kingdom rule.
        Unlike the popular movie Hunger Games, hunger will be a choice in the millennial kingdom. One can eagerly
embrace the societal ranking that God will put in place and earnestly seek the favor of His chosen people, which
many will do, acknowledging, “God is with you” (Is 45:14; Zech 8:23). Or they can choose not to comply and


       may well have been present to listen to Jesus (on this or on some other occasion); certainly this situation seems
       to be presupposed in 6:46–49.

the warnings. Yet, in deference to the application that is made between the non-emphatic you of Lk 6:46 and
generalized everyone and man of Lk 6:47-49, the passage as a whole may be properly presumed to have contextual
and neoclassical applicability in its warning to both believers and unbelievers. After all, Judas is addressed as well.

that the warnings are addressed to believers inclusively suffices to prove that even believers may hunger and thirst
misthologically.

perish as a result. God will be with the Jews in a special way. Everyone in the millennial kingdom will be with
God in a sense; they are in His kingdom after all, but not everyone will have equal access to Him. Those dwelling
in His kingdom who seek His blessings through the intermediary means He provides, such as the Jews living in the
Promised Land, will be blessed. Those who are reluctant to do so will go hungry.
                                              Millennial Hunger and Thirst
       Might the objection be entertained, though, that such hunger and thirsting are not applicable to transformed
believers of the church age who are living in the millennial kingdom? After all, they will have glorified bodies that
are no longer subject to such things, right? Wrong. Postmortem bodies do not need food and drink to survive but to
thrive. The rich man in postmortem torment was thirsty. One reasonably would presume that those who eat of the
tree of life will have an appetite to do so, as this will increase their enjoying the experience. What about rich church-
age believers who are warned by Jesus and James and who, according to John, will be denied the right to eat of the
tree of life? Would they have no appetite to do so? Might they not hunger eschatologically as well? Whereas one
might entertain such a theoretical objection as I raised above regarding Isaiah’s description of this hunger as not
being applicable to those in a glorified state because his warning does not address church-age believers and is
focused on those who will be living in flesh and blood bodies during the millennial state, Jesus, in contrast, addresses
these woes to those believers who presently were following Him. While these blessings and woes will be millennial
and eternal in their fulfillment, they are not limited in their application to those living in flesh and blood bodies
during the millennial kingdom. If you are a living, breathing, believing follower of Christ right now, these blessings
and warnings jointly apply to you. You are the you.
       To make this application a little more concrete, consider the fact that Dillow and Crawford believe that the
rich young ruler who approached Jesus was a living, breathing, believing follower of Christ right then in that time
frame. Imagine this young believer sitting there and hearing this sermon. He knew he had eternal life as a free gift.
Jesus had promised this gift to him freely as a believer. But what about these woes being addressed to Jesus’
followers? Would they not include him? Of course they would. Jesus was warning him that whereas he was rich
and receiving his comfort in full now, he might experience a woeful role reversal in the kingdom to come. Praying,
“Thy kingdom come,” was difficult for him since he worried that he might be poor in that coming kingdom. The
rich young believer would like to pray that prayer joyfully, though. So he inquires of Jesus as to how he might have
eternal life abundantly, not merely freely, in the kingdom to come. Jesus had promised him free entrance into the
kingdom, he got that, but Jesus just as clearly had promised him that he would be hungry and thirsty in that future
kingdom if he did not apply that kingdom rule to his heart and life right now. No wonder he went to Jesus to inquire
what he might do to have life at that level!
       Living, breathing believers today should not think that being in glorified, postmortem bodies in that day will
spare them from being hungry and thirsty. Jesus did not overlook the postmortem state of those who would stand
before Him in judgment when He issued these woes! He fully anticipated rebuking them in that state and casting
them into the outer darkness! The message of the outer darkness is not reserved for those carnal Jewish believers
who survive the tribulation, even though it includes them. Flesh and blood bodies or glorified bodies, it matters not,
if you have been unfaithful, these woes apply to you. James’ application of this warning to church-age believers
must not go unnoticed: “Come now, you rich, weep and howl for your miseries which are coming upon you”
(Jam 5:1). Woe to you church-age believers who fail to take this warning to heart. The very thing that James
anticipates is the postmortem judgment of church-age believers in the last days at the coming of the Lord (Jam 5:3,8-
9). Jesus says that they will “mourn and weep” (Lk 6:25). James says that they will “weep and howl” (Jam 5:1).
Jesus and James are both talking about the same fate, a fate anticipated by the prophet Isaiah.
                                             Outer Darkness in Isaiah
       Isaiah did not term this misthological fate “the outer darkness” as such, but he did anticipate the reality of it:
“Behold, My servants shall shout joyfully with a glad heart, but you shall cry out with a heavy heart, and you shall
wail with a broken spirit” (Is 65:14). The Tim LaHaye Prophecy Study Bible rightly provides Mt 8:12 as a cross
reference to Is 65:14. Jesus warns: “The sons of the kingdom shall be cast out into the outer darkness; in that place
there shall be weeping and gnashing of teeth.” LaHaye also provides Lk 13:28 as the other cross reference, and
indeed I am open to Dillow’s understanding of this passage: “There will be weeping and gnashing of teeth there
when you see Abraham and Isaac and Jacob and all the prophets in the kingdom of God, but yourselves being cast
out [from the Messiah Banquet]” (Lk 13:28; cp. Lk 13:29). But I just cannot imagine what Dillow tells me that I

11
     Peters, 3: 92-94.
Page 92                                                                                 Misthological Models Part 1
can imagine, as to what will happen next:

             Whatever the time period of remorse during their exclusion from the marriage banquet in the
      New Jerusalem during the tribulation, we are told that even in the [millennial] kingdom that unfaithful
      saints of all ages will know the experience of weeping because of being excluded from the Messianic
      Banquet (Luke 13:28. [Very true!] One can imagine [no I cannot!] the Lord at some time after the
      beginning of the millennium, forgiving and and [sic.] restoring to fellowship all those unfaithful
      Christians for whom He died and for whom His loving heart still desires. (Destiny, 762)

        Why it takes so long for Jesus’ death to kick in and save them from the outer darkness, Dillow does not tell
us. Is Jesus’ death not applicable to them before that time? Also, why does it take the Lord so long to forgive and
restore them if, as Dillow imagines, they repented at the Bema and were embraced like a prodigal son then? Does
the Lord forgive them, embrace them, put the ring on the finger, the robe on their back at the Bema, and then cast
them into the outer darkness, and then bring them out of the outer darkness years later and repeat the process!?! I
am finding it impossible to imagine what Dillow tells me I can imagine. Apart from straining our imaginations,
Dillow’s argument strains against the numerous inductive details noted above.
                                        Mutuality of Wiping Away of Tears
       Nevertheless, I do find it healthy to try to imagine what the picture might look like when viewed in harmony
with the details as we know them. So I imagine a different scenario. I do not imagine the Lord suffering for hundreds
of years from heartache over those whom He casts into the outer darkness. Rather, I imagine His Bride wiping away
His tears as He wipes away her tears. He is her reward, and she is His. Tears are wiped away by rewards. Those in
the outer darkness have no such rewards by which to wipe away their tears. Regardless as to my proposed mutuality
of the Groom-Bride wiping away each other’s tears, I do not imagine the Lord suffering from heartache over the
unfaithful while He is enjoying His honeymoon with His faithful. Perhaps one of the reasons the Groom and Bride
stop crying is because those who abused them on earth finally start crying. The tears of those cast into the outer
darkness may, in certain aspects, be a source of consolation, not heartbreak, for the Groom and Bride. If Sarah finds
the anticipation of such an event a consolation now, why not imagine that she will find the actual fulfillment of that
event an even greater consultation then? Does not the postmortem, imprecatory cry of Rev 6:10 by the martyrs,
imploring God to avenge their blood, not suggest their anticipation of consolation as a result of God doing so?
                    Excursus Regarding Misthological Death
       As already noted, Sapaugh correctly interprets the enemies of the cross in Phil 3:18-19 to an example of
regenerate believers (GNTC). His interpretation is entirely reasonable (cp. Phil 1:15-17; 2:21). Their end is physical
death, as he observes. However, misthological death and shame are certainly entailed as well.
