       These enemies of the cross are citizens of the kingdom who are to receive glorified bodies only to have their
glory turned into misthological shame (Phil 3:18-21). When Paul says that their glory is their shame (3:19), what
glory is he talking about? He immediately supplies the answer with an explanatory for (gar): Their glory of being
transformed into glorified bodies (3:21) will be the occasion and source of their shame. Their gloried bodies will
be their shame. For the prospects of a similar Pauline outcome, compare Col 3:4 with Col 3:23-25. John teaches the
same thing. When Christ appears and believers are transformed into glorious bodies, many of these transformed
believers will be put to shame. “When He appears” (1Jn 2:28; 3:2), they will see Him, become like Him (1Jn 3:2),
and shrink away in shame from before Him (1Jn 2:28). The imagery of this truth is most vivid in Rev 3:18, where
transformed believers are ashamed of their misthological nakedness because they are walking about in the raw—
completely devoid of white garments, just as Jesus anticipated (Mt 22:12).
       As I explained in RAE, the citizenship within Heaven affirmed in Phil 3:20 probably includes the
eschatological destruction of unfaithful believers, alluded to in Phil 3:19 (cp. 1:15-17; 2:21), within its scope of
possibilities, thus providing the impetus for the moral exhortation to stand firm in the Lord in Phil 4:1. An
exclusively soteric citizenship makes little sense as a grounds for appealing for moral performance in a context of
misthological destruction. The telic (telos) destruction of these particular heavenly citizens in Philippians gives
grounds for concluding that their names will be erased from the heavenly registry. They will reap misthological
destruction (lose misthological life) rather than gain misthological life. Citizenship suggests membership in a city.
For that matter, Govett would translate politeuma as city in Phil 3:20 and, like many other commentators, believes
that Paul is describing the Lord as coming from this city.1 In Phil 1:27 and 3:20 respectively, Paul exhorts us to act
as members of this heavenly city (politeuo) in view of our membership in that city (politeuma). This city is the literal
city of New Jerusalem. In terms of contingency and unrealized potentiality, their names can be erased from the
heavenly registry, thereby excluding them from residency in the Heavenly City. Paul is making explicit what John
made implicit in Rev 3:5. The names of unfaithful believers will be erased from the city register so that they lose
their citizenship in this heavenly city and thus experience misthological destruction. The names of those whose
names will remain in the heavenly registry are the “church of the firstborn who are registered in heaven” (Heb 12:23;
NKJ).
  Yes, according to 1Cor 15:40-58, we shall all be changed (v. 51) at the rapture, that is, all raptured believers will
receive glorified, spiritual bodies raised in glory (v. 43) that bear the image of the heavenly glory (vv. 40,49). Like
diamonds, these glorified bodies are imperishable (vv. 52-54). Yet, in this very passage no less, Paul does not

1
    Govett, Romans, 472.
Page 94                                                                                    Misthological Models Part 1
overlook the misthological distinctions when he reminds these believers that star differs from star in glory, alluding
back to an earlier differentiation he made in 1Cor 6:9. Immortal bodies are necessary to inherit the kingdom
(1Cor 15:50), but so is morality (1Cor 6:9). The injunction to morality in 1Cor 15:58, therefore, which concludes
this promise of gloried bodies for all believers in 1Cor 15:40-58, makes sense when one recalls, from the previous
context, that not all believers receiving gloried bodies will have the glory of inheriting the kingdom. Consequently,
not all believers are equally luminous, to speak in terms of Paul’s stellar imagery. Having a gloried spiritual body
is a gift given to all believers at the time of their transformation because they are all changed into stellar bodies. But
not all believers will have a body that is sufficiently luminous to qualify them as heirs of the kingdom. All believers
get a gloried body, but they do not get the same gloried body. Paul affirms as much with his variation in stellar
radiation. Just as all members of Christ are members of His body but not all members of His body are members of
His bridal body, so not all believers have the same body. Having a gloried body that is sufficiently luminous to
qualify them for kingdom rulership is a reward.
     To further illustrate the bodily difference, using the analogy of a diamond to express the Pauline affirmation of
imperishability, when Christ appears all believers will be transformed from coal into diamonds, so to speak. But
they will be raw, uncut diamonds. At the Bema, many of these diamonds in the rough will be cut into beautiful,
distinguishable shapes, magnificently radiating their transformed nature. Other believers, however, will remain in
their raw state. Their gloried bodies, left in this raw state, will become the occasion of their eternal shame. The
principle of God turning the glory of His people into shame is found in Hosea 4:7. Even more pointedly, though, in
these eschatological contexts, their glory of being transformed from coal into diamonds will be turned into their
shame of remaining raw diamonds. Some believers will be vessels of honor, some of dishonor (2Tim 2:20). Instead
of achieving the out resurrection (Phil 3:11), dishonored believers will be cast into the outer darkness in this
misthologically raw, unclothed state (Mt 22:11-14). They will remain unclothed in that undesirable misthological
state and place forever. The results are permanent. Uncut diamonds do not wedding rings make. Only the Bride
wears the diamond ring. For further discussion, see the illustrations related to Morphie the butterfly in Part 3 of this
series.
        Whether the destruction (apoleia) of Phil 3:18 alludes back to that of Phil 1:28 is questionable. If so, the
enemies of 3:18 could be the same type of opponents as that of Phil 1:28. More likely, the opponents and their
destruction in Phil 1:28 is generic and applies to either regenerate or unregenerate opponents, whose respective
soteriological or misthological destruction is to be contrasted with the misthological salvation of the Philippians.
Destruction (apoleia) in 1Tim 6:9 certainly applies to believers, but it may apply to the regenerate or unregenerate
alike. It is certainly misthological in Heb 10:39.
                       Excursus Regarding Flesh and Blood
                                            Two Competing Premises
      For a variety of reasons, discussion of the differences in our models keep intersecting, to varying degrees,
with our different understanding as what Paul means when he says, “Flesh and blood cannot inherit the kingdom of
God; nor does the perishable inherit the imperishable” (1Cor 15:50). Dillow, Crawford, and I concur that flesh and
blood refers to mortal bodies. Moreover, Crawford and I are agreed on a basic premise, as is Peters.
                               Illustration 44. Caulian-Crawfordian-Peterian Premise1
         Paul’s statement that flesh and blood cannot inherit the kingdom of God means that believers cannot
         inherit either the millennial or eternal phases of the kingdom in mortal bodies. Consequently, faithful
         believers who survive until the end of the tribulation inherit the millennial kingdom in immortal
         bodies. Conversely, carnal believers who survive until the end of the tribulation enter the millennial
         kingdom in mortal bodies.

        As a result, carnal believers, rather than faithful believers, would be the ones who procreate and thus
repopulate the millennial kingdom. This conclusion is derivable with deductive (i.e., absolute) certainty if the shared
premise by Crawford and myself is granted that the millennial and eternal phases of the kingdom are in view. Even
without this deductive certainty, this premise is strongly attested by inductive probability, as shown above. A
significant amount of inductive data was found to indicate the presence of a pronounced degree of carnality existing
in the millennial kingdom, an observation more consistent with the hypothesis that carnal parentage was involved.
        Significantly, if Caulian-Crawfordian-Peterian premise is correct, then it naturally leads to the perception
that the outer darkness is experienced during the millennial kingdom and is located on Earth, which is the position
taken in my classical model. In that classical model, both mortal and immortal believers would be thrown into the
outer darkness on Earth at that time. But how would this impact Crawford’s model? Initially, Crawford and I only
worked out the details as to what would happen within his model to carnal believers who had died. Placing them in
the courtyard off-planet was an attractive possibility. However, this would not work with carnal believers who lived
until the end of the tribulation in that they would have to be left on earth to repopulate the millennial kingdom. The
solution I posed to Crawford was a mediation between our two models that would allow him, within his model, to
propose that those carnal believers who live until the end of the tribulation will be cast into the outer darkness on
Earth rather than in the outer courtyard off-planet. However, when these carnal believers subsequently die, they
will be cast off-planet into the outer courtyard as pictured in his model. This modification would also be attractive
in that it would provide a suitable explanation within his model as to what happens to carnal believers who are born
and die during the millennial state. Upon death, they too would be cast off-planet into the outer courtyard. This
solution would also be applicable to the Crawfordian-Dillowian model.
        Contrastively, Dillow has expressed strong opposition to this premise, and he must. For if adopted, his model,
as he presents it in Destiny, is destroyed. For Crawford and me, the outer darkness pictures misthological exclusion
from the heavenly city during the millennial and eternal state. For Dillow it does not. Yet if these unfaithful believers
who live through the millennial kingdom are sentenced to live on Earth during the millennial kingdom, then they
are, in effect, being cast into the outer darkness. Dillow’s limitation of the outer darkness to the seven years before
the millennial kingdom, for example, would be falsified. Also, his not assigning spatial value to the outer darkness
would be nullified as well. For the outer darkness would be easily located in that case; it would not be some
nebulous, undefined space.
        Rejection of this premise is, consequently, critical for the Dillowian model. Adopting this model, on the other
hand, is optional for the basic outline of Crawfordian and Caulian models. For example, Crawford and I could allow
the Dillowian counter premise and still place carnal believers in the outer darkness outside the city during both the
millennial and eternal state. Crawford, for example, could have rejected my amendment to his original model and
posed that carnal believers do not survive until the end of the tribulation and do not exist during the tribulation,
which is a standard dispensational assumption. Or he could have posed that they do live until the end of the
tribulation but are then slain at the Bema and then cast into the off-planet outer darkness. Crawford’s rejection of
such options in favor of my amendment is due, therefore, to a desire on his part to be deductively and inductively

1
    Peters also holds this premise, and Dillow cites him as doing so. See below.
Page 96                                                                                 Misthological Models Part 1
true to the biblical data, which from our joint perception strongly favors our premise. Our rejection of the Dillowian
premise regarding this specific matter is, accordingly, not a matter of basic, structural model integrity. Our models
could survive Dillow’s premise easily enough, but Dillow’s model, as he has presented it, could not survive our
premise.
                                    Illustration 45. Dillowian Counter Premise
      Paul’s statement that flesh and blood cannot inherit the kingdom of God only means that believers
      cannot inherit the eternal phase of the kingdom in mortal bodies. Consequently, faithful believers
      who survive until the end of the tribulation inherit the millennial kingdom in mortal bodies. They,
      and they alone, repopulate the millennial kingdom.
                                                Dillowian Argument
        By sweeping consideration of underage children and carnal parents aside with his emotive remarks, Dillow
sets the stage for a false dichotomy in which he repeatedly tells his readers that the only way to harmonize
procreation within the millennial kingdom with Paul’s statement that flesh and bloodies cannot inherit the kingdom
is to pose that Paul is talking exclusively about the eternal phase of the kingdom. The only candidates alive at the
end of the tribulation who could enter the millennium kingdom in flesh and blood bodies to procreate it, according
to Dillow, are those faithful believers who live until the end of the tribulation. His argument might be summarized
as:
                                  Illustration 46. Primary Dillowian Deduction
    1. The only people who could qualify to enter the millennial kingdom in flesh and blood bodies to procreate
       and thus repopulate the millennial kingdom are faithful believers who survive until the end of the
       tribulation.
    2. Some people will enter the millennial kingdom in flesh and blood bodies, procreate, and thus repopulate
       the millennial kingdom.
    3. Therefore, the only people alive at the end of the tribulation who enter the millennial kingdom in flesh and
       blood bodies to procreate are faithful believers who survive until the end of the tribulation.

This conclusion becomes his premise for his next corresponding round of arguments:
                                 Illustration 47. Secondary Dillowian Deduction
      1. The only people alive at the end of the tribulation who enter the millennial kingdom in flesh and blood
         bodies to procreate are faithful believers who survive until the end of the tribulation.
      2. Flesh and blood cannot inherit the kingdom, but faithful believers in flesh-and-blood bodies do inherit
         the millennial kingdom.
      3. Therefore, the phase of the kingdom inherited by flesh-and-blood faithful believers is the millennial
         kingdom.

        The major premise of his primary deduction will not withstand inductive scrutiny. As a result, both of his
deductive conclusions fail. Let us begin, then, with an inductive examination of his major premise. After correctly
refuting a couple of models that are not applicable to the present discussion, Dillow presents two more options, the
first held by Peters, which is the same perspective held by Crawford and m, and the second option is the one which
Dillow will proceed to defend:

      Even if one grants that “inherit” means to “rule with Christ,” Paul says that flesh and blood cannot
      inherit. This means either that: (1) only resurrected and transformed saints will inherit the kingdom
      and no mortals will, or (2) that inheriting the kingdom in 1 Corinthians 15: 50 does not refer to either
      the rapture or the second coming. Instead, it refers to inheriting the eternal phase of the kingdom, the
      new heavens and the new earth.2

2
  Dillow adds this footnote regarding the first option: “This was the view of George N. H. Peters, George N. H.
Peters, The Theocratic Kingdom of Our Lord Jesus, the Christ, as Covenanted in the Old Testament and Presented
in the New Testament, reprint ed. (Grand Rapids: Kregel Publications, 1972), 2: 573” (Destiny, 101, n. 346;
Excursus Regarding Flesh and Blood                                                                                Page 97

Dillow then rejects option one with this explanation:

       Jesus tells the faithful unresurrected saints who have persevered through the tribulation horrors to
       “inherit the kingdom” (Matthew 25:34). There is no mention of them being translated at a
       posttribulational rapture or changed into glorified bodies. If they were transformed, there would be no
       mortals available to populate the millennial earth. Or one might say, “the faithful tribulation saints
       who persevered will be given resurrection bodies at this time and only carnal unfaithful
       tribulation saints in mortal bodies will enter the kingdom.” This has to be inserted to fit a
       posttribulational system of thought. The discussion descends into absurdity and speculation! (Destiny,
       102)

       Actually, pretribulationists could just as easily make the highlighted claim that Dillow poses as coming from
posttribulationalists. Crawford and I, for example, are pretribulationists, and yet, with a notable exception, we
basically hold to this position which Dillow rejects as absurd: “The faithful tribulation saints who persevered will
be given resurrection bodies at this time and only carnal unfaithful tribulation saints in mortal bodies will enter the
kingdom.” Instead of saying that only carnal believers who live through the end of the tribulation will enter the
millennial kingdom in mortal bodies, Crawford and I would add that children below the age of accountability will
enter also, which Dillow rejected as an ad hoc proposal. Of course, in Crawford and my models, faithful believers
will enter the millennial kingdom also, but as transformed rulers. I suppose, then, that Dillow would regard our
position to be doubly absurd since we postulate that both young children and carnal believers will enter the
millennial kingdom in mortal bodies. From our perspective, on the other hand, and for reasons already explained
above in part, it would seem to us that Dillow’s option 2 is really the option that “descends into absurdity and
speculation,” to use Dillow’s words.
                                                Dillowian Weaknesses
Age of Accountability
       For one thing, Dillow has presented a false dichotomy when he said, “If they [the faithful unresurrected saints
who have persevered through the tribulation] were transformed, there would be no mortals available to populate the
millennial earth.” The only mortals he poses as being available to enter the millennial kingdom and repopulate
it are the faithful believers who survived the tribulation. To the contrary, in my and Crawford’s models, young
children and carnal believers who survive the tribulation would also be possible candidates for entering the
millennial kingdom and repopulating the earth. From Dillow’s perspective, he apparently thinks that he can dismiss
children below the age of accountability from consideration because he considers it an ad hoc argument to pose
such a thing. But why is it ad hoc to apply OT typology and straightforward logic in order to derive a readily
attainable answer to explain the presence of mortal inhabitants in the millennial kingdom?3 Just as God did not kill

emphasis his).
3
   According to www.thefreedictionary.com, ad hoc means that an argument is implemented “for the specific
purpose, case, or situation at hand and for no other” or is “improvised and often impromptu.” I fail to see how either
of these definitions justifies Dillow’s characterizing these opposing models as ad hoc (Destiny, 102). My models,
for example, are formed with considerable thoughtfulness. He disagrees with these models, of course, which is fine.
Scientists should be free to challenge one another over what models best cohere with the data. But would it not be
incumbent upon him to demonstrate the inductive improbability of such models rather than just ignore relevant data
by failing to answer the questions as to what happens to the children and carnal believers who survive the
tribulation? Moreover, my hypothesis that children below the age of accountability enter the millennial kingdom in
flesh and blood bodies is not posed for the singular, and thus allegedly ad hoc, purpose of explaining how mortals
could be in the millennial kingdom. Because my model jointly insists that both children and carnal believers who
survive the tribulation will enter the millennial kingdom in mortal bodies, I am not driven to make this affirmation
regarding the mortal entrance of the children into the millennial kingdom as a necessary means of defending my
thesis that mortals will enter the millennial kingdom.
       If I thought it were inductively probable, I simply could pose that the carnal survivors will enter the kingdom
in mortal bodies and ignore the question about the children. However, probability is decisively against this
limitation. For, in that case, if one is to be forthright in the treatment of all relevant data in a scientific manner, one
Page 98                                                                                    Misthological Models Part 1
the children who were below the age of twenty, when He killed their carnal parents for failing to go in and claim
the Promised Land as their inheritance (Num 14:29), so Crawford and I find it natural to pose that God will not kill
the children below the age of accountability who survive the tribulation, whether they be the children of either
faithful believers, unfaithful believers, or unbelievers. Would it not be absurd to think that God kills these children?4
If they are below the age of accountability and do not have the mark of the beast, why not let them into the kingdom?
No mention of them being translated or transformed is made, so on Dillowian grounds, as stated above, we should
not presume that they are translated or transformed. This assessment is in fact consistent with what Dillow himself
repeatedly states: “There is no transformation of the living at the second coming of Christ” (Destiny, 103, italics
his). Even more strongly he asserts: “There can be no transformation of the living from the perishable to the
imperishable at the second coming” (p. 118). So what is left but to deduce that these children enter the kingdom in
mortal bodies? Surely, some children will survive the tribulation. So what will Dillow do with them in his model,
since they survive the tribulation but are ruled out as possible candidates by his model for entering the millennial
kingdom in mortal bodies? Dillow’s statements lead us to infer that according to his schema: (1) these children who
survive the tribulation will not enter the millennial kingdom in mortal bodies, (2) they will not be translated to
another location, and (3) they will not be transformed on Earth. So what is left but to pose that the Dillowian
model leads to one inescapable fact: Jesus kills the little children!?!5

would still have to answer the question, “What about the children?” Accordingly, my model regarding the children
is offered for two reasons, not just one. One reason is that it helps explain the presence of mortals in the millennial
kingdom, naturally. Secondly, however, and just as importantly, this model provides a rational explanation as to
what happens to children who survive the tribulation. If anyone’s model in this particular matter is truly ad hoc,
then it would appear to be Dillow’s model. He is the one guilty of his own charge because he seems to be ignoring
the fate of the children and carnal survivors so that he might press forward for his single-minded, and thus ad hoc,
purpose of limiting 1Cor 15:50a to the eternal phase of the kingdom, a proposition that strains against the very
wording of the text in question. My model, in contrast, flows deductively from 1Cor 15:50, just as it is written, and
additionally has considerable inductive support. If his new model, in stark contrast, has to ignore relevant data and
seemingly attempt to limit a critical component of the data to problematic perimeters, then would not his new model
be the one that is actually ad hoc? This aspect of his old position is better than his new one regarding these particular
issues.
        This is not to say that his attempt to relate 1Cor 15:50-52 to the end of the millennial kingdom does not have
scientific value, however. Just because Dillow has overstated his case regarding that passage, this does not mean
that he does not have a case. The passage is complex, and I am open to that applicability. Indeed, the passage should
not be limited to the rapture. The passage should be taken inclusively as applying to both the millennial and eternal
phases (Darkness, 64, 99-100). Dillow, though, has strained his case when he attempts to make 15:50a exclusively
applicable to the eternal state. Did he himself not admit, “It needs to be stressed that the resurrection at the Parousia
is not the only resurrection mentioned in the chapter”? (Destiny, 108, italics his). Agreed, both resurrections are
included. Whereas, he curiously would say, “The time of the kingdom inheritance in verse 50 is at the end of the
millennium” (Destiny, 113), I would conclude that the verse includes that resurrection without excluding the
previous resurrections, especially the one at the rapture. Additionally, he most certainly overstates his case when
he poses that his solution is the only option for millennial procreation. Far from it!
4
  The Lord leaves the “humble and lowly” (Zeph 3:12) to enter the millennial kingdom. Does not Jesus consider
little children to be humble and lowly? Does He not say that the millennial kingdom belongs to them? (Mt 19:2-
4,19:14; Mk 10:14; Lk 18:16).
5
  A truly scientific model would at least attempt to explain all relevant data. Not only must one account for the
children, one must account for each class of children (i.e., the children of: faithful believers, unfaithful believers,
and unbelievers), as I already have done in a section entitled, “What about the children?” (Darkness, 65-68). See
illustration therein. In that discussion, I gave rationales for thinking that the children of faithful believers enter the
millennial kingdom in glorified bodies. Alternatively, one might pose that they enter the kingdom in mortal bodies.
In the present book, I am entertaining that possibility. If so, they would live with their parents in the Promised Land
on Earth. Their parents, as overcomers, would be in immortal bodies and raise their children who are in mortal
bodies. I suppose that, at the very least, I should entertain this possibility since Dillow insists there will not be, and
cannot be, any transformation of those who live until the end of the tribulation. Since my understanding of
1Cor 15:50 does not require that I apply the transformation necessitated by that verse to the children of faithful
believers who live through the tribulation, the working thesis that I will adopt herein is that the children of faithful
Excursus Regarding Flesh and Blood                                                                               Page 99
Carnal Parents
        One would also be led to conclude that, according to Dillow’s model, the Lord will kill all the unfaithful
believers at the end to the tribulation. Unlike some within our ranks who pose that no unfaithful believers will
survive until the end of the tribulation, Dillow, much to his credit, fully acknowledges that some unfaithful believers
will live to the end of the tribulation: “It is clear that during the tribulation the love of many will grow cold (Matthew
24: 12), that there will be unfaithful Christians during this time, and some will live to the end” (p. 392). In regard
to the Sheep and Goat judgment, Dillow again correctly affirms: “While it is true that the text does not explicitly
say that there are both faithful and unfaithful sheep standing before the King, we know from the rest of the New
Testament that both will certainly be there” (p. 829). Once again, I fully concur with that aspect of Dillow’s
assessment and find it far superior to the typical dispensational position, even found in our own FG camp. Yet, once
again, one is led to conclude that, according to Dillow’s overarching schema, which only entertains the possibility
of faithful believers entering the millennial kingdom in mortal bodies, that (1) these carnal believers who survive
the tribulation will not enter the millennial kingdom in mortal bodies, (2) they will not be translated to another
location, and (3) they will not be transformed on Earth. Dillow’s model thus seems to lead to one inescapable
fact: Jesus kills the carnal Christians.
        If this is what Dillow proposes, then, at least it is less objectionable than the logical outcome of his model
picturing Jesus killing the little children.6 Personally, I cannot imagine Jesus killing the little children, but perhaps

believers enter the millennial kingdom in mortal bodies. After all, in deference to the position that Dillow then held
at that time (Reign, 79), I acknowledged that this proposal is plausible: “The solution proposed by Dillow is that
the children of the faithful elect will enter the millennial kingdom in their physical bodies and be able to procreate.
This modification of the typical dispensational proposal is plausible” (Darkness, 317). Consequently, we have two
competing alternatives to work with in the present discussion: (1) Jesus blesses the little children of faithful believers
by allowing them to enter the millennial kingdom in mortal bodies, and they will be raised by their parents (so
Dillow previously), or (2) Jesus kills the little children of faithful believers (so Dillow currently?). Scientifically
and emotively, I choose the first option. Children of faithful believers will enter the millennial kingdom in mortal
bodies and be raised by their parents who will be in immortal bodies. The children inhabit; whereas, their parents
inherit.
        Otherwise, my initial preference, as stated in Darkness, will stand. The children of faithful believers will
likewise be transformed along with their parents. For one thing, a model should consider what to do with pregnant
mothers who are faithful and overcome. According to my model, such mothers will inherit the millennial kingdom
and thus be transformed. What about their children, in this case, their unborn children? Picturing the pregnant
mother being transformed while leaving her unborn child as untransformed is difficult, particularly if one subscribes
to the point of view, as I do, that those in transformed bodies are not animated by blood but by spirit! Even aside
from this animation conjecture, I would have trouble supposing that the mother is transformed while the unborn
child is not. Therefore, my preference is to propose that both the mother and child are transformed in this case.
However, I would be able to conceive of the possibility of the older children of faithful believers being allowed to
enter into the millennial kingdom in flesh and blood bodies. With this supposition, I can split my preference between
my stated preference in Darkness and the option being entertained presently: some children of faithful believers
who survive the tribulation enter the millennial kingdom in transformed bodies (e.g., the unborn and newborn);
whereas, other children (the older ones) enter in mortal bodies. In response, one might postulate that some faithful
parents immediately enter into their inheritance when they enter into the millennial kingdom; whereas, other faithful
parents (i.e., those with unborn and newborn children) initially enter the millennial kingdom in mortal bodies and
only subsequently enter into their inheritance (i.e., receive transformed bodies and start exercising their regal
responsibilities) after their children have reached a certain age. In this way, I am able to entertain a plausible, even
probable, compromise between my model and Dillow’s model. Yet in the crucial matter regarding 1Cor 15:50,
Dillow’s model would still fail in that I am postulating that, even in this case, such parents do not inherit the kingdom
until such time that they are ready to do so in immortal bodies. Flesh and blood cannot inherit the kingdom—period.
6
  It is difficult to be sure that this is what Dillow intends because, alternatively, one simply could argue that Dillow
unwittingly distorts his own argument from its conception when he says, “There is no transformation of the living
at the second coming of Christ. Saved men and women in their mortal bodies will enter the kingdom, and the
faithful sheep among them will inherit it as their reward for living faithfully during the horrors of the tribulation
era” (Destiny, 103; italics his). One might infer that the faithful sheep are a subset of the saved men and women
who enter the millennial kingdom in their mortal bodies since the faithful are taken from among them. But if that is
Page 100                                                                                  Misthological Models Part 1
Dillow can. On the other hand, I can imagine the Lord slaying carnal believers at the end of the tribulation. My
ability to imagine it, however, does not mean that I can affirm it. Even this proposal, as noted above, leads to some
rather absurd speculations.7 But surely someone would raise the objection that if I appeal to the typology regarding
Israel entering the Promised Land in Num 14:29, to support my contention that the children below the age of twenty
were spared, then I should be consistent and acknowledge, from that same verse, that those twenty and above are
killed, with the corresponding typological result that carnal parents are killed before being allowed into the Promised
Land. Not so fast! The typology regarding those twenty and above actually supports my contention that carnal
survivors are not killed. They are allowed to enter the millennial kingdom in mortal bodies!
Who Lives Where
       In the OT background, those who failed to inherit the Promised Land did die outside the Promised Land—
eventually, not immediately. Over the course of the next forty years, they all died eventually. Where did they die?
In the wilderness, the same location where they were before failing to inherit the land. Likewise, where are the
carnal parents who survive the tribulation before they fail to qualify for their inheritance? They are living on Earth.
Where, in my model, do they live after they fail to inherit the Promised Land? On Earth! The parallelism matches.
When the Lord says that “he who takes refuge in Me shall inherit the land, and shall possess My holy mountain”
(Is 57:13) or that His chosen ones will be heirs of His mountains and inherit them (Is 65:9), this indicates that
faithful believers will live in this exalted portion of the land, and rule it, that is, the Promised Land, but unfaithful
believers will not live there.
       Yet children and their parents will live in this exalted portion of the land (Is 65:23). Consequently, I would
theorize that the children of faithful believers could live there, and that when they grow up, they, in turn, will have
children who live there. Many, if not most of those living in this part of the land undoubtedly will grow up to be
faithful believers. Additionally, the children who survive the tribulation could live there and produce predominately
faithful decedents as well. Both sets of these children would be taught by the faithful sheep who inherit the kingdom.
       In contrast, those unfaithful sheep who were cast into the outer darkness at the Sheep and Goat judgment will
not live in this exalted portion of the land. Instead, they will live in the remote parts of the planet. Their children
who survive with them through the tribulation presumably would live there with them, in the city outskirts, so to
speak. These unfaithful sheep and their flock, their children, will have children in these fringe locations. These
children, while not carnal without exception, will certainly be less inclined to faithfulness, being taught primarily
by the unfaithful sheep. This model flows easily and naturally, inductively and deductively, from the data with
which we have to build our models.
Grammatical
      As to Dillow’s treatment of the actual text, a syntactical strain is manifest: “Flesh and blood cannot inherit
the kingdom of God; nor [oude] does the perishable inherit the imperishable” (1Cor 15:50). The coordinating

the case, then Dillow’s entire argument immediately collapses in assuming that the only people available to enter
the millennial kingdom in mortal bodies are faithful believers. And Dillow needs this premise to justify his
thesis that faithful believers inherit the millennial kingdom in mortal bodies.
       For the sake of logical consistency then, I have not entertained the notion that Dillow allows carnal believers
to enter the millennial kingdom in mortal bodies, particularly since he says that it is “ad hoc” to suggest that children
below the age of accountability are allowed into the millennial kingdom in mortal bodies (p. 102, n. 348) and
“ridiculous” to suggest that unfaithful believers are allowed into the millennial kingdom in mortal bodies (p. 120,
n. 399). Given his strong statements against these possibilities, I am left to conclude that he unwittingly opened up
the possibility of carnal believers entering the millennial kingdom by his statement on p. 103, when he seemingly
indicated that faithful believers will be a smaller subset of those saved believers who enter the millennial kingdom
in mortal bodies. Yet this option is apparently needed in order to reconcile his subsequent statement: “All believers
will enter the millennium, but only the faithful will rule/inherit there” (pp. 119-120). I agree with this statement,
but this puts his other statements in a quandary since he acknowledges that unfaithful believers will live until the
end of the tribulation, not be transformed, yet enter the millennial kingdom. The only possibility I see to reconcile
these statements is to imagine that Dillow thinks that God will kill them and then immediately resurrect them.
Consequently, this understanding of Dillow’s model is what I have adopted as my working premise in working the
logical extrapolations of the Dillowian proposal, though I find it peculiar to imagine such a scenario. Why kill them
only to raise them immediately with transformed bodies?
7
  See Carnal Survivors, 86.
Excursus Regarding Flesh and Blood                                                                             Page 101
conjunction that Paul uses to connect the two statements in Greek is oude, normally translated as nor, not something
like gar, which could be translated as for (meaning because). Dillow’s argument would be better served by
something like an explanatory gar/for, as opposed to what Paul actually states. Dillow’s dependency on this
explanatory perception is illustrated by the simplicity of his own explanation: “There ‘must be a kingdom which
flesh and blood can inherit.’ Flesh and blood cannot inherit the eternal phase of the kingdom of God because it is
imperishable; but flesh and blood can inherit the millennial phase of the kingdom of God because it is not
imperishable” (Destiny, 116; italics his). The justification that he provides for assuming that flesh and blood must
be allowed to inherit the millennial kingdom is because it is not imperishable. That becausal explanation is a rather
tenuous position upon which to make such a strong statement. This must-because position is certainly open to
question. Yet this is the rather limited point of view that Dillow is taking in order to justify his position: “Flesh and
blood cannot inherit the eternal phase of the kingdom of heaven, because the perishable cannot inherit the
imperishable. But the perishable can inherit the millennial phase of the kingdom of God because that phase of the
kingdom is not imperishable” (Destiny, 116; italics his). Dillow draws the inference that the perishable can inherit
the millennial kingdom from his assumption that the second part of Paul’s construction provide nothing more than
the because for the second part of Paul’s construction. My objection is not to the must-because perception but to
the inference drawn when one limits oneself to that perception. Paul did not make such a limitation. In fact, he
avoids doing so by using nor rather than for.
       If Paul had said that “flesh and blood cannot inherit the kingdom of God; for [gar] the perishable does not
inherit the imperishable,” then one could concede that Dillow’s position is at least syntactically reasonable. But as
the text stands, his position remains highly questionable. This is not to say that the construction does not have
explanatory power. Of course it does. Nor is the objection being made herein to Dillow’s use of the word because
as a means to explain that relationship. For example, I use the same type of statements in Darkness: “They cannot
possibly enter the kingdom in flesh and blood bodies because, according to Paul, perishable flesh and blood bodies
cannot inherit the kingdom” (p. 60). “This metamorphosis is essential for rulership in the world to come because a
perishable body cannot inherit an imperishable kingdom” (p. 64). “They must be changed, as he explicitly states in
v. 53. Why? Because it is impossible for those in flesh and blood bodies to inherit the kingdom” (p. 65). I too take
a must-because view of the passage.
       Obviously, therefore, I fully concur with Dillow that the second half of Paul’s statement provides some type
of explanatory power for the first half of this statement. The difference is that Dillow seems to think that 50b
provides exhaustive explanatory power for 50a; whereas, I conclude that 50b only highlights part of the reason that
50a is true. In other words, 50b highlights a certain facet of the more general principle embodied in 50a. To illustrate,
as I write this section my oldest son is preparing to get married. My wife and I were looking at rings today. So
consider a similar type of construction drawn from my sitz im leben. For obvious reasons, we looked at diamond
engagement rings, not coal ones. The perishable nature of coal is only one reason that coal is not chosen for
engagement rings, but it is an important reason.
                              Illustration 48. Coal versus Diamond Engagement Ring
Page 102                                                                                Misthological Models Part 1
                                           Illustration 49. Coal Negation
                                             Coal cannot be used for gems;
                                      nor can perishable minerals be used for rings.

       In this statement, am I saying that the only reason my son could not use a piece of coal for an engagement
ring is because coal is perishable? Hardly! That inference would be entirely unjustified. The perishable nature of
coal is one reason that coal is not suitable for an engagement ring, but it is by no means the only reason. Just ask
his fiancée! She was excited about her ring. It glittered. It was shiny. It was beautiful. She was not particularly
focused on its imperishable nature, the fact that it was hard. However, that imperishable aspect is the reason
highlighted by my second phrase. One, therefore, could accurately explain my meaning as asserting, “Coal cannot
be used for gems because perishable minerals cannot be used for rings.” But one would be completely wrong in
asserting that I mean that this is the only reason that coal cannot be used for rings. Adopting that mistaken
impression leads to a faulty conclusion.
                                           Illustration 50. Coal Fallacy
                   1. Coal cannot be used for gems.
                   2. Perishable minerals cannot be used for rings.
                   3. Therefore, coal can be used for gems if the gem is perishable

       It would be wrong to conclude, as in the fallacy above, that the intent of my coal negation was to affirm that
coal can be used for gems if the gem is perishable. Such an affirmation would be completely outside the scope of
my affirmation. It makes the unnecessary inference that I wish to entertain the possibility of perishable gems being
used for engagement rings. Indeed, it is not the case that I wish to make a coal affirmation. To turn my coal negation
into a coal affirmation is an inversion I do not intend.8 Yet Dillow’s proposal is fostering the same type of
unwarranted inference upon Paul.
                                    Illustration 51. Flesh and Blood Negation
                                    Flesh and blood cannot inherit the kingdom of God;
                                nor does the perishable inherit the imperishable.

                                     Illustration 52. Flesh and Blood Fallacy
                   1. Flesh and blood cannot inherit the kingdom of God.
                   2. The perishable cannot inherit the imperishable.
                   3. Therefore, flesh and blood can inherit the kingdom of God if that kingdom is perishable.

      Dillow’s argument wrongly inverts Paul’s statement. Although Dillow and I both make becausal statements,
he goes beyond the reasonable becausal inference in placing because before the second proposition, to make the
unwarranted conclusion that flesh and blood can inherit the kingdom if the kingdom is perishable. His conclusion
does not necessarily follow; it runs counter to the syntax, the context, and the OT background. Zeller, for example,
makes an argument similar to that offered by Dillow. And just as I refuted Zeller’s argument, so I must refute
Dillow’s proposal. Concerning Zeller’s model, I responded: “If our bodies must be glorified, imperishable, spiritual,
and sinless in order to inherit the kingdom (1Cor 15:42-44,56), then why does Zeller [or in this case, Dillow]
postulate that tribulational believers can inherit the kingdom in sinful, physical, perishable, non-glorified bodies?”
(Darkness, 65.) As to the OT background, both the OT and Paul telescopically merge certain aspects of the
millennial and eternal kingdom too closely into one for Dillow to divide them so sharply in this particular manner.

8
  Likewise, in logic one cannot necessarily infer that if A then not B implies if B then A: (A  B) 
                                                                                                     X (B  A).
     • Proposition one: If (A) inherit the kingdom, then (B) not flesh and blood.
     • False contrapositive: If (B) not flesh and blood, then (A) inherit the kingdom.
Not true, just because one has an immortal body, this does not qualify one to inherit the kingdom. One must also be
moral. Dillow correctly maintains this facet of the argument. Unfortunately, he does not maintain the second facet,
that one must be immortal to inherit the kingdom.
Excursus Regarding Flesh and Blood                                                                          Page 103
Dillow acknowledges that the OT background for the passage is telescopically merging the data (Destiny, 114, n.
380). Why not allow Paul the same luxury?
        On the other hand, I do not wish to overstate my case. Dillow should not be misperceived as making simplistic
error in logic in this part of his argument. He is much too astute a theologian for such a schoolboy error here. His
argument is far more nuanced. Otherwise, it would not be worthy of this lengthy response. By removing
consideration of the children and carnal parents from the playing field, he sought to eliminate the possible
circumstances under which his conclusion (proposition number 3 above) could be falsified. The problem is that he
only supplied rhetoric rather than logic or Scripture in order to remove this relevant data from consideration.
Dillow’s assessment that such models (as what I am offering here and in Darkness) are ad hoc, strained, or
ridiculous needs to be proven rather than just asserted. As scientists, we need proof, not mere averment. Dillow
unjustly has averted dealing with all aspects of the relevant data. Furthermore, even if it were granted, which I do
not, that Paul is thinking exclusively of the eternal phase of the kingdom to the exclusion of the millennial phase,
this would not be sufficient grounds to infer proposition number 3 without first proving, not merely asserting, that
neither children or carnal believers can enter the millennial kingdom in flesh and blood bodies. Without this proof,
one could still maintain, as I do, that Paul is providing a general principle in 1Cor 15:50a that has particular
applicability to his sitz im leben.
        Nor do I wish to give the misimpression that Dillow could not offer scholastic defense for his take on the
syntax. For example, one might imagine a scholastic diatribe in which I point out that several translations render
oude in this verse as and: “Flesh and blood cannot inherit the kingdom of God, and corruption cannot inherit
incorruption” (CSB; cp. BBE), thus supporting my contention that more than just living forever is necessary to be
a kingdom ruler. Dillow, in response, could counter that some translations leave out translating oude so as to imply
that it means that is. “Flesh and blood cannot inherit the kingdom of God. [That is] What decays cannot inherit what
doesn’t decay” (GWN; cp. CSB; NLT). I could then counter that this meaning is not supplied by BDAG and that
the remaining twenty-five translations I consulted translated oude as nor or neither, which is the manner I take it.
Thus, we could also engage in scholastic debate about the probability of oude being taken as a simple explanatory
that-is statement.
        For example, stated more fully, the construction in 1Cor 15:50 is ou dunamai...oude, typically translated as
cannot...nor/neither. This construction is used six times in the NT:

        •   “A city set on a hill cannot be hidden; nor do men light a lamp, and put it under the peck-measure”
            (Mt 5:14-15).
        •   “A good tree cannot produce bad fruit, nor can a bad tree produce good fruit” (Mt 7:20).
        •   “I will give you utterance and wisdom which your opponents cannot resist nor refute (Lk 21:15;
            TM).
        •   “The Spirit of truth, whom the world cannot receive, because it does not behold Him nor know
            Him” (Jn 14:17; TM).
        •   “As the branch cannot bear fruit of itself, unless it abides in the vine, so neither can you, unless
            you abide in Me” (Jn 15:4).
        •   “Flesh and blood cannot inherit the kingdom of God; nor does the perishable inherit the
            imperishable (1Cor 15:50).

       In none of these constructions does it function in the manner in which Dillow supposes it functions in
1Cor 15:50. One might say, for instance, “A city set on a hill cannot be hidden because men do not light a lamp
and put it under the peck-measure.” Or one might state, more concisely, “A city cannot be hidden because men do
not hide a lit lamp.”9 But one would not misconstrue this statement to mean that one could then turn around and
claim that if a man hides a lit lamp, then a city on a hill can be hidden. Neither should one invert Paul’s statement
by saying that if the kingdom of God is perishable, then it can be inherited by mortals.

9
  The only pertinent example from the OT appears to be Dan 5:8: “They could not read the writing, nor make known
to the king the interpretation thereof” (KJV). (A) Could not read ≠ (B) could not interpret. A because B but not A
just because B. For even if they had been able to clearly read it, they would not have been able to interpret it.
Similarly, the statement is true that (A) mortals cannot inherit because (B) they are perishable. Yet it is false to
assume that (A) mortals cannot inherit just because (B) they are perishable.
Page 104                                                                                 Misthological Models Part 1
       Yet, for present purposes, it suffices to conclude that it is unfitting to pose that such misthological benefits,
as those which are bestowed upon those who inherit the kingdom, are conferred upon those in mortal bodies.10 Do
those given the crown of life die, for example? Or do those who eat of the tree of life die? Are those who litotetically
incapable of being hurt by the second death still subject to physical death? Surely not! Peters is correct: “Flesh and
blood cannot inherit (i.e., to become a ruler in it) this Kingdom...the covenant and promises demanded an immortal
Ruler, so it is with His members...As King He is to be manifested in His glorified form...so also the saints, as co-
heirs, kings and priests must be in their glorified condition before they receive the Kingdom” (emphasis his).11
Dillow himself, as noted above, provided reference to the following summation by Peters (2: 573):

      But this Kingdom—this rulership with Jesus—cannot be inherited by mortal men, it requiring
      immortal beings who resemble the mighty Theocratic King; for the heirship with Jesus, the identity of
      associated rule, the unspeakable honor, etc., which can only safely be confided to persons previously
      prepared for it; the duration, the perfection, design, and results of the reign—all demand this
      previous resurrection and glorification. (Italics his.)

       Peters does not limit the necessitation of the glorification to the duration of the rulership; rather, he
emphatically insists that the design and results of that rulership all demand that those inheriting the kingdom do so
in immortal bodies. I concur. When I say that coal cannot be used for gems, thinking particularly of my son’s
engagement ring for his fiancée, and then mention the issue of coal being perishable in particular, I am not
suggesting that this is the only consideration. Far from it! A piece of coal is ugly compared to the exquisite diamond
that coal can become. Coal must undergo its transformation into a diamond before it is worthy to be used in an
engagement ring.12 So, too, Christ’s Bride must undergo her transformation before she can sit with her Groom. The
Lord will not have a Bride left in an ugly state, or wearing a piece of coal for a wedding ring, when she rules with
10
   Thiselton puts it this way: “Augustine, among others, recognizes that fully to enter into the reign of God there
must be a resolution of the inner conflict which arises from self-will: ‘There will be no further conflict within
ourselves … just as there will be no more external enemies to bear with’” (emphasis his). Anthony C. Thiselton,
The First Epistle to the Corinthians: A Commentary on the Greek Text (Grand Rapids: Eerdmans Publishing
Company, 2000), s.v., 1Cor 15:50. Those who are to rule over Christ’s enemies can no longer be at war within
themselves with the enemy of a sin nature still residing within their flesh. Or as Godet puts it: “Such a body would
be a curtain which would veil from us the face of God, too weak an instrument to bear such emotions, too dull an
agent to execute the works to be done in this new state.” Godet, 1 Corinthians, 862
11
   Peters, 1: 602. See his extended discussion for “Proposition 125. The Kingdom to be inherited by these gathered
saints requires their resurrection from the dead” (Peters, 2: 227-243).
12
   I have not engaged in the controversy as to whether the parallelism in 1Cor 15:50 is synonymous or synthetic. If
synthetic, it further supports my point (1Cor 15:50a living ≠ 1Cor 15:50b dead). If synonymous, it does not distract
from my point. In that case, another facet of the physical living is being entertained as to the necessity of the
transformation. For one thing, the inheritance is imperishable, but inherit ≠ imperishable. Also, the fact that mortal,
living bodies are perishable is not the only thing that prevents them from inheriting the kingdom. The crown of
glory is to be worn by the kingdom rulers (Ps 8:5; 1Pet 5:4). But the crown of glory will only rest upon heads of
glory—glorified heads that are part of a glorified body. Putting such a crown on a mortal head would be far below
the dignity displayed by such a crown.
       One might as well try picturing the crown on a frog. This crown does not belong on the head of a frog, but
on the head of a prince. The frog must be transformed into a prince before he can wear this crown. Unlike the
children’s fairytales, in this rendition it is the young lady who needs to be transformed from a frog into a princess
before she is qualified to sit with the Lord on His throne. By His kiss, He will transform this lowly frog into a
beautiful Bride. The Lord will not marry a frog. The mental imagery I get from Dillow’s argument is that he would
have the Lord marry millennial frogs. I would not.
       At the conclusion of the millennial kingdom, those mortals then living and not involved in the rebellion will
be able to enter the eternal kingdom in flesh and blood bodies, procreate, and extend the human population
throughout the universe. Although they are in flesh and blood bodies, they will not be subject to death. Nevertheless,
they will not qualify to wear this crown of glory or to inherit the kingdom. It takes more than having a body that
will not die to qualify for the kingdom inheritance: 1Cor 15:50a ≠ 1Cor 15:50b even when the subject matter of
both parts of the verse are subjected to dealing with living bodies that do not die.
Excursus Regarding Flesh and Blood                                                                              Page 105
Him from His throne. Paul would have been most surprised to learn that some contemporary scholars are suggesting
otherwise. He did not intend for his negation to be inverted into an affirmation that is logically unnecessary,
syntactically improbable, contextually unlikely, telescopically questionable, and inductively impossible. Paul would
have been blindsided by that misrepresentation of his position.
        If Paul had intended to limit his vision to the eternal state, he could have at least hinted at this tunnel vision
by saying, “Flesh and blood cannot inherit the eternal kingdom of God” (1Cor 15:50, so Dillow). Why did Paul
not insert the word eternal? After all, Peter uses this phrase: “The entrance into the eternal kingdom of our Lord
and Savior Jesus Christ will be abundantly supplied to you” (2Pet 1:11). But even then, Peter does not engage in
the tunnel vision that Dillow imagines that Paul is using, nor would Dillow press on Peter the same limitation that
he imposes on Paul, even though Peter is the only one who actually uses the term that Dillow imagines that Paul
intends! Dillow would not limit the abundant entrance into the eternal kingdom to the eternal phase of that kingdom.
Rather, he correctly would argue that the millennial phase of the kingdom is intended by Peter as well. Peter simply
is stressing the eternal phase because rewards are eternal, not merely millennial. As Peter emphasizes, believers are
seeking to “obtain an inheritance which is imperishable [aphthartos] and undefiled and will not fade away”
(1Pet 1:4). Paul likewise says that we are trying to win an imperishable (aphthartos) wreath (1Cor 9:25). So when
Paul says that “the dead will be raised imperishable [aphthartos]” (1Cor 15:52), one should put two and two together
to conclude, inductively, that the imperishable wreath will be put on an imperishable head. Paul would have been
left scratching his head in wondering how Dillow misunderstands him as suggesting that the imperishable wreath
will be placed on a perishable head during the millennial state. By no means did Paul intend to exclude the millennial
phase of the kingdom from 1Cor 15:50a. To suggest otherwise is to fail to grasp the full scope of Paul’s logic in
1Cor 15:50b: Imperishable rulership necessities imperishable rulers. True. But when does this imperishable
rulership commence? Not until the eternal sate? Hardly!
        If a perishable body cannot inherit an imperishable kingdom (1Cor 15:50b), why assume that a perishable
head can wear an imperishable crown, as Dillow’s model misconstrues 1Cor 15:50a to imply? Imperishable crowns
are for imperishable heads, just as imperishable kingdoms are for imperishable rulers. Imperishable crowns and
imperishable kingdoms are for imperishable kings. Just as an imperishable kingdom rulership cannot be inherited
by recipients who themselves are not imperishable, so an imperishable crown cannot be worn by rulers who are not
imperishable themselves. Imperishable rulership necessarily entails imperishable rulers. Both the wreath and the
kingship denote imperishable rulership and thus imperishable recipients. One reason, among others, that flesh and
blood cannot inherit the kingship is because the kingship/rulership to be inherited is eternal.13 Perishable rulers

13
   Paul is giving 1Cor 15:50b as one reason, not the only reason, for his statement in 1Cor 15:50a. If pressed, Paul
could supply other reasons for his overarching principle in 1Cor 15:50a, as is being demonstrated in the present
discussion. In Paul’s mind, our mere citizenship in Heaven necessitates, eventually, that Christ “transform the body
of our humble state into conformity with the body of His glory” (Phil 3:20-21). Dillow and I would both concur
that there will be exceptions to the expectation that this transformation will occur immediately in all cases, in that
some of the heavenly citizens of the tribulational period who survive the tribulation will not be transformed
immediately when the Lord returns at the conclusion of the tribulation. Where we differ is concerning which group
experiences the delay. He believes that the transformation will not apply immediately to those who become kingdom
rulers. Conversely, I believe that it will not apply immediately to those who are disqualified to become kingdom
rulers. If mere citizenship necessitates such a transformation eventually, then would not rulership demand such
transformation immediately?
       While on the subject of delayed but inevitable transformation, one might as well mention 1Jn 3:2: “When He
appears, we shall be like Him, because [hoti] we shall see Him just as He is.” Even though all believers who survive
the tribulation will see Him at the same time, when He appears, this does not mean that all of them will be
transformed instantly. As dispensationalists, Dillow and I would concur that this transformation is automatic to all
believers living at the time of the rapture (cp. Col 3:4) and also that it is not automatic to those believers living at
the end of the tribulation. The becausal relationship provided in 1Jn 3:2 does not require immediate transformation.
That would be a false contraposition of the relationship: (A) we will be like Him (B) because we will see Him.

     •   Logically, A because B does not necessarily imply if B then A.
     •   Proposition one: (A) Coal cannot be used for gems because (B) coal is perishable.
     •   False contrapositive: (B) Because it is not perishable, (A) it can be used for a gem.

cannot wear imperishable crowns because the rulership conferred by such crowns is to denote an imperishable
rulership, necessitating imperishable rulers. Imperishable rulership requires imperishable rulers. These rulers come
into possession of their imperishable rule at the beginning of the millennial kingdom and thus must be in immortal
bodies at the beginning of the millennial kingdom.
       Impressible crowns, rulers, and rule are intended to be enjoined, not separated. Deathless rulership
necessarily entails deathless rulers, and an imperishable crown entails deathless rulership. The nature of the
kingdom over which they rule may transform after they commence their rule,14 but they themselves must already
have undergone their transformation. Just as the perishable cannot inherit the imperishable kingship, so the
perishable cannot inherit an imperishable crown. The very phrase, upon which Dillow’s argument hangs, regarding
the imperishable nature of the rulership, turns against him when interpreted in light of Paul’s previous use of
imperishable in relation to that rulership. Millennial rulers are awarded these imperishable crowns. These
imperishable crowns are worn only by imperishable rulers. Therefore, these millennial rulers are imperishable.
Last Trumpet
       Paul’s reference to the last trumpet is taken by many premillennialists, myself included, as referring to the
pretribulational rapture: “Behold, I tell you a mystery; we shall not all sleep, but we shall all be changed, in a
moment, in the twinkling of an eye, at the last trumpet; for the trumpet will sound, and the dead will be raised
imperishable, and we shall be changed” (1Cor 15:51-52). In Darkness (p. 164) I took the rather common
dispensational position that this last trumpet is the last trumpet for the church. In Destiny (p. 109) Dillow now
regards such proposals to be “ad hoc.” Supposedly, “1 Corinthians 15:50-52 cannot refer to the rapture!” (Destiny,
114.) Yet later within Destiny, he will still cite this very passage as referring to the rapture (p. 750). One can read
our respective arguments and make up one’s own mind as to who has the better argument. Rather than rehash those
discussions, I would like to throw a new monkey wrench into Dillow’s new model.
       Even if the typical dispensational arguments are regarded by Dillow as ad hoc because they are implemented
for the singular purpose of explaining how the last trumpet in this particular passage is not the last one in the Bible,
Dillow’s dismissing an alternative pro-rapture proposal by other authors with such a wave of the hand is not
permissible. For this alternative proposal is not offered to provide a rationale for how this trumpet need not be the
last one forever, as reasonable as that conjecture may be, but to understand the last trumpet of this passage in its
historical and prophetical setting. According to authors such as Biltz,

            On the Feast of Trumpets, the shofar, or trumpet, is blown one hundred times....The one
      hundredth blast on the Feast of Trumpets is known as “the last trump”....
            ....When Paul said at “the last trump,” he was referring to the hundredth blast on the Feast of



       Not true, just because the item is not perishable does not mean that it can be used for a gem. Stainless steel is
imperishable, relatively speaking, but it is not a gem. Or, in the present case, just because people see Him does not
mean that they will be like Him, depending on the timing, circumstances, and personages involved. God operates
in an orderly manner, and thus expects us to operate in an orderly manner also (Jdg 6:26; 1Cor 14:40). Should we
not also think in an orderly manner and think that He will operate in an orderly manner regarding the timing of the
transformation of those living at the end of the tribulation (cp. 1Cor 15:23)?
14
   Since an imperishable ruler cannot inherit a perishable kingdom, some inversely may insist that millennial rulers
must be perishable. Such an argument, if made, would be ill-advised, however. An imperishable rulership requires
an imperishable ruler; conversely, an imperishable ruler requires an imperishable rulership. Nonetheless, these
imperishable rulers can commence their imperishable rulership with a perishable kingdom as long as that perishable
kingdom is followed by an imperishable kingdom so that there is no break in the rulership. For this reason, one need
not misconstrue the angel’s words in Lk 1:33 (“His kingdom will have no end) to mean that His rulership must
await the eternal phase of the kingdom! The millennial kingdom is but the first phase of, and therefore a part of, the
eternal kingdom in that it will have the same Kings and kings and include the same geographical location. The
renovation will not entail a change in ownership or location. For this reason, just as the imperishable Ruler can
inherit a perishable kingdom, if one considers the millennial kingdom a perishable kingdom, so imperishable rulers
can inherit this same perishable kingdom also. For neither His rulership nor their rulership ends, even though the
form of the kingdom over which they rule transforms. Eventually, their kingdom will catch up to the imperishable
form its King and kings already will have assumed.

      Trumpets....

      The objection will be raised that since no one knows when the rapture will occur or when the trumpet
announcing it will be sounded, posing that it occurs on the Feast of Trumpets reduces its surprise element. Blitz
responds with multiple counters. For one thing, he acknowledges that he does not know what year it will take place.
As to its occurring as a thief in the night, he challenges that this only applies to those believers who are not watchful
and ready. But what about the objection that we do not know the day or hour, so we cannot know that it will occur
on this day at this hour? Blitz’s response here is particularly interesting:

            When you properly understand God’s calendar and the feasts, you will realize that the Feast of
      Unleavened Bread, which falls on Nisan 15, is always during a full moon, as is the Feast of
      Tabernacles, on Tishri 15. But the Feast of Trumpets, which falls on Tishri 1, is always during a new
      moon. Because of the Diaspora (the scattering of the Jews in other nations), it was celebrated for two
      days but was known as one long day. Why was it two days long? It fell on the first, the new moon,
      and everyone needed to know when it would begin, especially those in other countries, and it took a
      while for their notification. They would light torches on the hilltops to pass the word on. It’s easy to
      determine when a feast is when it falls in the middle of the month. You just start with the first day
      of the month and keep counting. But how do you know when the first is? Because it was based on
      the Temple declaration in Jerusalem and on the sighting of two witnesses, no one knew for sure the
      day or the hour the Feast of Trumpets would begin.16

       If this historical backdrop is correct, then when Jesus said that no one knows the day or hour (Mt 24:36;
25:13; Mk 13:32), He is dropping a hint—a very big one at that—that this event will happen on the Feast of
Trumpets! Klein and Spears confirm that Jesus was giving this detail as one of “seven specific references that any
Jew of that day would understand to mean ‘Feast of Trumpets.’”17 Pertaining to this particular reference they
explain:

      While it occurred just one day on the calendar, it occupied a 24-hour time slot within a forty-eight-hour
      period. No one knew exactly which hour or on which of the two days the trumpet blast, literally the
      “alarm” trumpet blast (call to war) called Yom Teruah, would sound. In Yeshua’s [Jesus’] time, Rosh
      HaShariah [The Feast of Trumpets] was known as “The Day and the Hour Which No Man
      Knows.”18

Other writers, such as Keyser, explain similarly:

      Even aside from such specifics, the projection that the rapture will occur on the Feast of Trumpets is a
common one, when the discernible pattern from the Jewish feasts is factored into consideration. Just do a Google
search for Jewish Feasts Rapture. Charts and illustrations linking the rapture with the Feast of Trumpets are
common. For example, www.WaitingForJesus.com gives one such chart with this explanation:

        The Feast of Trumpets may soon find its prophetic fulfillment. Here are some reasons why the Rapture
        may occur during this Festival:
        • All the Spring Feasts were fulfilled at Christ’s first coming, and on the exact day of the feast. All the

        the rapture.
        • The Feast of Trumpets is when the last trump of the rapture of 1st Corinthians 15 is blown.

        Christ, and the rapture is when the Church is caught up to heaven to be wed with Christ.
        • The Feast of Trumpets happens on the new moon, which is 29.5 days after the last one, meaning
        it might occur on the 29th or 30th day, nobody knows for sure. Of that day or hour no man
        knows is an expression referring to this feast, and thus, the rapture.
        • Of that day or hour no man knows, but my Father only is an expression used by a groom when
        asked when his wedding will be. He says this because it is his Father that will tell him when his
        preparations on the bridal chamber are completed and it is time. Again, the wedding pictures
        the rapture.
        • The Open Door of the rapture in Matthew 25, and Revelation 3, & Revelation 4:1 is a symbol of the

        • There are seven days of awe in between the Feast of Trumpets and the Day of Atonement. These
        picture the seven years of Tribulation. Atonement pictures Satan being defeated and cast away at the
        end of the Tribulation. If you add the two-day Trumpets feast, and the Day of Atonement, the 7 days
        of awe are ten days of tribulation which might be referred to in Revelation 2:10.
        • In the Jewish Wedding, the groom comes for his bride like a thief in the night to take (sieze [sic] /

        • The Feast of Trumpets is also known as the coronation of the Messiah, when He will start reigning

      The spring feasts were fulfilled to the day; the fall feasts will be fulfilled to the day also. For computational
purposes, I will grant the probability of this prophetic model being correct to be very high and give it a 90%.
Correspondingly, I will give my earlier interpretation in Darkness, that the rapture will occur at some unknown time
and the last trumpet simply signifies the last trumpet for the church, a probability of only 10%. Consequently, I give
zero probability to Dillow’s new interpretation. I believe it is wrong. Moreover, I now believe that in Darkness I
